Methodology: The RecursiveLink R is a two-layer residual MLP: inner variant R_in(h) = h + W2·σ(W1·h) maps last-layer embeddings back to the same agent's input embedding space; outer variant R_out(h) = W3·h + W2·σ(W1·h) adds a linear map W3 to handle dimension mismatch between heterogeneous agents. During a recursion round, each agent generates m latent steps auto-regressively using its inner link, passes the resulting latent thoughts through the outer link to the next agent, and the loop closes by feeding the last agent's outputs back to the first. Only in the final recursion round does the last agent decode text. Training proceeds in two stages: (1) inner-loop — each agent's inner RecursiveLink is trained in parallel by minimizing 1 - cosine_similarity(R_in(H), Emb(y)), aligning latent thoughts to ground-truth token embeddings without explicit decoding; (2) outer-loop — the full system is unrolled for n rounds and outer links are jointly optimized via cross-entropy on the final textual output, with gradients propagated recursively through the complete computation graph. All base LLM parameters are frozen; only RecursiveLink weights are updated. Four collaboration patterns are evaluated: Sequential (Planner-Critic-Solver), Mixture (domain specialists + summarizer), Distillation (Expert + Learner), and Deliberation (Reflector + Tool-Caller). Critical design decisions: (a) residual connections in RecursiveLink — well-justified as reducing the projection task to learning distributional shifts rather than full reconstruction; (b) cosine similarity for inner training — reasonable as a distribution-alignment loss, though the choice of ground-truth Emb(y) as target is not ablated against alternatives such as teacher-forcing from the frozen model; (c) freezing all LLM parameters — a practical constraint that limits expressivity but enables parameter efficiency. Key Results: Against the primary text-based recursive baseline (Recursive-TextMAS, same structural pattern but text communication), RecursiveMAS achieves average accuracy improvements of 8.1% at r=1, 19.6% at r=2, and 20.2% at r=3. Against the broader baselines at r=3 (Table 3): MATH500 88.0% vs. TextGrad 84.9% (best baseline); AIME2025 86.7% vs. 73.3%; AIME2026 86.7% vs. 76.7%; GPQA-Diamond 66.2% vs. 62.5%; LiveCodeBench 42.9% vs. 39.8%; MedQA 79.3% vs. 77.2%. Inference speedup over text-based recursion scales from 1.2× at r=1 to 2.4× at r=3; token reduction from 34.6% to 75.6%. Baselines include LoopLM (looped single model), TextGrad (text-gradient optimization), Mixture-of-Agents, and single-agent LoRA/full-SFT — these are a reasonable but not complete set; notably, no reasoning-specialized models such as DeepSeek-R1 or QwQ are compared at equivalent parameter counts. Evaluation scale is 1B–10B parameters across diverse Qwen/Llama/Gemma/Mistral families. A critical caution: AIME2025/2026 results are Pass@10, not Pass@1, making those headline numbers (86.7%) substantially higher than what single-inference comparisons would show. Mixture-style distillation results also show that RecursiveMAS (Learner+Expert, 83.3% on AIME2026) does not surpass the Expert alone (90.0%), a gap the paper does not discuss. Novelty Classification: NEW METHOD + NEW SYSTEM/TOOL. The framing of MAS as system-level recursive computation, the RecursiveLink architecture for cross-agent latent communication, and the inner-outer training paradigm with shared gradient credit are genuinely novel as a combined contribution. Individual components — residual adapters, latent-space communication, recursive depth scaling — appear in concurrent work (Du et al. 2025, Zou et al. 2025, Geiping et al. 2025), and the paper explicitly positions itself as the first to extend recursive scaling to the system level. The theoretical results (Proposition 3.1, Theorem 4.1) formalize known intuitions about vocabulary-decoding cost and softmax gradient collapse. Rigor & Reproducibility: Five independent runs are reported with standard deviations (±0.0041 accuracy, ±26s runtime, ±33 tokens) — acceptable but modest sample sizes. RecursiveLink architecture ablations (Table 4) are present and cleanly show the residual + 2-layer design is best. Latent thought length m is ablated (Table 9), showing saturation at m≈80. Training cost is directly compared to LoRA and full-SFT (Table 5). However, several gaps undermine rigor: (a) The gradient stability theorem (Theorem 4.1) is proved under the assumption W3=I for the outer RecursiveLink — the actual deployed outer link has W3≠I, so the theorem does not directly cover the claimed setting. (b) Recursive-TextMAS is an author-implemented baseline, raising possible implementation-gap concerns. (c) No ablation isolates the contribution of the two-stage curriculum (inner-loop warmup) from the latent communication mechanism — it is unclear whether the performance gain requires both components. (d) Pass@10 metric on AIME benchmarks is not flagged in the main tables, where results appear alongside Pass@1 metrics on other benchmarks. Code availability: a project page is referenced (https://recursivemas.github.io) but no code or model weights are explicitly confirmed as released in the preprint. Deployability (4-property check): EMPIRICAL profile. - Practical: Runs on H100/A100 GPUs with sub-10B models; GPU memory per agent (15.29 GB) is within commercial hardware limits. However, the method requires simultaneous white-box access to all agents' hidden states, ruling out API-based or closed-model deployments. - Efficient: RecursiveLink adds only 13.12M parameters (0.31% overhead) and reduces token generation by 34–76% compared to text-based recursive MAS; absolute runtime at r=3 remains high (2000–10000s per benchmark run), partly due to the recursive multi-agent overhead. - Effective: Consistent, statistically stable improvements across 9 benchmarks and 4 collaboration patterns provide reasonable evidence of task-general benefit, though gains on AIME benchmarks are inflated by the Pass@10 metric. - Scalable: Only tested up to r=3 recursion rounds and models up to 10B; behavior at deeper recursion, larger models (70B+), or more than 4 agents is untested and unknown. The paper shows a scaling law plot (Figure 1) but only across r=1–4 for training and inference, not across model scale. The paper delivers a software framework (code claimed at project page) and trained RecursiveLink adapter weights, stopping short of a standalone library. Strengths: - The core architectural insight — replacing decode/re-encode with direct residual projection — simultaneously addresses both efficiency (complexity reduction) and trainability (gradient stability), and both claims are supported by theory and measurement. - The framework is genuinely structure-agnostic: four qualitatively different collaboration patterns (sequential, mixture, distillation, deliberation) all benefit, demonstrating non-trivial generality beyond a single task configuration. - Cost analysis (Table 5) showing RecursiveMAS outperforms both LoRA and full-SFT on accuracy while using fewer parameters, less GPU memory, and lower estimated cost is a strong practical result if it replicates. - The semantic distribution visualization (Figure 7) provides interpretable evidence that latent representations converge toward ground-truth distributions across recursion rounds, corroborating the accuracy improvements mechanistically. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors note in passing that all LLM parameters are frozen and only RecursiveLink weights are updated, implicitly limiting agent-level learning. They do not explicitly call out the white-box model requirement, the Pass@10 metric inflation on AIME, the W3=I assumption in the gradient proof, or the Expert underperformance in distillation style. The paper confronts limitations minimally rather than directly. Analyst-Identified Gaps: (1) AIME2025/2026 Pass@10 results are mixed into main result tables alongside Pass@1 metrics without prominent disclosure — the most impressive headline numbers are measured under a more lenient protocol. (2) The outer RecursiveLink proof assumes W3=I (identity), but the actual deployed module trains W3 freely; the theoretical guarantee does not cover the real system. (3) No ablation separates the inner-loop warmup stage from the outer-loop optimization — a version that skips inner-loop training is absent, making it impossible to determine how much of the gain comes from the training curriculum versus the latent communication architecture. (4) The Recursive-TextMAS baseline is author-constructed and may not represent an optimally-tuned text-based recursive MAS. (5) Distillation-style RecursiveMAS (83.3%) underperforms the Expert alone (90.0%) on AIME2026 — the paper presents this as a speed-accuracy trade-off but does not explain the accuracy gap. (6) All agents require simultaneously loaded GPU hidden states, which substantially constrains memory-efficient deployment compared to the paper's per-agent memory figures. (7) No evaluation on long-horizon agentic tasks (multi-step tool use, code execution pipelines) where the sequential latency advantages would be most relevant. Bottom Line: RecursiveMAS delivers a technically coherent and broadly validated framework for latent-space recursive collaboration across heterogeneous agents, with genuine efficiency and accuracy gains, but practitioners should account for the Pass@10 metric inflation on AIME benchmarks, the white-box model requirement, and the unablated training curriculum before adopting it.

AnchorMem: Anchored Facts with Associative Contexts for Building Memory in Large Language Models

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces AnchorMem, a memory framework for LLMs that decouples retrieval units (atomic facts extracted from interaction history) from generation context (immutable raw interaction chunks), and organizes facts into an Associative Event Graph for long-term conversational memory. - **Why it's important:** Long-term LLM agents degrade in quality when memory systems repeatedly rewrite history into summaries, losing specific contextual nuances needed to answer factual and multi-hop questions accurately. - **How it does it:** An LLM extracts atomic facts from each interaction turn; facts are embedded and mapped back to their immutable source chunks; semantically related facts are clustered via cosine similarity thresholding into higher-order "event" nodes; at query time, dual vector search over facts and events retrieves the associated raw contexts for generation without any graph traversal. - **The good:** On LoCoMo with Qwen2.5-32B-Instruct, AnchorMem achieves 50.57% average F1, outperforming the next-best method LightMem (38.24%) by over 12 percentage points, while also running 1.6× faster (5,074s vs. 8,144s). - **The missing:** Evaluation is restricted to a single benchmark (LoCoMo) across 10 conversations; whether the design generalizes to non-conversational, knowledge-intensive, or streaming-interaction settings is untested. - **Read it or not:** Researchers and engineers building long-term conversational agents should read it — the design is clean, the ablations are credible, and the gains over competitive 2025 baselines are large enough to be actionable.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces AnchorMem, a memory framework for LLMs that decouples retrieval units (atomic facts extracted from interaction history) from generation context (immutable raw interaction chunks), and organizes facts into an Associative Event Graph for long-term conversational memory. - Why it's important: Long-term LLM agents degrade in quality when memory systems repeatedly rewrite history into summaries, losing specific contextual nuances needed to answer factual and multi-hop questions accurately. - How it does it: An LLM extracts atomic facts from each interaction turn; facts are embedded and mapped back to their immutable source chunks; semantically related facts are clustered via cosine similarity thresholding into higher-order "event" nodes; at query time, dual vector search over facts and events retrieves the associated raw contexts for generation without any graph traversal. - The good: On LoCoMo with Qwen2.5-32B-Instruct, AnchorMem achieves 50.57% average F1, outperforming the next-best method LightMem (38.24%) by over 12 percentage points, while also running 1.6× faster (5,074s vs. 8,144s). - The missing: Evaluation is restricted to a single benchmark (LoCoMo) across 10 conversations; whether the design generalizes to non-conversational, knowledge-intensive, or streaming-interaction settings is untested. - Read it or not: Researchers and engineers building long-term conversational agents should read it — the design is clean, the ablations are credible, and the gains over competitive 2025 baselines are large enough to be actionable. Problem & Motivation LLMs are stateless; long-horizon multi-turn interaction requires an external memory system to maintain consistency and personalization. Two dominant paradigms each have structural flaws. Generative/rewriting systems (A-Mem, Mem0) compress history by repeatedly summarizing and merging memories, which dilutes specific details — a user's exact allergy or precise preference — that are critical for single-hop factual recall. Graph-based indexing (GraphRAG, HippoRAG 2) extracts entity-relation triples, but high-frequency generic entities ("oil", "person", "yesterday") create spurious bridges that route retrieval to semantically unrelated events. Neither paradigm preserves original interaction fidelity while enabling fine-grained retrieval. Key Idea The core insight, motivated by the Proust Phenomenon / Involuntary Autobiographical Memory theory, is to separate the retrieval index from the generation payload: store raw interaction chunks as immutable, unrewritable context, extract compact atomic facts as the searchable index, and bind each fact to its source chunk via a surjective mapping. Retrieval operates on precise facts; generation operates on the unmodified original text. An Associative Event Graph clusters related facts across turns into higher-order event nodes, enabling multi-hop bridging without relying on entity co-occurrence. Methodology The pipeline has three phases. (1) Fact-Context Construction: For each incoming interaction chunk C_i, an LLM prompt extracts a set of atomic facts F_i. A surjective mapping M(f_i) = C_i binds every fact to its immutable source chunk. Facts are added to a flat vector store using all-MiniLM-L6-v2 embeddings. (2) Associative Event Graph (AEG): Pairwise cosine similarity between all fact embeddings identifies candidate clusters: for each fact f_i, the top-N neighbors with sim > τ form a candidate set N_i. A redundancy pruning rule discards N_i if the maximum number of facts from any single source chunk reaches ⌈N/2⌉ (preventing within-turn over-integration). Retained clusters are synthesized by an LLM into event nodes e_k stored in a separate event vector store. The chosen operating point is τ=0.85, N=3. (3) Memory Retrieval: At query time, dual top-k vector search retrieves facts R_F and events R_E. Facts serve only as anchors to recover associated raw chunks M_C; final generation context = M_C ∪ R_E. No graph traversal occurs at inference. Key design decisions: immutability (well-justified by ablation showing 39 pp accuracy drop when context is removed), redundancy pruning (well-justified by hyperparameter sweep), and dual retrieval over facts+events vs. facts alone (validated by ablation). Key Results All experiments use the LoCoMo benchmark (10 long conversations, avg. 600 turns / 16k tokens, four question types: Single Hop, Multi Hop, Open Domain, Temporal). Metrics: F1, BLEU-1, and LLM-judge Accuracy (ACC). Three LLM backbones: GPT-4o-mini, Qwen2.5-32B-Instruct (local via vLLM), Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct. Baselines: NaiveRAG, HippoRAG 2, Mem0, A-Mem, MemoryOS, LightMem. On Qwen2.5-32B-Instruct: AnchorMem achieves 50.57% avg F1 vs. LightMem 38.24% (second best), a +12.3 pp margin; ACC 69.54% vs. LightMem 59.87% and HippoRAG 2 59.48%. On GPT-4o-mini: AnchorMem 49.87% avg F1 vs. Mem0* 45.10% (a result taken from the original Mem0 paper, not re-run). Cost efficiency (Qwen2.5-32B, Table 2): AnchorMem achieves the highest accuracy at 5,074s runtime — 1.6× faster than LightMem (8,144s) and 20× faster than A-Mem (99,252s). Online inference averages 4ms per query. Surprising finding: increasing neighbor count N from 3 to 5 reduces token consumption (at τ=0.90: 2,997k → 613k tokens) due to more aggressive redundancy pruning, at a small accuracy cost. Novelty Classification NEW METHOD. The fact-anchor/immutable-context decoupling and the higher-order event construction via fact-cluster synthesis are the genuine contributions. Individual components — atomic fact extraction, vector similarity, event summarization — are established; the novelty lies in the architectural contract that raw context is never rewritten, resolving a specific failure mode of generative memory systems that the paper diagnoses concretely. Rigor & Reproducibility Ablation study (Table 3) evaluates w/o Context, w/o FCU, and w/o AEG variants on Qwen2.5-32B-Instruct, clearly attributing contributions: immutable context is by far the dominant component. Hyperparameter sweep covers τ ∈ {0.70, 0.75, 0.80, 0.85, 0.90, 0.95} × N ∈ {3, 5}, tabulated in Table 6. Top-k sensitivity is analyzed from 10 to 50. No error bars or multiple random seeds are reported for any experiment. Code is publicly released at https://github.com/RayNeoAI-2025/AnchorMem. Two methodological concerns: (a) ACC is evaluated by a locally deployed Qwen2.5-32B-Instruct judge — the same model family used as backbone in the primary comparison, which may systematically favor Qwen-generated responses; (b) Mem0* (45.10% F1) uses numbers from the original paper while the re-implemented Mem0 scores only 29.07% — a 16 pp gap the paper does not explain, leaving it unclear whether AnchorMem's GPT-4o-mini lead is against a weakened baseline. Deployability (4-property check) Profile: EMPIRICAL. - Practical: Runs on standard GPU hardware using vLLM-served open models (Qwen2.5-7B/32B) or the OpenAI API; the embedding model (all-MiniLM-L6-v2) is lightweight and CPU-deployable. - Efficient: Offline construction costs 5,073s total for 10 LoCoMo conversations (~600 turns each); online retrieval is ~4ms/query; total token usage (7.5M) is mid-range compared to baselines (1.1M for LightMem, up to 14.6M for A-Mem), with the event integration phase consuming 5.1M of the 7.5M total. - Effective: The ablation evidence is credible — each component contributes measurably, and the immutable-context design directly addresses the stated failure mode of rewriting systems. - Scalable: Tested only on 10 conversations of ~600 turns each; the claim that redundancy pruning bounds graph growth at larger scale is a theoretical argument supported by observational statistics (4,821 discards vs. 3,570 events on this corpus), not an experiment at 10× or 100× the tested volume. The paper releases code but not pretrained models or data (LoCoMo is a public benchmark). Strengths - The immutable-context design decision has unusually strong ablation support: removing it collapses average accuracy from 69.54% to 30.52%, a 39 pp drop that makes the contribution's causal role unambiguous. - The AEG fills a specific architectural gap — enabling cross-turn multi-hop reasoning — with measurable impact (Multi Hop ACC drops 7.5 pp when the AEG is removed) without reintroducing entity-graph spurious-link pathologies. - The cost-efficiency analysis (Table 2 and Table 4) is unusually complete for a memory methods paper, reporting token consumption by phase, call counts, and wall-clock runtime, allowing genuine apples-to-apples comparison. - The counter-intuitive N↑ → cost↓ finding is explained mechanistically via the pruning threshold condition, demonstrating the authors understand their system's behavior rather than just reporting numbers. Author-Acknowledged Limitations The authors explicitly note: (1) atomic fact extraction and event grouping depend on LLM quality — ambiguous phrasing and implicit references can produce missing or misgrouped anchors; (2) the AEG is built in a single integration pass, and iterative multi-round integration could improve event boundary stability over long-running interactions. These are acknowledged directly and paired with future work directions, which is a credibility positive. Analyst-Identified Gaps - Single benchmark: All quantitative results are on LoCoMo alone (10 conversations). No evaluation on LongMemEval, LOCRET, document-grounded QA, or tool-use agent benchmarks; generalizability beyond social-dialogue memory is unestablished. - Mem0 re-implementation gap unexplained: The re-implemented Mem0 scores 29.07% avg F1 while Mem0* (from the original paper) scores 45.10% — a 16 pp gap that is not attributed to any specific setup difference, raising the possibility that AnchorMem's advantage on GPT-4o-mini is partly against a degraded baseline. - Evolving preferences not handled: The immutable-context design has no mechanism for invalidating outdated facts (e.g., a user changes dietary restrictions). In real long-term interactions this is a correctness hazard, not merely a limitation in scope. - Scale untested: LoCoMo has 10 conversations; the redundancy pruning scalability argument is observational, not experimental. Storage and retrieval complexity at O(N²) pairwise similarity computation for fact clustering could become a bottleneck with thousands of conversations. - LLM judge self-evaluation: Using the same model family (Qwen2.5-32B) as both backbone and judge for the primary evaluation set introduces potential circular bias that the stability analysis in Appendix B.3 does not fully address. Bottom Line This is a solid, well-ablated paper with a clean architectural idea and the largest empirical gains over a competitive 2025 baseline set among recent memory papers; practitioners building multi-turn conversational agents should read it, though the single-benchmark scope and unexplained Mem0 re-implementation gap warrant caution before treating the reported margins as definitive. This is a preprint (arXiv:2604.17377v1, April 2026).

Evaluating CUDA Tile for AI Workloads on Hopper and Blackwell GPUs

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper presents the first independent, cross-architecture benchmark of NVIDIA's CUDA Tile (CuTile) Python DSL against cuBLAS, Triton, WMMA, and raw SIMT on three GPUs—H100 NVL, RTX PRO 6000, and B200—covering GEMM, fused multi-head attention, and end-to-end LLM inference at BF16/FP16 precision. - **Why it's important:** Practitioners adopting new Blackwell GPUs must decide whether to invest in CuTile or stay with Triton/FlashAttention-2; the wrong call means either leaving 2.5× attention throughput on the table or shipping kernels that regress 47% versus existing libraries. - **How it does it:** The study runs identical mathematical workloads across five kernel implementations on three physical GPUs, measures TFLOP/s via CUDA event timing over 50 iterations, and normalizes results to vendor-library baselines (cuBLAS for GEMM, FlashAttention-2 for attention). - **The good:** On B200 (sm_100), the 60-line CuTile attention kernel achieves 1,007 TFLOP/s at seq=4096, 2.51× higher than FlashAttention-2 (401 TFLOP/s) and 1.92× higher than Triton (526 TFLOP/s)—a practically significant result that is not a marginal improvement. - **The missing:** No hardware performance counter profiling (roofline analysis, Tensor Core utilization, memory bandwidth breakdown) is provided, so the 5.6× cross-GPU performance gap between sm_100 and sm_120 is attributed to compiler maturity and shared-memory constraints but not empirically decomposed. - **Read it or not:** Read it if you write or maintain custom GPU kernels for LLM inference on Blackwell hardware; the actionable decision tables (Table VIII) and architecture-specific TFLOP/s numbers are immediately deployable; skip it if your stack sits entirely on cuBLAS/torch.matmul with no need for custom fusions.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper presents the first independent, cross-architecture benchmark of NVIDIA's CUDA Tile (CuTile) Python DSL against cuBLAS, Triton, WMMA, and raw SIMT on three GPUs—H100 NVL, RTX PRO 6000, and B200—covering GEMM, fused multi-head attention, and end-to-end LLM inference at BF16/FP16 precision. - Why it's important: Practitioners adopting new Blackwell GPUs must decide whether to invest in CuTile or stay with Triton/FlashAttention-2; the wrong call means either leaving 2.5× attention throughput on the table or shipping kernels that regress 47% versus existing libraries. - How it does it: The study runs identical mathematical workloads across five kernel implementations on three physical GPUs, measures TFLOP/s via CUDA event timing over 50 iterations, and normalizes results to vendor-library baselines (cuBLAS for GEMM, FlashAttention-2 for attention). - The good: On B200 (sm_100), the 60-line CuTile attention kernel achieves 1,007 TFLOP/s at seq=4096, 2.51× higher than FlashAttention-2 (401 TFLOP/s) and 1.92× higher than Triton (526 TFLOP/s)—a practically significant result that is not a marginal improvement. - The missing: No hardware performance counter profiling (roofline analysis, Tensor Core utilization, memory bandwidth breakdown) is provided, so the 5.6× cross-GPU performance gap between sm_100 and sm_120 is attributed to compiler maturity and shared-memory constraints but not empirically decomposed. - Read it or not: Read it if you write or maintain custom GPU kernels for LLM inference on Blackwell hardware; the actionable decision tables (Table VIII) and architecture-specific TFLOP/s numbers are immediately deployable; skip it if your stack sits entirely on cuBLAS/torch.matmul with no need for custom fusions. Problem & Motivation: GPU kernel developers and ML infrastructure teams face a recurring dilemma: writing high-performance Tensor Core kernels requires hundreds to thousands of lines of architecture-specific CUDA C++ (e.g., FlashAttention-2, CUTLASS), and every new GPU generation—Hopper to Blackwell—demands substantial retuning. NVIDIA introduced CuTile in late 2025 as a Python-based tile-centric DSL intended to reduce this burden, claiming Tensor Core and TMA efficiency at dramatically reduced code volume. No independent, multi-GPU evaluation of CuTile existed at the time of submission; existing benchmarks cover Triton vs. CUTLASS but leave CuTile's performance portability across Blackwell variants (sm_100 vs. sm_120) entirely uncharacterized. Key Idea: The core insight is methodological rather than algorithmic: by fixing identical mathematical formulations across five abstractions and testing on two Blackwell micro-architectures simultaneously, the study isolates compiler maturity effects from hardware generation effects. The 5.6× attention performance gap between B200 (sm_100) and RTX PRO 6000 (sm_120) with identical CuTile kernel code is the central empirical finding, demonstrating that CuTile's tileiras compiler is datacenter-first and that the "Blackwell" label covers meaningfully different targets. Methodology: The experimental pipeline runs three kernel families: square and rectangular BF16/FP16 GEMM (M=N=K ∈ {4096, 8192, 12288, 16384} plus LLaMA-7B FFN shapes), causal fused multi-head attention (32 heads, d=128, seq ∈ {512, 1024, 2048, 4096, 8192}, batch=8), and end-to-end LLM inference on a 4-layer LLaMA-7B-like proxy (batch ∈ {1, 8, 32}). Timing uses CUDA event-based measurement, 10 warmup + 50 timed iterations, arithmetic mean reported; variance was below 1% for all non-SIMT implementations. TFLOP/s is computed from standard GEMM and attention FLOPs formulae with causal-masking halving. Correctness was validated against PyTorch/cuBLAS reference outputs. Three critical design decisions: (1) holding kernel code identical across GPUs for CuTile (valid to isolate compiler effects), (2) allowing Triton autotuning over 18 configurations (fair, but gives Triton an advantage over the non-autotuned CuTile kernel), (3) limiting attention to standard MHA rather than GQA/MQA (intentional to isolate compute-bound prefill behavior, but limits production relevance). Key Results: For BF16 square GEMM at N=8192: B200—cuBLAS 1,672 > Triton 1,033 > CuTile 876 > WMMA 200 TFLOP/s; RTX PRO 6000—cuBLAS 385 ≈ Triton 390 > CuTile 303 > WMMA 195 TFLOP/s; H100—cuBLAS 509 > Triton 385 > WMMA 133 TFLOP/s (CuTile unavailable). For fused attention at seq=4096: B200—CuTile 1,007 > Triton 526 > FlashAttention-2 401 > cuDNN SDPA 163 TFLOP/s; RTX PRO 6000—FlashAttention-2 335 > Triton 288 > cuDNN SDPA 133 > CuTile 179 TFLOP/s; H100—FlashAttention-2 262 > Triton 247 > cuDNN SDPA 134 TFLOP/s. The 5.6× CuTile attention gap between B200 and RTX PRO 6000 is the largest cross-architecture variance observed for any tested abstraction. Triton sustains 62–101% of cuBLAS across all three GPUs without architecture-specific code changes. End-to-end LLM decode performance is memory-bandwidth-bound and uniform across fused attention backends (4,594–4,647 tok/s on B200 at batch=32), confirming CuTile offers no decode advantage. Baselines are current and strong: FlashAttention-2 v2.8.3, Triton 3.3–3.4, CUDA 12.6–13.1. Novelty Classification: NEW EMPIRICAL FINDING. The primary contribution is empirical characterization of a recently released tool (CuTile v1.1.0) across two Blackwell variants that were not previously benchmarked together. The methodology is standard benchmarking; there is no new algorithm, system, or formalism. What is genuinely novel is the quantified, independent demonstration that CuTile's performance is strongly architecture-variant-dependent within a single GPU family, and the discovery of the 5.6× attention gap between sm_100 and sm_120—a finding with direct practical consequence that was not documented by NVIDIA. Rigor & Reproducibility: Variance below 1% is reported but no standard deviations or confidence intervals are provided; given 50-iteration means on deterministic hardware this is defensible. No ablations over CuTile kernel configurations are shown (e.g., varying CTA cluster size to demonstrate the claimed 4× sensitivity). The software version difference between H100 (PyTorch 2.7.1/CUDA 12.6) and Blackwell GPUs (PyTorch 2.8.0/CUDA 12.8) introduces a potential confounder the authors acknowledge but do not bound empirically. Triton is autotuned over 18 configurations while CuTile is not; the paper does not evaluate whether additional CuTile tuning could close the gap. Code is publicly available at an anonymous repository (https://anonymous.4open.science/r/CuTile_Benchmarkings-4F23/README.md). This is an arXiv preprint, not peer-reviewed. Deployability (4-property check): Profile: EMPIRICAL (benchmarking of existing systems on physical hardware). - Practical: All benchmarks run on commercially available or accessible datacenter hardware (H100 NVL, RTX PRO 6000, B200); CuTile requires CUDA Toolkit 13.1 which was available at submission time. - Efficient: CuTile GEMM reaches 52–79% of cuBLAS throughput and requires 22 kernel lines vs. 123 for WMMA; for attention on B200, CuTile is 2.51× more compute-efficient than FlashAttention-2, but relies on 227 KB shared memory per block available only on B200/H100, not the 48 KB constraint of RTX PRO 6000. - Effective: For fused attention on B200, the evidence is strong and concrete (1,007 vs. 401 TFLOP/s measured); for GEMM, CuTile is a clear WMMA replacement but not a cuBLAS replacement, which the paper states honestly. - Scalable: Sequence lengths up to 8,192 and matrix sizes up to 16,384×16,384 are tested; multi-GPU scaling, larger batch sizes, and full 32-layer models are not evaluated; the end-to-end inference uses a 4-layer proxy that may not represent bottleneck distribution at full scale. The work delivers benchmark code as a software artifact; CuTile itself is NVIDIA's externally maintained library. Strengths: - The cross-architecture comparison within a single GPU family (B200 vs. RTX PRO 6000) is the paper's most valuable contribution: it establishes that compute-capability version matters more than marketing family name for CuTile adoption decisions. - The performance–productivity frontier visualization (Figure 1) and lines-of-code table (Table VII) give practitioners a direct, quantified cost-benefit signal that is absent from vendor documentation. - The paper identifies a concrete micro-architectural cause for the sm_100 vs. sm_120 gap (48 KB vs. 227 KB shared memory per block) and connects it to CuTile's tile-size and double-buffering requirements—a mechanistic hypothesis, not mere reporting. - The decision framework (Table VIII, Section X) is actionable and honest, explicitly stating the cases where CuTile is worse than alternatives. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors directly acknowledge: single-GPU-instance evaluation (1–3% manufacturing variation risk); absence of hardware counter profiling (no roofline decomposition); BF16/FP16 only (FP8/INT8 performance unknown); 4-layer proxy model for end-to-end inference; Blackwell-only CuTile (no Hopper/Ampere); software version differences between H100 and Blackwell test beds; and compiler immaturity (tileiras 1.1.0 may improve dramatically). This is a creditable disclosure posture—the authors confront scope boundaries rather than minimizing them. Analyst-Identified Gaps: (1) Triton is autotuned over 18 configurations; CuTile is not subjected to equivalent hyperparameter search (CTA cluster size, tile shape). The paper claims a 4× sensitivity to CTA cluster configuration but does not show the sweep, making it unclear how much of the RTX PRO 6000 underperformance is compiler versus tuning. (2) The attention benchmark covers only standard MHA; GQA and MQA (dominant in current LLM deployment) have different memory-access patterns that could change the relative rankings. (3) No latency measurements under varying batch sizes for the kernel benchmarks—only throughput—which matters for latency-sensitive serving. (4) The end-to-end inference section tests CuTile's kernels individually but CuTile is not integrated into the inference stack, so the claimed "potential 2.5× prefill speedup" at system level is speculative and not measured. (5) No evaluation of multi-GPU or tensor-parallel configurations, which is the dominant regime for B200 deployment. Bottom Line: Practitioners deploying fused attention on B200 datacenter GPUs should evaluate this paper immediately—the 2.51× throughput advantage over FlashAttention-2 in 60 lines of Python is credible, independently measured, and large enough to matter; for all other hardware or standard GEMM, the paper correctly concludes that Triton or cuBLAS remain the better options.

ClawEnvKit: Automatic Environment Generation for Claw-Like Agents

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces ClawEnvKit, an automated pipeline that generates verified evaluation and training environments for CLI-style LLM agent harnesses from natural language descriptions, and releases Auto-ClawEval, a 1,040-environment benchmark across 24 task categories. - **Why it's important:** Manual construction of agent benchmarks requires ~2 person-hours per task (208 hours for 104 tasks in Claw-Eval), produces static artifacts that saturate and leak into training data, and cannot scale alongside rapidly improving agent capabilities. - **How it does it:** A three-module LLM pipeline (Parser → Generator → Validator) decouples declarative task specification from deterministic verification by representing each environment as a (P, M, C) triple — natural language task spec, mock API interface, evaluation functional — so an LLM can generate valid environments without reasoning about full execution semantics. - **The good:** Auto-ClawEval-Mini (104 auto-generated tasks) scores higher than human-curated Claw-Eval on both Coherence (0.59 vs 0.51) and Clarity (3.54 vs 3.38) at 13,800× lower cost, with a manually audited 0% false-negative rate across 52 high-effort low-score cases. - **The missing:** The coherence advantage is partially self-defeating: the authors acknowledge it arises because the LLM judge can directly inspect the structured P↔M↔C alignment in generated tasks but cannot read Claw-Eval's rubrics embedded in code — a measurement artifact, not a genuine quality signal. - **Read it or not:** Worth reading for researchers building agent evaluation infrastructure or studying harness engineering effects; less valuable if your interest is in novel LLM agent architectures or training algorithms, as the contribution is tooling and benchmarking rather than new learning methods.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces ClawEnvKit, an automated pipeline that generates verified evaluation and training environments for CLI-style LLM agent harnesses from natural language descriptions, and releases Auto-ClawEval, a 1,040-environment benchmark across 24 task categories. - Why it's important: Manual construction of agent benchmarks requires ~2 person-hours per task (208 hours for 104 tasks in Claw-Eval), produces static artifacts that saturate and leak into training data, and cannot scale alongside rapidly improving agent capabilities. - How it does it: A three-module LLM pipeline (Parser → Generator → Validator) decouples declarative task specification from deterministic verification by representing each environment as a (P, M, C) triple — natural language task spec, mock API interface, evaluation functional — so an LLM can generate valid environments without reasoning about full execution semantics. - The good: Auto-ClawEval-Mini (104 auto-generated tasks) scores higher than human-curated Claw-Eval on both Coherence (0.59 vs 0.51) and Clarity (3.54 vs 3.38) at 13,800× lower cost, with a manually audited 0% false-negative rate across 52 high-effort low-score cases. - The missing: The coherence advantage is partially self-defeating: the authors acknowledge it arises because the LLM judge can directly inspect the structured P↔M↔C alignment in generated tasks but cannot read Claw-Eval's rubrics embedded in code — a measurement artifact, not a genuine quality signal. - Read it or not: Worth reading for researchers building agent evaluation infrastructure or studying harness engineering effects; less valuable if your interest is in novel LLM agent architectures or training algorithms, as the contribution is tooling and benchmarking rather than new learning methods. Problem & Motivation: Evaluating and training CLI-style LLM agents (called "claw-like agents" throughout — a pseudonymous term for a class of open-source agent harnesses) requires constructing environments that specify what the agent must do, what tools it can call, and how its actions are graded. Prior benchmarks (Claw-Eval, ClawArena, SkillsBench) are human-authored, requiring hundreds of person-hours, and freeze into static artifacts vulnerable to benchmark contamination as agents absorb them through training. Training pipelines (OpenClaw-RL, MetaClaw) are similarly bounded by whatever tasks users happen to perform. The gap is that neither evaluation nor training can scale alongside agent capabilities when environment construction is a manual bottleneck. Key Idea: The core insight is that modern agent environments have an asymmetry: they are semantically infinite from the agent's perspective (unbounded NL context, tool outputs, conversation history) but implementationally finite (a small set of in-memory mock service databases). Exploiting this asymmetry, the paper proposes a declarative formalism E = (P, M, C) — task spec, interaction interface, evaluation functional — that separates what to verify from how to verify it. An LLM can generate a valid (P, M, C) triple without reasoning about state transitions; the deterministic Validator enforces correctness structurally. This makes automated generation tractable where prior approaches required per-task grader logic. Methodology: The pipeline has three modules. The Parser converts a natural language request into structured "intent atoms" (actions, objects, constraints) via a single LLM call. The Generator has three sub-workflows: task generation (LLM writes task.yaml with tools, fixtures, scoring components), service generation (designs new FastAPI mock servers when needed), and fixture generation (mounts files into containers). The Validator runs 12 structural checks (required fields, weight sums, check type validity, LLM judge weight cap at 55%, safety/scoring contradiction detection) and semantic coverage checks (every intent atom must appear in P, M, or C). Execution uses Docker containers with `--network none`, mock services injecting 25% random HTTP 429/500 errors to test robustness, and three harness integration tiers (native plugin, MCP server, SKILL.md+curl). The GradingEngine applies 15 check types (audit-based, output-based, file-based, plus LLM judge) weighted by a safety gate that zeroes the entire score on any forbidden action. Critical design decisions: (1) capping LLM judge weight at 55% to keep the majority of every score deterministic — well-justified to reduce grading variance; (2) deduplication via action rotation and passing last 10 task names to the LLM — reasonable but heuristic; (3) using server-side audit logs rather than agent-reported actions to prevent credit for described-but-not-performed actions — well-justified. Key Results: On quality: Auto-ClawEval-Mini achieves Coherence 0.59 vs. 0.51 for Claw-Eval and Clarity 3.54 vs. 3.38, with 100% validity, generated in 1.8 hours vs. 208 person-hours. On agent performance across 1,040 environments: completion scores range from 34% (MiniMax M2.5) to 76% (MiniMax M2.7 — oddly, the smaller model variant scores worse), with no model saturating the benchmark. GPT-5.4 achieves the highest mean score (58.8). Safety (≥83%) and robustness (100% across all models) are near-perfect, making completion the primary discriminating axis. Across 8 harnesses, all structured harnesses outperform the ReAct Agent Loop baseline (53.3%), with NemoClaw reaching 69.0 (+15.7 pp). Counter-intuitively, Tier 3 SKILL.md harnesses (NemoClaw 69.0, Hermes 66.9) outperform several Tier 2 MCP harnesses (ZeroClaw 57.1, PicoClaw 53.2), suggesting harness integration tier does not determine performance. Auto-ClawEval and Mini scores differ by <2% across all models and harnesses. The system produces 1,040 environments for $80 in API costs (claude-sonnet-4.6). A false-negative audit of 52 high-effort low-score trajectories finds 0 genuine grading errors. Novelty Classification: NEW SYSTEM/TOOL + NEW DATASET/BENCHMARK. The declarative (P, M, C) environment formalism and the three-module generation pipeline are genuinely novel for the claw-like agent domain; the broader idea of automated benchmark generation via LLM (c.f. SWE-smith, RandomWorld, Agent World Model, Endless Terminal) is an active area, and this paper's specific contribution is the application to multi-harness API-calling agents with structured verification. The cross-harness evaluation design revealing that harness engineering matters as much as model choice is an empirical finding with practical significance. Rigor & Reproducibility: Ablations on individual pipeline components (Parser, Validator, Generator sub-workflows) are absent — it is not possible to determine how much each module contributes to final quality. No error bars or significance tests are reported on coherence/clarity comparisons (small N for quality study: 104 tasks). The Pass³ aggregation (requiring success in 3 independent runs) is used for methodology description but not clearly applied as the primary reported metric in Tables 3–4. A notable methodological concern: the coherence metric is assessed by an LLM judge that can directly read ClawEnvKit's explicit P↔M↔C YAML structure but cannot inspect Claw-Eval's rubrics embedded in task-specific Python graders — the authors acknowledge this explicitly, which partially deflates the coherence advantage claim. Code and benchmark are publicly released at https://github.com/xirui-li/ClawEnvKit. This is a preprint; results have not been peer-reviewed. All experiments ran on a single Apple M-series Mac with Docker Desktop. Deployability (4-property check): Profile: SYSTEMS — a built tool and infrastructure evaluated by deployment. - Practical: Runs on a single Apple M-series Mac with Docker Desktop; no GPU required; estimated $20–50 per 1,040-task run using Haiku. - Efficient: Generates 1,040 environments in 18 hours at $80 API cost vs. 208 person-hours for 104 human-curated tasks; evaluation cost scales linearly with task count and model API pricing. - Effective: The evidence that generated environments are evaluation-grade is moderately strong: 0% false negatives in audited cases and comparable agent score distributions to Claw-Eval; but the coherence comparison is partially confounded by judge visibility into task format. - Scalable: Tested at 1,040 environments across 8 harnesses and 5 model families; the authors claim unlimited scalability but do not demonstrate what happens at 10,000+ environments or whether task diversity degrades at scale due to the 24-category constraint anchored to Claw-Eval's service library. The work delivers a software tool (ClawEnvKit) and a benchmark dataset (Auto-ClawEval), both released publicly. Strengths: - The declarative (P, M, C) formalism is well-motivated and cleanly separates environment specification from verification, enabling LLM generation without explicit state-space reasoning — a genuine architectural insight. - The server-side audit log grading design (rather than agent-reported actions) closes a subtle but important loophole in agent evaluation where models receive credit for hallucinated tool calls. - The LLM judge weight cap at 55% and the Pass³ aggregation are thoughtful engineering choices that reduce non-determinism in grading without eliminating qualitative assessment. - The cross-harness evaluation (8 frameworks, 4 model families) is more comprehensive than any prior benchmark in this space and yields the non-obvious finding that harness tier does not strictly determine performance. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors directly confront three limitations: (1) mock services cannot replicate production API messiness (auth flows, schema drift, subscription-tier rate limits); (2) coverage is limited to 24 categories lacking GUI automation, voice, multi-agent delegation, and domain-specific workflows; (3) tasks are capped at 20 tool-calling rounds, not designed for long-horizon multi-session workflows. The authors also acknowledge the coherence metric advantage is partly a measurement artifact of judge visibility into structured format. This directness is a credibility positive. Analyst-Identified Gaps: - The entire "claw-like agent" ecosystem (OpenClaw, NanoClaw, IronClaw, etc.) uses pseudonymous names for what appear to be real systems (OpenClaw maps to open-source Claude Code-style CLI agents; the paper explicitly includes Claude Code and Codex as Tier 2 MCP harnesses alongside these fictional names). This opacity makes it impossible to independently verify the harness results or replicate experiments without access to the named systems. - All 1,040 Auto-ClawEval environments are seeded from the same 104 Claw-Eval scenarios (10 variants per scenario), meaning task diversity is anchored to human-curated scenarios — the system is a diversity-amplifier, not a truly unconstrained generator. The benchmark's coverage is bounded by Claw-Eval's original 24 categories and 15 services. - No ablation shows what fraction of generated tasks fail validation and require regeneration, which would reveal how often the Generator produces invalid environments and the true cost of validation. - The MiniMax M2.7 (more capable) vs. M2.5 result is anomalous: M2.7 scores 49.4 on Auto-ClawEval but M2.5 scores 43.6, yet on Auto-ClawEval-Mini, M2.7 scores only 44.9 vs. M2.5's 51.4 — a rank reversal with no discussion. - Diversity within generated task categories is not quantified; 10 variants per scenario might produce semantically redundant tasks that over-represent narrow sub-patterns. Bottom Line: A practitioner building or evaluating CLI-style LLM agent harnesses gains concrete infrastructure and a ready-to-use benchmark from this work, but should weight the coherence/clarity claims conservatively given the measurement confound, and should note that the pseudonymous ecosystem naming complicates independent replication.

Claw-Eval-Live: A Live Agent Benchmark for Evolving Real-World Workflows

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces Claw-Eval-Live, a live benchmark for LLM workflow agents that derives its task distribution from public workflow demand signals (ClawHub Top-500 skill popularity) and grades agents on observable execution evidence rather than final-text plausibility. - **Why it's important:** Static agent benchmarks freeze task distributions at publication time and score only final responses, causing silent drift from real automation demand and masking agents that produce fluent output without actually executing required actions. - **How it does it:** A refreshable signal layer (updated quarterly from ClawHub popularity snapshots) is separated from a fixed time-stamped release snapshot; tasks are selected via MILP to maximize pilot-model discrimination while covering all 22 task families, and graded deterministically from execution traces, audit logs, and post-run workspace state. - **The good:** The result that no model clears 70% pass rate — with HR/management workflows near-zero and workspace repair near-ceiling — provides a concrete and actionable capability map that a static, author-curated task set would not have surfaced. - **The missing:** GPT-5.4 is simultaneously an evaluated model and the LLM judge for semantic grading dimensions — a direct conflict of interest the paper acknowledges but addresses only by limiting judge scope, with no cross-judge validation using a model absent from the leaderboard. - **Read it or not:** Worth reading for researchers and practitioners building or benchmarking workflow agents who need a quantified breakdown of where frontier models fail on real enterprise tasks; less necessary for those focused on code agents or single-turn evaluation.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces Claw-Eval-Live, a live benchmark for LLM workflow agents that derives its task distribution from public workflow demand signals (ClawHub Top-500 skill popularity) and grades agents on observable execution evidence rather than final-text plausibility. - Why it's important: Static agent benchmarks freeze task distributions at publication time and score only final responses, causing silent drift from real automation demand and masking agents that produce fluent output without actually executing required actions. - How it does it: A refreshable signal layer (updated quarterly from ClawHub popularity snapshots) is separated from a fixed time-stamped release snapshot; tasks are selected via MILP to maximize pilot-model discrimination while covering all 22 task families, and graded deterministically from execution traces, audit logs, and post-run workspace state. - The good: The result that no model clears 70% pass rate — with HR/management workflows near-zero and workspace repair near-ceiling — provides a concrete and actionable capability map that a static, author-curated task set would not have surfaced. - The missing: GPT-5.4 is simultaneously an evaluated model and the LLM judge for semantic grading dimensions — a direct conflict of interest the paper acknowledges but addresses only by limiting judge scope, with no cross-judge validation using a model absent from the leaderboard. - Read it or not: Worth reading for researchers and practitioners building or benchmarking workflow agents who need a quantified breakdown of where frontier models fail on real enterprise tasks; less necessary for those focused on code agents or single-turn evaluation. Problem & Motivation: LLM agent benchmarks face two structural problems. First, static benchmarks freeze a task mixture at publication time; as enterprise tool stacks evolve and automation priorities shift, that mixture becomes stale while remaining reproducible — the benchmark measures yesterday's demand. Second, grading final responses cannot verify whether an agent actually called the required APIs, updated the correct records, or repaired the intended artifact; a polished output can conceal complete execution failure. Both problems compound in deployed business workflows, which combine cross-service state manipulation (CRM lookups, calendar writes, ticket updates) with local workspace repair (file edits, shell commands, test verification). Existing benchmarks address one surface or the other, and none derive their task distribution from external demand signals. Key Idea: The benchmark separates construction into two layers with different time scales: a refreshable signal layer that reads workflow popularity from ClawHub Top-500 skill downloads and translates it into a distributional prior over task families, and a fixed release layer that materializes that prior into executable tasks with fixtures, controlled services, and deterministic graders. The release is reproducible; the construction pipeline is rerunnable. This decoupling allows future releases to absorb demand shifts without invalidating prior evaluations. Methodology: The signal-to-task pipeline has five stages. Stage 1 collects a time-stamped ClawHub Top-500 snapshot, treating each skill as a public workflow demand signal. Stage 2 clusters related signals into workflow patterns by user goal, target artifact, and execution surface (33 patterns collapse to 6 signal families). Stage 3 converts normalized signal mass into family weights (w_f = sum of pattern masses in family / total mass) used as distributional priors for task seeding. Stage 4 expands weighted patterns into executable task candidates, each with a YAML task definition, RESTful mock services that load fixture data and record audit logs, and a task-specific grader.py inheriting from AbstractGrader. Candidates pass pilot screening only if they are runnable, reproducible, and produce score variation. Stage 5 selects the public release using a MILP over 157 screened candidates: maximize pairwise pilot-ordering preservation across K=5 pilot models, subject to N=105 tasks, at least one task per fine-grained family, and zero-discrimination exclusions. Grading uses a deterministic-first hybrid: tool dispatch logs verify data retrieval (15–20% of task score), ground-truth fixture comparison verifies data accuracy (40–60%), and service audit trails or post-run workspace state verify action completion (10–20%); GPT-5.4 judges semantic dimensions only when deterministic signals are insufficient. Workspace repair tasks use fully deterministic script-first verification. Pass threshold is τ=0.80; models are ranked by Pass Rate, ties broken by Overall Completion Score (mean score × 100). Key Results: The best model, Claude Opus 4.6, reaches 66.7% pass rate (70/105 tasks) with overall completion 83.6; GPT-5.4 follows at 63.8% and 81.7. No model clears 70%. The leaderboard spans 22.9 percentage points from top (Opus 4.6) to bottom (Doubao Seed 2.0, 43.8%). Task family structure is more informative than aggregate rank: Development/Terminal tasks (18 tasks) score 100% for Opus 4.6, GPT-5.4, and Sonnet 4.6; HR/People tasks (9 tasks) average only 6.8% across all 13 models with several at 0.0%; MGMT is all-fail under the public pass rule. The service-backed surface (87 tasks) sees a best pass rate of 59.8% (Opus 4.6), while workspace repair (18 tasks) sees all models above 72.2% with several near 100%. Under the public pass rule, 27 tasks are all-fail and 19 are all-pass, concentrating discrimination in a middle band. Among top-ranked models, GPT-5.4 consumes the fewest tokens (1.26M vs. 3.32M for Opus 4.6) at an estimated API cost of $6.27 vs. $31.61. Novelty Classification: NEW BENCHMARK / NEW SYSTEM. The core novelty is the signal-driven, refreshable benchmark construction pipeline — deriving task distributions from external popularity data rather than author judgment is a principled and uncommon design choice. The MILP-based discrimination-aware subset selection is a concrete methodological contribution that makes curation auditable. The combination of service-backed and workspace repair under one signal-calibrated release is a scope contribution. The grading methodology (deterministic-first, LLM-for-semantics-only) is not novel in principle but is applied carefully. The individual controlled services and mock fixtures are engineering execution rather than conceptual contribution. Rigor & Reproducibility: No ablations are presented on the signal-to-task pipeline itself — neither the effect of different signal sources nor alternative MILP objective formulations are evaluated. No inter-rater agreement or reliability statistics are reported for the LLM judge component. The full 105×13 score matrix is released as raw_matrix_v3.csv, and the project page (claw-eval-live.github.io) is referenced, but the completeness of the public artifact release is not specified in the paper. The GPT-5.4 judge conflict is acknowledged but resolved only by scope restriction; no validation against human annotation or an independent judge model is presented. The ClawHub Top-500 signal source is proprietary and not independently verifiable, which limits external replication of the construction pipeline's validity claim. This is a preprint; results have not undergone peer review. Deployability (4-property check): SYSTEMS profile — the paper presents and evaluates a benchmark infrastructure. - Practical: The evaluation infrastructure runs against controlled mock services and sandboxed workspaces accessible via API, requiring no specialized hardware. - Efficient: Full evaluation of 13 models over 105 tasks costs $0.56–$31.61 per model at list prices and 79–241 minutes wall time; the MILP over 157 binary variables is tractable with off-the-shelf solvers. - Effective: The benchmark produces a 22.9 pp spread across models and reveals structured failure patterns by task family, demonstrating it measures meaningful capability differences beyond general language fluency. - Scalable: The quarterly refresh pipeline scales linearly with screened candidates; MILP tractability at larger candidate pool sizes is not analyzed. Task counts per family remain small (1–17), making family-level statistics noisy as the benchmark stands. The benchmark delivers a software artifact (YAML task definitions, grader.py files, mock service fixtures, score matrix CSV) and a rerunnable construction pipeline. Strengths: - Signal-driven task construction is a principled antidote to author-snapshot bias; anchoring the task distribution to an external popularity proxy makes the distributional prior externally inspectable and updateable. - MILP-based discrimination-aware subset selection converts subjective curation into an auditable optimization with explicit size, coverage, and ordering constraints — a methodological transparency step that most benchmark papers skip. - Deterministic-first hybrid grading reduces judge gaming surface area: the LLM judge cannot override hard evidence from service audit logs or workspace state, and its scope is explicitly bounded to semantic dimensions only. - The execution surface comparison (workspace repair near-ceiling vs. service-backed workflows below 60% for every model) is a concrete, actionable finding that reshapes where agent research effort should focus. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors acknowledge GPT-5.4 judge bias, noting they mitigate it by limiting LLM judging to semantic dimensions and grounding every judge input in collected traces and rubrics. They note the 105-task release is modest in scale and position each release as a time-stamped snapshot rather than a permanent capability definition. ClawHub signals are explicitly described as proxies for workflow salience, not ground-truth deployment frequency, economic value, or task difficulty. The authors confront these limitations directly, which is a credibility positive. Analyst-Identified Gaps: - The live benchmark's core value proposition depends entirely on ClawHub Top-500 popularity data, which is proprietary, potentially gameable, and not independently verifiable. If skill downloads reflect developer curiosity or marketing rather than enterprise automation demand, the external grounding claim is weakened and the paper provides no validation of signal validity. - GPT-5.4 judge bias is not resolved: the paper presents no comparison against a neutral judge (e.g., a model absent from the leaderboard) or human annotation, so the magnitude of judge self-favoritism on semantic dimensions remains unmeasured. - 44 of 105 released tasks are non-discriminative under the public pass rule (27 all-fail, 19 all-pass), reducing the effective evaluation set to approximately 61 tasks — smaller than the headline count implies. - The paper presents only one release; the defining property of a live benchmark (tracking demand shifts across releases and demonstrating that rankings change) is claimed but not empirically demonstrated. - Several task families have extremely small counts (SEC: 1, R: 1, DOC: 1, C: 2), making family-level pass rates highly sensitive to single-task scoring artifacts and statistically unreliable. - The pass threshold τ=0.80 is not justified, ablated, or compared to alternatives; different thresholds would shift the all-fail/all-pass balance and potentially reorder the leaderboard. - No human baseline is reported for any task family, making it impossible to determine whether MGMT (all-fail) and HR (<7% average) represent genuine agent capability limits or flawed task design. Bottom Line: A practitioner evaluating workflow agents for enterprise deployment gains a useful structured failure map and a more honest grading methodology from this work, but the live/refreshable claim rests on a proprietary signal source that cannot be independently validated, the single-release paper cannot demonstrate the benchmark's defining temporal property, and the effective discrimination base of ~61 tasks is too narrow to support confident fine-grained conclusions.

Synthetic Computers at Scale for Long-Horizon Productivity Simulation

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces Synthetic Computers at Scale, a methodology for generating persona-grounded, artifact-rich computer environments with realistic file hierarchies and cross-file dependency graphs, and running month-long productivity simulations on them to produce experiential learning signals for AI agents. - **Why it's important:** Training long-horizon productivity agents requires realistic, user-specific work context (files, history, collaborator dynamics), which is impossible to collect at scale from real computers due to privacy constraints — without this, synthetic data degenerates into generic toy workflows. - **How it does it:** A persona is progressively elaborated into a user profile, filesystem policy, and artifact dependency graph; an LLM-based setup agent generates objectives and simulated collaborators; a work agent then executes 20 simulated workdays navigating the filesystem and coordinating with collaborators, producing both process trajectories and final deliverables. - **The good:** Occupation-specific skills extracted from 900 training simulations improve agent performance from 61.6% to 68.6% rubric score in-domain (83/100 test computers), and the same skills transfer out-of-domain to GDPVal (105 wins vs. 67 losses on 220 tasks, p=0.005 two-sided) despite GDPVal being structurally very different from the simulation setting. - **The missing:** No ablation isolates which components — dependency graph, collaborator simulation, weekly planning — actually drive the gains; the entire system is evaluated only as a monolith. - **Read it or not:** Essential reading for researchers building long-horizon agent training pipelines or synthetic data infrastructure; practitioners interested only in benchmark leaderboard results can skip it.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces Synthetic Computers at Scale, a methodology for generating persona-grounded, artifact-rich computer environments with realistic file hierarchies and cross-file dependency graphs, and running month-long productivity simulations on them to produce experiential learning signals for AI agents. - Why it's important: Training long-horizon productivity agents requires realistic, user-specific work context (files, history, collaborator dynamics), which is impossible to collect at scale from real computers due to privacy constraints — without this, synthetic data degenerates into generic toy workflows. - How it does it: A persona is progressively elaborated into a user profile, filesystem policy, and artifact dependency graph; an LLM-based setup agent generates objectives and simulated collaborators; a work agent then executes 20 simulated workdays navigating the filesystem and coordinating with collaborators, producing both process trajectories and final deliverables. - The good: Occupation-specific skills extracted from 900 training simulations improve agent performance from 61.6% to 68.6% rubric score in-domain (83/100 test computers), and the same skills transfer out-of-domain to GDPVal (105 wins vs. 67 losses on 220 tasks, p=0.005 two-sided) despite GDPVal being structurally very different from the simulation setting. - The missing: No ablation isolates which components — dependency graph, collaborator simulation, weekly planning — actually drive the gains; the entire system is evaluated only as a monolith. - Read it or not: Essential reading for researchers building long-horizon agent training pipelines or synthetic data infrastructure; practitioners interested only in benchmark leaderboard results can skip it. Problem & Motivation: Long-horizon AI agents operating over entire user computers — navigating filesystems, reading artifacts, coordinating with collaborators, and producing multi-week deliverables — need training data grounded in realistic user-specific environments. Real trajectories cannot be collected at scale because personal computers contain private enterprise documents, project state, and interaction history. Prior synthetic data approaches generate isolated tasks (e.g., GDPVal, OSWorld) rather than the full work context that conditions realistic productivity behavior: prior files, evolving project state, collaborator feedback, and cross-artifact dependencies. The gap is not missing tasks but missing environments. Key Idea: The core insight is "synthesize the context, not only the task." Rather than sampling a task and generating a prompt, the methodology first builds a user-specific computer — complete with a directed dependency graph of correlated artifacts reflecting realistic professional work history — and then conditions both objectives and agent behavior on that computer. The dependency graph (topologically ordered using Kahn's algorithm) ensures each file is generated with reference to its predecessors, so later artifacts genuinely build on earlier ones rather than being independent samples conditioned only on the persona. Methodology: The pipeline has two major stages. Computer creation: (1) A persona (brief professional description) is expanded into a detailed user profile covering identity, career stage, responsibilities, collaborators, tool preferences, and document habits. (2) A filesystem policy is derived, specifying drive layout, naming conventions, storage patterns, and organization style. (3) A filesystem plan enumerates files with paths, artifact types, timestamps, origins, and cross-file dependency edges. (4) Files are instantiated in dependency order — web-downloadable artifacts retrieved first, user-specific artifacts generated by an LLM agent equipped with Office-creation skills (MiniMax minimax-docx/xlsx, pptx-generator, minimax-pdf), each conditioned on its predecessor artifacts. Long-horizon simulation: A setup agent reads the user profile and computer state and infers month-long productivity objectives comprising 4–5 deliverable work packages with per-week milestones and expected output artifacts. The setup agent also generates simulated collaborators (managers, clients, peers, compliance officers) with roles, backgrounds, communication styles, and private reference files not initially visible to the work agent. A work agent then executes weekly planning cycles followed by 20 daily agent sessions, each session restoring context from the activity log and collaborator exchanges, producing files and sending messages. The simulation runtime uses Claude Code SDK; the work agent is Claude Sonnet 4.6, the setup agent Claude Opus 4.6. In-domain evaluation uses occupation-specific skills extracted from retrospective trajectory reports generated by an LLM analyzer, frequency-ranked across the 900 training simulations, and compiled into occupation-specific behavioral guides by a skill-creator agent. These skills are injected as context for the work agent on held-out test computers. Key Results: 1,000 synthetic computers were created and simulated. Pre-simulation computers averaged 112 files; post-simulation 197 files. Productivity artifacts (DOCX, XLSX, PDF, PPTX) account for 67.8% of all files. Each simulation averaged 2,272 agent turns and 8.59 hours wall-clock time with 5.5 simulated collaborators and ~31 communications per run. Rubric-based evaluation on 100 held-out computers: baseline score 61.6%, occupation-skill-augmented score 68.6% (+7.0 pp); skill-augmented wins 83/100 paired comparisons. Scaling trend: 10/100/500/900 training computers yield 50%/64%/75%/83% win rates, indicating the improvement monotonically increases with training coverage. Out-of-domain evaluation on GDPVal (220 tasks; avg 31 turns, 17 minutes, vs. the simulations' 2,272 turns and 8.59 hours): Sonnet-4.6 wins 105, loses 67 (p₂=0.005); Opus-4.6 wins 99, loses 71 (p₂=0.038); Haiku-4.5 wins 104, loses 80 (p₂=0.090, not significant two-sided). The retrospective analysis reveals that the dominant failure mode across all deliverables is cross-document numerical inconsistency, not failure to produce files. Novelty Classification: NEW SYSTEM/TOOL + NEW METHOD + NEW EMPIRICAL FINDING. The combination of persona-grounded filesystem synthesis, artifact dependency graph generation, and month-long multi-deliverable agent simulation with simulated collaborators is new. The finding that trajectory-derived occupation skills transfer out-of-domain to a structurally very different benchmark (GDPVal) is a non-obvious empirical result. However, the individual components — LLM-based persona expansion (building on Ge et al. 2024), skill extraction from trajectories, and rubric-based LLM judgment — are all combinations of existing ideas applied to a new setting. Rigor & Reproducibility: This is a preprint technical report, not peer-reviewed. No ablations isolate individual components (dependency graph, collaborator simulation, weekly planning structure, retrospective analysis quality). The rubric generation procedure is somewhat principled — five runs per computer are used to generate and merge draft rubrics, reducing idiosyncratic rubric bias — but the rubric judge (Claude Opus 4.6) is the same model family as the agents being evaluated, raising potential circular evaluation concerns. Error bars, confidence intervals, and significance tests are reported for the GDPVal out-of-domain comparison (sign tests) but not for the in-domain rubric scores. The 4.2% tool-error rate in agent turns (213 errors across 5,114 turns in the appendix example) is described qualitatively but not systematically analyzed across all 1,000 simulations. The dataset (100 synthetic computers, 500 retrospective reports) is publicly released on HuggingFace at huggingface.co/datasets/microsoft/synthetic-computers-at-scale; trained model weights and extracted skills are not released. Deployability (4-property check): Profile: EMPIRICAL (a methodology and system evaluated by running agents on synthetic computers). - Practical: The pipeline runs on Claude Code SDK with commercially available models (Sonnet 4.6, Opus 4.6) and MiniMax open-source Office skills; no specialized hardware is required, but API access and sufficient quota are prerequisites. - Efficient: Each simulation averages 8.59 hours wall-clock time and 2,272 agent turns, making 1,000 simulations extremely expensive; the paper does not report total compute cost, token counts, or API spend, which is a significant omission for practitioners considering scaling. - Effective: The +7.0 pp in-domain improvement and statistically significant GDPVal gains are real signal; however, the absolute baseline score of 61.6% on LLM-judged rubrics leaves open how much of the gain reflects genuine productivity improvement versus rubric-gaming by the same model family. - Scalable: The paper claims the methodology can scale to billions of computers given sufficient compute, with personas available at that scale from prior work; however, only 1,000 computers are demonstrated, and the compute cost per simulation makes billion-scale deployment a distant extrapolation unsupported by current evidence. The work delivers a dataset artifact (100 synthetic computers, 500 reports) and a methodology description, but not a library, CLI, or trained model. Strengths: - The dependency graph over files is a genuine conceptual advance over treating synthetic artifacts as independent samples; it produces correlated, coherent work histories that better reflect real computer environments. - The collaborator simulation — including private reference materials that are only revealed through interaction, and intentional errors introduced by junior collaborators for the agent to catch — creates rich grounding challenges not present in existing benchmarks. - The out-of-domain transfer to GDPVal is the most credible result in the paper: GDPVal tasks are structurally very different (31 turns vs. 2,272 turns, no full computer context), yet the skills derived from computer-grounded simulations significantly improve performance, suggesting the extracted lessons generalize beyond the specific simulation setup. - The retrospective analysis appendix (Appendix A) provides unusually detailed, domain-specific diagnosis of agent failure modes — cross-document inconsistency, blank messages, end-of-context degradation — which are actionable and non-obvious. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors explicitly identify three future directions that implicitly acknowledge current limitations: (1) artifacts have uniform visual style across computers despite varying content (insufficient personalization of formatting); (2) filesystems are too clean and lack realistic noise (temporary downloads, abandoned files, clutter); (3) simulated collaborators are mostly reactive rather than having their own evolving state, deadlines, and proactive agendas. The authors also acknowledge that the web-downloadable file check is performed after planning, so some planned web downloads fall back to synthesis. These are treated as opportunities rather than deficiencies, but they represent real fidelity gaps relative to real computers. Analyst-Identified Gaps: - No component ablation: it is unknown whether the dependency graph, the collaborator simulation, the weekly planning structure, or simply the quantity of synthetic data drives the in-domain improvement; a practitioner cannot know which components to implement. - Circularity in evaluation: the rubric judge (Opus 4.6), the work agent (Sonnet 4.6), and the skill-extraction model are all from the same Claude family; the GDPVal judge is also Claude Opus 4.6; this creates a risk that the extracted skills teach the agent to produce outputs that Opus judges as better without necessarily representing true productivity improvement. - Occupation imbalance: the 1,000 personas are heavily skewed toward Management & Executive (17.7%), Tech (13.7%), and Engineering (13.4%); occupations like Healthcare Support (2.2%), Production & Trades (2.0%), and Office & Admin (0.9%) are severely underrepresented, limiting the diversity claims. - No comparison to alternative synthetic data baselines: there is no comparison to simpler approaches such as task-conditioned single-turn generation, OSWorld-style sandbox simulation, or other agentic data generation pipelines. - Compute cost is entirely absent: 1,000 simulations × 8.59 hours of Claude Opus/Sonnet usage is a substantial and unreported expense; the scalability claim requires this information to evaluate. - The rubric merging procedure (5 runs per computer) relies on the setup agent's objectives and collaborator expectations as ground truth, but these are themselves LLM-generated and may share systematic biases. Bottom Line: This paper makes a credible case that synthesizing realistic work context — not just tasks — is the right framing for long-horizon agent training data, and the out-of-domain GDPVal transfer results are the strongest evidence for this claim; researchers building productivity agent training pipelines should read it, though practitioners will need to wait for ablations, compute cost disclosure, and component-level analysis before adopting the full methodology.

Efficient Agent Evaluation via Diversity-Guided User Simulation

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces DIVERT (Diversity-Induced EValuation via branching of Trajectories), a snapshot-based evaluation framework that replaces linear Monte Carlo rollouts for multi-turn LLM agent evaluation with tree-structured branching from LLM-selected critical junction turns, guided by cosine-similarity-based diversity maximization over generated user responses. - **Why it's important:** Linear restart-from-root rollout evaluation of customer-facing agents wastes tokens on identical early prefixes, fails to expose rare deep failure modes, and becomes prohibitively expensive as agents scale to longer horizons and frontier API costs rise. - **How it does it:** DIVERT serializes the full orchestrator state (dialogue history, agent state, environment/tool state, random seed) at each user turn, uses an LLM to identify the junction whose modification would maximally change downstream agent behavior, generates K=3 candidate user responses conditioned on context, selects the maximally dissimilar one via all-MiniLM-L6-v2 cosine similarity, and resumes execution from the snapshot. - **The good:** Consistent improvement in failure discovery efficiency (errors per 100K agent tokens) across all three τ-bench domains (Airline, Retail, Telecom) and three models (GPT-OSS-120B, Gemini-2.5-Flash, LLaMA-4-Maverick), with even a single branch improving over the restart baseline while yielding a net 929.94-token saving per branch after overhead. - **The missing:** The LLM junction chooser is validated only by downstream ablation, not by ground-truth causal analysis of which turns actually drive failures;
