no significance tests or multiple seeds are reported on the main results; and results are confined to three τ-bench service domains. - **Read it or not:** Practitioners running multi-turn LLM agent evaluations against frontier API models should read this, as DIVERT is a drop-in protocol that demonstrably improves cost-coverage tradeoffs; those working on single-turn evaluation, training, or agentic coding tasks will find the contribution orthogonal to their setting.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces DIVERT (Diversity-Induced EValuation via branching of Trajectories), a snapshot-based evaluation framework that replaces linear Monte Carlo rollouts for multi-turn LLM agent evaluation with tree-structured branching from LLM-selected critical junction turns, guided by cosine-similarity-based diversity maximization over generated user responses. - Why it's important: Linear restart-from-root rollout evaluation of customer-facing agents wastes tokens on identical early prefixes, fails to expose rare deep failure modes, and becomes prohibitively expensive as agents scale to longer horizons and frontier API costs rise. - How it does it: DIVERT serializes the full orchestrator state (dialogue history, agent state, environment/tool state, random seed) at each user turn, uses an LLM to identify the junction whose modification would maximally change downstream agent behavior, generates K=3 candidate user responses conditioned on context, selects the maximally dissimilar one via all-MiniLM-L6-v2 cosine similarity, and resumes execution from the snapshot. - The good: Consistent improvement in failure discovery efficiency (errors per 100K agent tokens) across all three τ-bench domains (Airline, Retail, Telecom) and three models (GPT-OSS-120B, Gemini-2.5-Flash, LLaMA-4-Maverick), with even a single branch improving over the restart baseline while yielding a net 929.94-token saving per branch after overhead. - The missing: The LLM junction chooser is validated only by downstream ablation, not by ground-truth causal analysis of which turns actually drive failures; no significance tests or multiple seeds are reported on the main results; and results are confined to three τ-bench service domains. - Read it or not: Practitioners running multi-turn LLM agent evaluations against frontier API models should read this, as DIVERT is a drop-in protocol that demonstrably improves cost-coverage tradeoffs; those working on single-turn evaluation, training, or agentic coding tasks will find the contribution orthogonal to their setting. Problem & Motivation: Multi-turn customer-facing LLM agents must be evaluated under diverse, stochastic user behaviors to expose reliability failures, but standard evaluation adopts linear Monte Carlo rollout from the initial state. This forces repeated regeneration of near-identical early conversation segments (authentication, greetings, routine diagnostic questions), incurring unnecessary token cost. Because these early prefixes are semantically similar but not token-identical across rollouts, KV-cache reuse is also structurally impossible. More critically, standard user simulators are biased toward cooperative, high-probability behaviors, making it unlikely that rare but realistic user patterns — fabricated credentials, ambiguous intent, insistent repetition — will be sampled often enough to surface the agent failures they trigger. As agents operate over increasingly long horizons and frontier API costs rise (Claude Opus at $25/M output tokens, GPT-5 at $10/M), this inefficiency becomes a practical bottleneck for comprehensive reliability assessment. Key Idea: Agent conversations are inherently tree-structured: many trajectories share a long common prefix and diverge at only a small number of critical decision points. The core insight is that restarting evaluation from the root discards this structure, forcing redundant recomputation of shared prefixes and precluding targeted exploration of behaviors that emerge only mid-conversation. By snapshotting the full execution state at LLM-identified junction turns and resuming with maximally diverse but intent-consistent user responses, DIVERT reuses computation on shared prefixes while directing new computation toward unexplored regions of the interaction space. Methodology: DIVERT operates as a four-stage pipeline on top of an initial pool of R full rollouts. (1) Initial rollouts and caching: R complete trajectories are executed per task, with the full orchestrator state (dialogue history, agent scratchpad, user simulator context, environment/tool database, random seed, routing metadata) serialized as state.pkl + metadata.json immediately before each user turn. (2) Junction selection: an LLM receives the full serialized trajectory and outputs the index of the user turn whose modification would produce maximal downstream behavioral change while preserving intent; temperature is 0.7 with a fallback to the previous turn on parse failure; overhead averages 103.48 tokens per branch. (3) Diversity-guided user response generation: K=3 candidate responses are generated independently at temperature 0.7, each conditioned on full conversation history and user backstory; the candidate with the lowest cosine similarity to the original response under all-MiniLM-L6-v2 embeddings is selected as the divergent input; total candidate generation overhead is 326.04 tokens per branch. (4) Snapshot-based resumption: the cached state is restored and execution proceeds forward with only the modified user turn injected, until termination (success, 100-turn limit, or 10 errors). Branching is iterative: branched trajectories are eligible for further branching up to a configurable budget. Total DIVERT overhead per branch is 429.52 tokens against an average full-trajectory cost of 5,763.52 tokens, yielding a net savings of 929.94 tokens per branch after overhead. Two primary metrics: errors per 100K agent tokens (efficiency) and task failure count (unique tasks with at least one observed failure). Key Results: On the Airline domain with GPT-OSS-120B, the 8-rollout baseline achieves 14.7 errors/100K and 41 task failures (out of 50) at 1.5M tokens; adding 4 DIVERT branches raises efficiency to 16.4 errors/100K and coverage to 44 failures at 2.1M tokens. Reallocating budget from 8 rollouts to 2 rollouts + 8 branches achieves 19.7 errors/100K and 44 task failures at only 1.4M tokens — higher efficiency and equal coverage at lower cost than the 8-rollout baseline. On Gemini-2.5-Flash (Telecom), the 8+0 baseline yields 21.0 errors/100K; adding 4 branches raises this to 24.8. Results are consistent across LLaMA-4-Maverick and in heterogeneous agent/simulator pairings. The heatmaps (Figure 3) confirm that increasing rollouts alone shows clear saturation, while increasing branches continues to expose new failing tasks. Diversity validation (Table 2) confirms that selected candidates have lower mean utterance similarity (0.711) than 2nd (0.769) and 3rd (0.817) candidates, and this ordering propagates to downstream trajectory divergence (0.743 vs. 0.799 vs. 0.849). Exact prefix sharing under DIVERT reaches 34.80% (Airline), 42.50% (Retail), 58.35% (Telecom) versus ~0.5-0.6% for standard independent rollouts. Ablation (Table 3) shows junction chooser alone lifts efficiency (13.6 → 15.1 errors/100K) but slightly reduces task failure count (78 → 75); adding directed generation restores and extends both (15.8, 80); diversity selection adds a further marginal improvement (16.2, 81). Novelty Classification: NEW METHOD, NEW SYSTEM/TOOL. Applying snapshot-based tree-structured branching to agent evaluation (as opposed to training, planning, or reasoning, where MCTS ideas already appear) is the primary novel contribution. The junction-selection-plus-diversity-generation pipeline for evaluation is new. The individual components — cosine-similarity diversity, LLM-as-selector, sentence-transformer embeddings, τ-bench evaluation protocol — are all standard; the novelty lies in their composition into a branching evaluation harness with full execution-state serialization. Rigor & Reproducibility: An ablation study (Table 3) cleanly decomposes each component's contribution. Three models and three domains are tested, including heterogeneous agent/simulator pairings. A fixed random seed (42) is used throughout — no variance estimates, error bars, or significance tests are reported, which is a notable weakness given that τ-bench results are known to vary across seeds. The intent preservation analysis uses GPT-OSS-120B as both the generator and the judge, a mild circularity. Code is promised "upon publication" but is not yet publicly available. KV-cache benefit is reported as structural prefix overlap, not as measured latency or throughput. Evaluations are bounded in scope due to frontier API costs, which the authors explicitly acknowledge. Deployability (EMPIRICAL): - Practical: Runs on commercially available infrastructure; the diversity selection step requires only a lightweight sentence-transformer on CPU (demonstrated on M1 Pro); the framework adds LLM API calls for junction selection and candidate generation but requires no specialized hardware. - Efficient: Total DIVERT overhead is 429.52 tokens per branch (<8% of a full trajectory), with a net saving of 929.94 tokens per branch after accounting for the overhead; as a fraction of frontier agent API cost, the overhead is 0.08-0.20%. - Effective: Consistently improves both errors/100K and task failure count across all tested domains and models, with even a single branch producing measurable gains; the ablation shows each component adds incrementally to the result. - Scalable: Tested at τ-bench scale (up to 278 tasks × 8 rollouts × 14 branches). No stress testing at 10× task count or with significantly longer trajectories; branching is currently limited to user turns only — extension to tool outputs and environment dynamics is acknowledged as future work. The paper delivers a described software system with full state-serialization specification (pickle + JSON schema), but code is not yet released. Strengths: - The observation that trajectories are tree-structured and that restart-from-root discards this structure is precise and well-motivated; it cleanly separates DIVERT from prior work on user simulation diversity, which focuses on the simulator's behavior rather than the evaluation protocol's structure. - Multi-domain, multi-model empirical validation with consistent results across Airline/Retail/Telecom and GPT-OSS-120B/Gemini-2.5-Flash/LLaMA-4-Maverick, including heterogeneous pairings, makes the findings hard to attribute to a single model's idiosyncrasies. - The cost accounting is unusually thorough: exact token breakdowns, shared-prefix overlap analysis, per-domain diversity validation at both candidate and trajectory levels, and concrete monetary estimates all appear in the appendix, enabling independent reproducibility assessment. - The ablation reveals a non-obvious result: junction chooser alone hurts task failure count despite improving token efficiency, showing that prefix reuse without directed diversity narrows rather than expands coverage — this mechanistic insight is the paper's most informative finding. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors note that branching is currently restricted to user turns and does not vary tool outputs or environment dynamics, which limits counterfactual depth. They identify the LLM-based junction chooser and cosine-similarity diversity metric as potentially replaceable with perplexity-based or alternative signals. Large-scale sweeps over closed frontier APIs were not conducted due to cost. KV-cache reuse is quantified as a structural proxy, not a measured speedup. These limitations are acknowledged directly and constructively. Analyst-Identified Gaps: The LLM junction chooser is validated only by downstream ablation, not by ground-truth causal analysis (e.g., counterfactual experiments that identify which turns actually drive failure); it is not demonstrated that the LLM picker outperforms simple heuristics such as always branching at the last user turn before a policy-relevant tool call. The intent preservation analysis uses GPT-OSS-120B as both the generator and the judge, and the finding (branched messages miss intent 25.27% vs. 28.12% for originals) is a modest difference whose significance is not tested. All experiments use a single fixed seed with no confidence intervals — given that τ-bench pass rates are known to be noisy at small rollout counts, the quantitative margins claimed deserve confirmation across multiple seeds. No experiment demonstrates additive benefit when combining DIVERT with an adversarial user simulator (the paper claims orthogonality but does not show synergy). Results are confined to service-domain τ-bench tasks; performance on open-domain web agents, coding agents, or tasks with much longer horizons is untested. Bottom Line: A practitioner evaluating costly multi-turn LLM agents at scale gains a concrete, deployable protocol that consistently improves failure discovery per token with negligible overhead — but the absence of variance estimates and the narrow τ-bench scope mean the quantitative magnitude of gains should be treated as indicative rather than definitive until code is released and replicated.

GREATER: Gradients Over Reasoning Makes Smaller Language Models Strong Prompt Optimizers

proceedings.iclr.cc · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces GREATER, a prompt optimization method that uses numerical loss gradients computed through chain-of-thought reasoning to iteratively refine discrete prompt tokens for small open-source LLMs, eliminating dependence on large proprietary optimizer models. - **Why it's important:** Existing automated prompt optimization methods require expensive closed-source models like GPT-4 to generate textual feedback, making prompt optimization for small deployed models inaccessible without ongoing API costs and external model dependencies. - **How it does it:** GREATER generates candidate prompt tokens via the target model's own forward probabilities, produces a reasoning chain with the current prompt, extracts answer logits through a fixed extractor prompt appended after the reasoning, and backpropagates cross-entropy loss through the full sequence to select the best token replacement per position. - **The good:** On 5 randomly selected BBH tasks, GREATER optimized with only Llama-3-8B achieves 71.6 average accuracy versus 62.8 for the best GPT-4-optimized baseline (APE), and 75.4 with Gemma-2-9B versus 69.8 for APO-GPT-4 — a small model beating a large-model optimizer. - **The missing:** The method requires white-box gradient access, restricting it to open-source models, and no experiments are conducted on models larger than 9B parameters during optimization, leaving the method's scaling behavior as the optimizer entirely untested. - **Read it or not:** Worth reading for practitioners optimizing open-source small LLMs on reasoning tasks who want a gradient-based alternative to GPT-4-dependent textual feedback; those using API-only model stacks or needing statistically validated results should look elsewhere.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces GREATER, a prompt optimization method that uses numerical loss gradients computed through chain-of-thought reasoning to iteratively refine discrete prompt tokens for small open-source LLMs, eliminating dependence on large proprietary optimizer models. - Why it's important: Existing automated prompt optimization methods require expensive closed-source models like GPT-4 to generate textual feedback, making prompt optimization for small deployed models inaccessible without ongoing API costs and external model dependencies. - How it does it: GREATER generates candidate prompt tokens via the target model's own forward probabilities, produces a reasoning chain with the current prompt, extracts answer logits through a fixed extractor prompt appended after the reasoning, and backpropagates cross-entropy loss through the full sequence to select the best token replacement per position. - The good: On 5 randomly selected BBH tasks, GREATER optimized with only Llama-3-8B achieves 71.6 average accuracy versus 62.8 for the best GPT-4-optimized baseline (APE), and 75.4 with Gemma-2-9B versus 69.8 for APO-GPT-4 — a small model beating a large-model optimizer. - The missing: The method requires white-box gradient access, restricting it to open-source models, and no experiments are conducted on models larger than 9B parameters during optimization, leaving the method's scaling behavior as the optimizer entirely untested. - Read it or not: Worth reading for practitioners optimizing open-source small LLMs on reasoning tasks who want a gradient-based alternative to GPT-4-dependent textual feedback; those using API-only model stacks or needing statistically validated results should look elsewhere. Problem & Motivation Automated prompt engineering methods that improve small LLM performance — APO, PE2, TextGrad — all rely on a much larger, more capable oracle model to diagnose errors and propose prompt refinements via "textual gradients." Zhang et al. (2024) empirically confirmed that small LMs cannot generate reliable optimization feedback, creating a hard dependency: improving an 8B production model requires paying for GPT-4 API calls. Prior gradient-based discrete prompt methods (AutoPrompt, PEZ) exist but are architecturally tied to simple classification verbalizers (fixed label token targets) and cannot extend to tasks requiring multi-step reasoning chains, which is exactly where smaller models need the most help. Key Idea Route the optimization loss signal through the model's own chain-of-thought reasoning rather than directly against output tokens. By generating reasoning r, then appending a fixed extractor prompt to force the model to emit answer logits, GREATER backpropagates cross-entropy loss through the concatenated [input ⊕ prompt ⊕ reasoning ⊕ extractor] sequence. Gradients then reflect how a change to a prompt token would alter the reasoning path that leads to the correct answer — a more direct signal than the downstream-only gradient, and computed entirely within the model being optimized rather than outsourced to a larger model. Methodology GREATER optimizes prompt token positions sequentially for T=10^5 steps. At each position i, three phases execute: (1) Candidate Proposal — compute top-k (k=10) next tokens from f_LLM conditioned on q=5 random task inputs, then take their intersection to get domain-relevant candidates; create a one-hot indicator ε_i over this small candidate set, extracted from the LM's embedding table. (2) Reasoning Generation and Extraction — generate reasoning r ~ f_LLM(x ⊕ p), then compute ŷ = f_LLM(x ⊕ p ⊕ r ⊕ p_extract), reusing KV-cache from the generation step for efficiency. (3) Gradient-Driven Selection — compute loss L = CE(ŷ, y) + λ·Perplexity(p) with λ=0.2, backpropagate to ∂L/∂ε_i, pick top-μ=3 candidates by highest negative gradient, evaluate each via a full forward pass on training data, and select the one with lowest loss. Dynamic prompt length is supported by checking for end tokens. Critical design decisions: the intersection operation enforces cross-sample relevance of candidates (well-motivated but unablated); the extractor prompt architecture isolates answer logits from free-form generation (well-justified by CoT literature); the top-3 forward-pass reranking adds robustness against gradient imprecision (reasonable but unablated). All experiments run on 2× A100 80GB GPUs. Key Results On BBH (21 tasks, 100-sample test): GREATER achieves 76.6% with Gemma-2-9B and 68.7% with Llama-3-8B, versus best textual-feedback baselines of 72.9% (TextGrad/Gemma) and 62.7% (APO/Llama). On GSM8K (1319-sample test): 89.4%/Gemma and 82.6%/Llama versus ZS-CoT baselines of 88.6%/79.6%. On FOLIO (203-sample validation): 69.1%/Gemma and 62.6%/Llama (ties PE2 for Llama). Compared directly to GPT-4-optimized prompts on 5 BBH tasks: GREATER (Llama) averages 71.6 versus 62.8 for APE-GPT4; GREATER (Gemma) averages 75.4 versus 69.8 for APO-GPT4. On the 1B parameter Llama-3.2-1B, GREATER averages 53.6 versus 46.0 for PE2-GPT4 on 5 BBH tasks. Ablation: removing gradient-over-reasoning and using direct-output gradients drops performance from 70 to 56 on movie recommendation and from 70 to 33 on tracking shuffled objects (Llama-3-8B). Optimization time is ~5 hours per task versus ~14 hours for TextGrad on the same hardware. Baselines include the most recent competitive methods (TextGrad 2024, PE2 2023) and are tested with both same-model and GPT-4 optimizers, making the comparison fair and strong. Novelty Classification NEW METHOD. The paper extends gradient-based discrete prompt search (AutoPrompt-style token replacement) to chain-of-thought reasoning tasks by routing the optimization loss through the generated reasoning chain via an answer-extractor architecture. The combination of LM-guided candidate generation, reasoning-conditioned loss, and gradient-over-chain-of-thought is novel; prior gradient methods optimized against answer logits without reasoning, and prior reasoning-integrated prompt methods used textual feedback rather than numerical gradients. The extension is non-trivial because naive backpropagation through next-token targets ignores reasoning entirely and empirically performs poorly. Rigor & Reproducibility Ablation for the gradient-over-reasoning component is present (Figure 3) but covers only 2 of 21 BBH tasks and 2 models — insufficient to establish generality of the component contribution. No ablations on k, q, T, or λ hyperparameters are provided. No error bars, multiple seeds, or significance tests appear anywhere in the paper; with ~100-sample test splits, differences of 1–2 accuracy points are likely within noise. Code is publicly released at https://github.com/psunlpgroup/GreaTer. Two BBH tasks (word sorting, dyck languages) are excluded from evaluation with the justification that small LLM outputs are "difficult to reliably evaluate" — this exclusion is not principled and removes tasks where the method might underperform. The perplexity regularization term is described as "less important" without ablation to verify this claim. Deployability (EMPIRICAL) Profile: EMPIRICAL. - Practical: Optimization requires white-box gradient access to the model and 2× A100 80GB GPUs; inference uses only the resulting prompt string and runs on any hardware that can load the model. - Efficient: Optimization costs ~5 hours per task (versus ~14 hours for TextGrad) but scales linearly with the number of tasks and models, requiring ~210 A100-hours to reproduce Table 1 across 21 tasks and 2 models. - Effective: Consistently outperforms textual-feedback baselines across all three benchmark suites with both tested models, and matches or beats GPT-4-optimized prompts in direct comparison. - Scalable: Tested only on 8B–9B parameter models as the optimizer; Table 4 shows the performance advantage narrows when GREATER prompts transfer to Gemma-2-27B, but GREATER is never run as the optimizer on a model larger than 9B. The work delivers a code artifact and optimized prompt strings; no trained model weights or datasets are released beyond the GitHub repository. Strengths - The gradient-over-reasoning insight concretely breaks the dependency on closed-source oracle LLMs, enabling self-contained prompt optimization with the same model that runs at inference — a meaningful practical advance for open-source deployments. - The ablation in Figure 3 cleanly isolates the contribution of routing loss through the reasoning chain versus directly against output tokens, with 14–37 point drops when reasoning is removed, providing strong evidence for the core design choice. - Transferability experiments across both model families (Llama↔Gemma) and up to 27B show that gradient-optimized prompts generalize beyond the optimization model, which textual feedback methods do not always achieve. - The O(NL²) complexity argument and empirical 5-vs-14 hour comparison demonstrate that GREATER is not only more effective but computationally cheaper than TextGrad. Author-Acknowledged Limitations The authors explicitly note that optimized prompts can exhibit grammatical errors or informal tone (Section 5.6, Table 11/14 examples) and propose dynamic top-k selection as a mitigation without follow-up experiments. Performance saturates on FOLIO for Llama-3-8B, attributed to a model-capability ceiling. No other limitations are explicitly discussed. Analyst-Identified Gaps - The white-box gradient access requirement — which restricts the method entirely to open-source models and excludes all API-only deployments (GPT-4, Claude, Gemini) — is never acknowledged as a limitation, which is a significant omission given the paper's framing around competing with GPT-4-based optimizers. - No statistical significance testing on any reported number; with 100-sample test sets, many reported gaps (e.g., 89.4 vs 88.6 on GSM8K for Gemma) are likely within noise. - The intersection over q=5 candidates (Eq. 4) can produce very few or zero candidates at certain positions; the algorithm's behavior in this degenerate case is not described. - DSPy (Khattab et al., 2023), a well-known self-optimizing pipeline that could serve as a comparison point, is cited in related work but absent from all experimental tables. - The evaluation is restricted to multiple-choice or extractable-answer reasoning tasks where the extractor prompt architecture works naturally; applicability to open-ended generation is not discussed. - Optimization costs (5 hours/task on 2× A100 80GB) make per-task optimization impractical at scale; the paper does not address this or suggest amortization strategies. - All FOLIO results for Llama-3-8B show GREATER tied with PE2-GPT4 at 62.6%, which is less impressive than the framing suggests for this dataset. Bottom Line A practitioner optimizing open-source 8–9B parameter LLMs on reasoning tasks with white-box access will find a clean, code-released method that outperforms GPT-4-based textual feedback optimizers at a fraction of the cost; practitioners without gradient access or working beyond the reasoning-task domain will need to look elsewhere.

Intern-Atlas: A Methodological Evolution Graph as Research Infrastructure for AI Scientists

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces Intern-Atlas, a large-scale methodological evolution graph built from 1,030,314 AI papers that explicitly encodes method-level lineage relationships, bottleneck-to-mechanism transitions, and typed causal edges, together with three graph operators for lineage reconstruction, idea evaluation, and idea generation. - **Why it's important:** Existing scholarly infrastructure (Google Scholar, Semantic Scholar, OpenAlex) indexes papers and citation counts but leaves method evolution entirely implicit — an acute problem for AI research agents that cannot reliably reconstruct causal method topologies from unstructured text. - **How it does it:** An LLM-based two-phase extraction pipeline classifies every citation edge into one of seven causal types, attaches verbatim bottleneck-mechanism-tradeoff evidence records to each causal edge, and a deterministic post-checker discards edges whose quoted spans fail substring match against the source paper; a Self-Guided Temporal MCTS (SGT-MCTS) then traverses the resulting causal subgraph to reconstruct evolution chains. - **The good:** SGT-MCTS achieves Node Recall of 84.8% and Edge Recall of 79.0% against expert-curated survey chains, compared to 44.9% / 23.2% for the strongest beam-search baseline — a large margin on a meaningful external benchmark. - **The missing:** The primary idea generation evaluation scores outputs using Intern-Atlas's own evaluator, creating a circularity that inflates the method's apparent advantage; independent task-specific benchmarks (e.g., actual downstream experiment quality, accepted paper prediction) are absent. - **Read it or not:** Worth reading for researchers building AI research agents, automated literature review systems, or structured knowledge graphs over scientific corpora — the infrastructure design, verbatim-grounded evidence schema, and SGT-MCTS formulation are all practically reusable.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces Intern-Atlas, a large-scale methodological evolution graph built from 1,030,314 AI papers that explicitly encodes method-level lineage relationships, bottleneck-to-mechanism transitions, and typed causal edges, together with three graph operators for lineage reconstruction, idea evaluation, and idea generation. - Why it's important: Existing scholarly infrastructure (Google Scholar, Semantic Scholar, OpenAlex) indexes papers and citation counts but leaves method evolution entirely implicit — an acute problem for AI research agents that cannot reliably reconstruct causal method topologies from unstructured text. - How it does it: An LLM-based two-phase extraction pipeline classifies every citation edge into one of seven causal types, attaches verbatim bottleneck-mechanism-tradeoff evidence records to each causal edge, and a deterministic post-checker discards edges whose quoted spans fail substring match against the source paper; a Self-Guided Temporal MCTS (SGT-MCTS) then traverses the resulting causal subgraph to reconstruct evolution chains. - The good: SGT-MCTS achieves Node Recall of 84.8% and Edge Recall of 79.0% against expert-curated survey chains, compared to 44.9% / 23.2% for the strongest beam-search baseline — a large margin on a meaningful external benchmark. - The missing: The primary idea generation evaluation scores outputs using Intern-Atlas's own evaluator, creating a circularity that inflates the method's apparent advantage; independent task-specific benchmarks (e.g., actual downstream experiment quality, accepted paper prediction) are absent. - Read it or not: Worth reading for researchers building AI research agents, automated literature review systems, or structured knowledge graphs over scientific corpora — the infrastructure design, verbatim-grounded evidence schema, and SGT-MCTS formulation are all practically reusable. Problem & Motivation: Scholarly platforms treat papers as atomic units connected by untyped citation links, which suffices for human researchers who reconstruct method genealogies mentally. AI research agents — systems that automate hypothesis generation, retrieval, and ideation — cannot perform this reconstruction reliably: their parametric memory underrepresents low-frequency methodological knowledge, and they cannot distinguish genuine research gaps from gaps in their own representation. The result is that every agent system (AI Scientist, CoI, SPARK, CycleResearcher) reconstructs transient method-context chains from scratch at launch, with no shared persistent structured layer. The paper frames this as a historical infrastructure transition analogous to PDB preceding AlphaFold and ImageNet preceding CNNs: the emergence of a new computational consumer makes latent structure an explicit requirement. Key Idea: The core insight is that citation edges are not semantically uniform — a citation can mean extends, improves, replaces, adapts, uses_component, compares, or background — and that attaching verbatim bottleneck-mechanism evidence records to the strong-causal subset of edges converts a flat citation graph into a queryable causal topology of methodological evolution. Grounding every causal edge in a direct quote from the citing paper serves two purposes: it prevents the LLM from hallucinating bottleneck motivations, and it provides auditable provenance for downstream operators. Methodology: The system operates in two stages. Stage 1 (graph construction) processes 1,030,314 AI papers from major venues (NeurIPS, ICML, ICLR, CVPR, ACL, KDD, etc., 1965–2025) through: (i) entity resolution — a hand-curated seed of 247 methods expanded by LLM scanning, yielding 8,155 canonical methods with 9,545 aliases and 3,173,187 stub nodes for out-of-corpus references; (ii) Phase-1 edge typing — Qwen3.6-35B-A3B classifies each citation into seven causal types, tuned for high recall; (iii) Phase-2 evidence extraction — a four-field record (bottleneck, mechanism, tradeoff, each as verbatim quoted spans, plus LLM confidence) is populated for every non-background edge; (iv) a deterministic post-checker discards edges failing substring match, year-ordering, or bidirectional conflicts. Stage 2 defines three operators over the resulting graph: (a) SGT-MCTS lineage reconstruction modifies UCT with a graph-aware prior combining edge confidence and temporal coherence (peaked at 1–3 year publication gaps), ranks chains by length, mean confidence, and MCTS visit count, and re-runs from branch points to surface parallel evolutions; (b) graph-grounded idea evaluation computes five dimension scores (Novelty, Feasibility, Significance, Validity, Clarity) entirely from deterministic graph statistics — topological disconnection, duplicate-risk detection via BGE + cross-encoder reranking, method maturity curves, time-decayed in-degree — combined via a non-linear cross-dimensional regularizer with four conjunctive penalties; (c) strategy-driven idea generation extracts four structural gap patterns (open axes, recent improvement directions, sacrifice axes, disconnected pairs) and maps each to a generation strategy with required evidence certificate exact-matched against stored bottleneck records. Critical design decisions: excluding `uses_component` from lineage traversal (preserving the distinction between methodological inheritance and compositional reuse) is well-justified; the zero-trainable-parameter evaluator core is a deliberate reproducibility commitment; the TC function calibrated on post-2015 AI literature is a scope limitation the authors acknowledge. Key Results: On the method-evolution benchmark derived from 30 survey papers (2,268 nodes, 1,462 edges, 133 chains), Intern-Atlas achieves NMR=91.0%, ERR=89.7%, PSC=92.0%. SGT-MCTS achieves NR=84.8, ER=79.0, CAS=84.8 versus Beam@10's 44.9 / 23.2 / 44.9 — roughly 2× improvement across all metrics. On the Strata Dataset (1,200 papers, 300 per tier), mean overall idea scores are 8.48 (top-tier ICLR/ICML/NeurIPS) > 7.83 (core) > 6.85 (workshop) > 5.84 (rejected), showing monotonic stratification. Spearman correlation with 10 AI PhD raters on 100 profiles: 0.81 for Intern-Atlas vs. 0.58 for pure LLM-as-Judge (overall); Novelty 0.84 vs. 0.52. For idea generation scored by Intern-Atlas's own evaluator: overall 7.20 vs. best baseline (Semantic Scholar) 6.18; blind pairwise win rates 88.0% vs. No-KB, 82.0% vs. OpenAlex, 81.0% vs. BM25 RAG. The production Phase-1 extraction model (Qwen3.6-35B) achieves 70.4% edge-type classification accuracy; the audit model (Claude Sonnet 4.6) achieves 93.0% — a 22.6-point gap that the paper acknowledges but does not deeply analyze in terms of downstream impact. Novelty Classification: NEW SYSTEM/TOOL + NEW DATASET/BENCHMARK + NEW METHOD (SGT-MCTS). The methodological evolution graph concept is genuinely novel as a standing queryable infrastructure at this scale; method-entity-centric knowledge graphs over scientific literature exist (Papers With Code, S2ORC) but none attach structured verbatim causal evidence records per citation. SGT-MCTS is a focused adaptation of MCTS with a temporal-coherence prior — novel in application though algorithmically derivative. The deterministic graph-based idea evaluator without trainable parameters is a non-obvious design choice that distinguishes it from prior LLM-as-judge approaches. Rigor & Reproducibility: The primary ablation for SGT-MCTS (beam search and random walk baselines) is sound and the margins are large. The idea generation evaluation contains a circularity: the same Intern-Atlas evaluator used to score idea quality is the one whose output is being evaluated — human pairwise win rates partially compensate, but the expert panel size (10 raters, 100 queries) is modest. The benchmark of 30 survey papers is relatively small and biased toward well-known, heavily-cited lineages; performance on niche or nascent areas is not tested. The Phase-1 production model accuracy of 70.4% is concerning — the paper argues downstream operators treat edge types as routing signals and localized misclassifications only shift ranking quality, but no ablation measures the effect of edge-type noise on lineage reconstruction or evaluation quality. Error bars and seeds: the strata evaluation reports means ± 1 s.d. at n=300, which is adequate. The graph and pipeline are stated to be released as open infrastructure; no repository URL is provided in the main text. This is a preprint (arXiv:2604.28158v1, April 30, 2026), not yet peer-reviewed. Deployability (4-property check): Profile: SYSTEMS (built infrastructure evaluated by deployment and benchmarks). - Practical: The pipeline requires running Qwen3.6-35B-A3B across 1M+ papers for extraction, which demands significant GPU infrastructure, though the released artifact does not require re-running extraction to use the graph. - Efficient: Extraction cost is paid once; per-query operators use BM25 + deterministic graph traversal with B=200 MCTS rollouts, which is lightweight at inference time, though total construction compute is not stated. - Effective: SGT-MCTS recovers expert-curated evolution chains with NR=84.8% and the evaluator correlates 0.81 with PhD-expert ratings — both are meaningful but measured on AI-domain benchmarks only, leaving chemistry, physics, and biology generalization untested. - Scalable: The graph currently covers 1.03M papers; there is no ablation measuring quality degradation or construction cost at larger scales, and temporal coherence is calibrated only for post-2015 AI publication cadences. The paper delivers a software artifact (graph + pipeline, open-release) and a reusable extraction schema with verbatim evidence grounding. Strengths: - The verbatim-grounded evidence record per causal edge is a strong design choice: it provides deterministic auditability, enables post-hoc verification, and prevents hallucinated motivations from propagating through generation. - The SGT-MCTS lineage reconstruction improvement over beam search is large and measured against an independently constructed external benchmark (30 survey papers), not a self-generated reference. - The non-trivial decision to separate `uses_component` (compositional reuse) from the strong-causal subset for lineage traversal is well-reasoned and distinguishes methodological inheritance from module borrowing. - The zero-trainable-parameter evaluator design, while limiting flexibility, is auditable and reproducible in a way that learned scorers are not — a genuine trade-off acknowledged and justified. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: Phase-1 edge-type classification accuracy of 70.4% (production model) reflects difficulty in distinguishing fine-grained causal relations; the 14-axis bottleneck taxonomy is fixed at release and will require future revision; the TC function is calibrated on post-2015 AI literature and would need recalibration for other fields or research cadences; the four generation strategies cover only the most frequent topological patterns observed. The authors directly confront these, which is a credibility positive. Analyst-Identified Gaps: (1) Circular evaluation: idea generation quality is measured primarily via Intern-Atlas's own evaluator — a system cannot validly use its own scoring function to demonstrate superiority in the same task; the human win-rate study partially compensates but does not eliminate this concern. (2) The production extraction model achieves only 70.4% Phase-1 accuracy yet the paper provides no ablation showing how edge-type noise affects downstream lineage recall or evaluation quality — this is a missing chain of evidence. (3) The benchmark of 30 surveys is small and drawn from highly cited, well-documented lineages (ConvNet backbones, Transformer families) where the graph is most likely to succeed; a stress test on emerging or interdisciplinary areas is absent. (4) The strata evaluation conflates paper quality with idea quality — accepted top-tier papers are not randomly higher-quality ideas than rejected ones; venue-level selection effects (reviewers, area chairs, topic fit) confound the signal. (5) No cross-domain evaluation: the graph, extraction taxonomy, and TC function are all calibrated on AI literature; claims of general scientific infrastructure are not supported by evidence outside this domain. Bottom Line: Intern-Atlas delivers a well-engineered, large-scale infrastructure artifact with a principled evidence-grounding design and strong lineage reconstruction results, and practitioners building AI research agents will find concrete and reusable components here — but the circular idea-generation evaluation and the untested 70.4% extraction accuracy leave the downstream utility claims undersubstantiated.

How Open Must Language Models be to Enable Reliable Scientific Inference?

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces a structured framework identifying three fundamental inferential threats that closed language models pose to scientific research — versioning instability, credit assignment ambiguity, and insufficient information access — and maps which levels of model openness resolve or mitigate each threat. - **Why it's important:** A large and growing body of scientific research uses closed proprietary models (ChatGPT, Gemini, Claude) as subjects of study, but those models silently update, embed undisclosed compound-system components, and withhold token probabilities and internal states, making any published findings potentially non-reproducible and non-generalizable by design. - **How it does it:** The paper constructs a taxonomic argument by defining three inferential goals (model evaluation, model comparison, model interpretability), deriving what information each requires, and showing systematically that closed models cannot supply that information while open-weight models can. - **The good:** The credit assignment section is the sharpest contribution: it precisely delineates why the behavior of a closed model endpoint cannot be attributed to the language model it contains — because decoding strategy, system prompts, guardrails, and API pipelines are all unspecified and uncontrollable, making any behavioral finding a property of the compound system, not the model. - **The missing:** The paper does not quantify how frequently published NLP research actually falls into each threat category, nor does it survey the literature to estimate what fraction of recent closed-model papers would have reached different conclusions with open-weight alternatives — empirical grounding that would substantially strengthen the normative argument. - **Read it or not:** Essential reading for researchers who use or review studies involving closed LLMs; it provides the clearest formalization to date of why "we used GPT-4" is scientifically insufficient as a methods statement.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces a structured framework identifying three fundamental inferential threats that closed language models pose to scientific research — versioning instability, credit assignment ambiguity, and insufficient information access — and maps which levels of model openness resolve or mitigate each threat. - Why it's important: A large and growing body of scientific research uses closed proprietary models (ChatGPT, Gemini, Claude) as subjects of study, but those models silently update, embed undisclosed compound-system components, and withhold token probabilities and internal states, making any published findings potentially non-reproducible and non-generalizable by design. - How it does it: The paper constructs a taxonomic argument by defining three inferential goals (model evaluation, model comparison, model interpretability), deriving what information each requires, and showing systematically that closed models cannot supply that information while open-weight models can. - The good: The credit assignment section is the sharpest contribution: it precisely delineates why the behavior of a closed model endpoint cannot be attributed to the language model it contains — because decoding strategy, system prompts, guardrails, and API pipelines are all unspecified and uncontrollable, making any behavioral finding a property of the compound system, not the model. - The missing: The paper does not quantify how frequently published NLP research actually falls into each threat category, nor does it survey the literature to estimate what fraction of recent closed-model papers would have reached different conclusions with open-weight alternatives — empirical grounding that would substantially strengthen the normative argument. - Read it or not: Essential reading for researchers who use or review studies involving closed LLMs; it provides the clearest formalization to date of why "we used GPT-4" is scientifically insufficient as a methods statement. Problem & Motivation: Scientific research on language models has rapidly shifted from open-weight models (BERT, T5, GPT-2) to closed proprietary systems accessed only through APIs or chat interfaces. Researchers using ChatGPT, Gemini, or Claude as experimental subjects cannot control or even observe model versioning, the compound-system components layered around the core LM, or the full output probability distribution. The gap is not merely inconvenient: it threatens the three inferential goals that underlie most LM research — evaluating what a model does, comparing models, and understanding why a model behaves as it does. Prior work on model transparency (Mitchell et al. 2019; Bommasani et al. 2023) addressed accountability and governance; this paper focuses specifically on the epistemic validity of scientific inference. Key Idea: The central insight is that a closed model is not a language model but a compound system: a core LM wrapped in system prompts, decoding strategies, guardrails, retrieval APIs, and user personalization — none disclosed — and subject to silent, undated updates. Scientific findings about a closed model endpoint are therefore findings about an unknown, unstable, multi-component black box, not about a language model. Open weights resolve this because a researcher can instantiate a fixed, isolated LM and query its full output distribution, separating the LM from the compound system. Methodology: The paper's methodology is analytic rather than experimental. It defines terms precisely (closed model, open-weight model, model evaluation, model comparison, model interpretability), then derives for each inferential goal what information is required and whether closed or open-weight models supply it. Three problem categories are established: (1) The Versioning Problem — closed models update silently and irregularly, so the same label (e.g., "GPT-4o") does not refer to a stable artifact; the paper cites a 60% performance swing between March and June 2023 versions of GPT-3.5/4 on a prime-detection benchmark. (2) The Credit Assignment Problem — the behavioral output of a closed endpoint conflates the core LM with decoding strategy (unspecified), system prompt (hidden), guardrails (undocumented), and compound-system APIs, making attribution to the LM impossible. (3) The Insufficient Information Problem — closed models provide only text output; log-probabilities are rarely available; internal states are never available; yet many evaluation paradigms (multiple-choice benchmarks, surprisal-based psycholinguistic tests, calibration estimation) require per-token probabilities rather than top-prediction strings. The paper also identifies limited legitimate uses for closed models: existence proofs, upper baselines against open models, and sociotechnical research where ecological validity demands studying deployed systems. Key Results: The paper produces no quantitative experimental results. The evidence is primarily documentary: citations to platform changelogs showing undisclosed GPT updates; citations to empirical studies showing LM sensitivity to prompt formatting, temperature, and decoding strategy; citations showing that task reformulation to fit closed-model query formats systematically advantages instruction-tuned models over smaller open models (Hu and Levy 2023; Hu and Frank 2024). The 60% prime-detection performance variance across GPT versions (Chen et al. 2024c) is the most concrete data point. The paper also notes that no closed model since the original GPT-3 and Jurassic-1 has provided user-selectable token probabilities. Novelty Classification: NEW FORMULATION + NEW EMPIRICAL FINDING (secondary). The paper does not introduce a new method, dataset, or system. It contributes a precise taxonomic formalization of inferential threats that prior work on AI transparency had gestured at without fully developing. The three-problem framework (versioning, credit assignment, insufficient information) and the mapping onto specific inferential goals is the genuine intellectual contribution; the individual observations (models update, system prompts exist) are individually known but not previously organized into a coherent inferential framework with actionable scope conditions. Rigor & Reproducibility: No experiments are run, so standard reproducibility criteria (code, seeds, error bars) do not apply. The argument's rigor rests on the logical derivation and on cited empirical evidence from third parties. The taxonomy is internally consistent. The paper does not engage in self-evaluation on its own dataset, so Goodhart's Law concerns do not arise. The main weakness is selective citation: the paper cites primarily supportive evidence (sensitivity to prompts, performance instability) and does not seriously engage with the counterargument that closed models' instability may be overstated for many use cases, or that practical mitigation strategies (locking API version strings, recording execution dates) may be sufficient for some research goals. No code or data artifact is released, as none is produced. This is a preprint (arXiv:2603.26539v1, March 2026); it has not undergone peer review. Deployability (4-property check): Profile: THEORETICAL (the paper makes formal claims about what information is and is not available from model types, with no primary experimental claim). - Sound: The core logical structure — you cannot attribute compound-system behavior to a component if the other components are unobserved — is sound; the versioning argument is well-supported by cited empirical evidence. The framing is careful to distinguish what closed models "cannot" do by definition from what they "do not currently" do in practice. - General: The argument holds for any closed model that embeds an LM in an undisclosed compound system with silent versioning; it is appropriately scoped and does not overclaim for open-weight models, noting that even open weights do not guarantee reliable inference. - Tight: The framework identifies necessary (not sufficient) conditions: open weights are necessary but not sufficient for reliable mechanistic interpretability research, which also requires training data, checkpoints, and gradients. This is clearly stated. - Implementable: The paper yields a concrete decision procedure: for any research study, map the inferential goal to the framework's table (Appendix A, Table 1), identify the threats, and select a model class that resolves them. The recommendations are actionable for authors, reviewers, and editors. The paper delivers no software artifact; it stops at paper form, though it provides a summary decision table. Strengths: - The credit assignment problem section is genuinely clarifying: the observation that decoding strategy alone — an active research area with multiple non-equivalent strategies — is unspecified in every major closed model API is a precise, underappreciated point. - The scope conditions are honest: the paper explicitly carves out legitimate uses for closed models (existence proofs, sociotechnical research) rather than arguing for blanket prohibition. - The framework is extensible: the three-problem taxonomy provides a vocabulary that reviewers and editors can operationalize without requiring deep technical background. - The distinction between "open weights resolve the versioning problem" and "open weights do not guarantee it" (a hosted open-weight endpoint could still update) reflects unusual care in avoiding overclaim. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors acknowledge that the paper focuses only on model evaluation, comparison, and interpretability research on pretrained models, excluding human-computer interaction and some sociotechnical questions. They note the analysis was written from the perspective of external researchers without insider access. They acknowledge the three problems are not exhaustive — training data contamination presents additional inferential threats not fully treated. They explicitly state the discussion of mitigations beyond open weights (training data, checkpoints, gradients) is not exhaustive. Analyst-Identified Gaps: The paper's central normative argument — that closed models are generally unsuitable for scientific use — rests on logical derivation rather than empirical demonstration of how frequently this actually produces wrong conclusions in published literature. A survey of published NLP papers using closed models, assessing what fraction report findings that cannot be reproduced or are likely confounded by compound-system effects, would substantially strengthen the case. The paper also largely ignores the practical economics: running competitive open-weight models at the scale needed to study frontier capabilities requires infrastructure unavailable to most academic groups, which its §5.2 discussion only partially addresses. Additionally, the paper treats closed model APIs as monolithic, but some (e.g., OpenAI's Completions endpoint with logprobs, Anthropic's token counting) partially mitigate the insufficient information problem; the granularity of this partial mitigation is not analyzed. Finally, the paper does not address the possibility that future closed model providers could resolve all three problems while remaining commercially closed, which would undercut the paper's practical recommendation to treat open weights as the default. Bottom Line: A practitioner who designs or reviews empirical NLP research using closed models will gain an immediately usable framework for identifying and documenting inferential threats — the credit assignment and versioning sections alone justify reading.

STEM: Structure-Tracing Evidence Mining for Knowledge Graphs-Driven Retrieval-Augmented Generation

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces STEM (Structure-Tracing Evidence Mining), a KG-RAG framework that reframes multi-hop question answering as schema-guided subgraph matching by projecting natural language queries into KG-aligned schema graphs and using a Triple-Dependent GNN to inject global structural priors into edge-level retrieval scoring. - **Why it's important:** LLM-based KGQA systems routinely fail on complex multi-hop questions because they generate relations that are semantically plausible but topologically absent in the target KG (schema hallucination), and retrieve fragmented paths rather than complete evidence subgraphs, causing systematic accuracy and coverage losses. - **How it does it:** Two fine-tuned 8B models (SGDA, SAGB) convert natural language queries into KG-symbolic schema graphs; a 6-layer Triple-Dependent GNN over those triples produces a Global Guidance Subgraph whose entity and triple membership biases the cosine-similarity-based isomorphic search at each step. - **The good:** STEM+GPT-4o achieves 90.94% Hit@1 / 76.18% F1 on WebQSP and 74.09% Hit@1 / 65.33% F1 on CWQ — SOTA on 3 of 4 metrics — while running ~6× faster than the strongest prompting baseline (FiDeLiS). - **The missing:** All training and evaluation is confined to Freebase-based benchmarks; no zero-shot or cross-KG transfer experiments are run, so generalizability to heterogeneous KGs is entirely undemonstrated despite real-world deployment claims. - **Read it or not:** Worth reading for researchers building structured KG-RAG systems, particularly those dealing with schema heterogeneity or multi-answer retrieval; less useful if your focus is open-domain or schema-free RAG.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces STEM (Structure-Tracing Evidence Mining), a KG-RAG framework that reframes multi-hop question answering as schema-guided subgraph matching by projecting natural language queries into KG-aligned schema graphs and using a Triple-Dependent GNN to inject global structural priors into edge-level retrieval scoring. - Why it's important: LLM-based KGQA systems routinely fail on complex multi-hop questions because they generate relations that are semantically plausible but topologically absent in the target KG (schema hallucination), and retrieve fragmented paths rather than complete evidence subgraphs, causing systematic accuracy and coverage losses. - How it does it: Two fine-tuned 8B models (SGDA, SAGB) convert natural language queries into KG-symbolic schema graphs; a 6-layer Triple-Dependent GNN over those triples produces a Global Guidance Subgraph whose entity and triple membership biases the cosine-similarity-based isomorphic search at each step. - The good: STEM+GPT-4o achieves 90.94% Hit@1 / 76.18% F1 on WebQSP and 74.09% Hit@1 / 65.33% F1 on CWQ — SOTA on 3 of 4 metrics — while running ~6× faster than the strongest prompting baseline (FiDeLiS). - The missing: All training and evaluation is confined to Freebase-based benchmarks; no zero-shot or cross-KG transfer experiments are run, so generalizability to heterogeneous KGs is entirely undemonstrated despite real-world deployment claims. - Read it or not: Worth reading for researchers building structured KG-RAG systems, particularly those dealing with schema heterogeneity or multi-answer retrieval; less useful if your focus is open-domain or schema-free RAG. Problem & Motivation LLMs used for knowledge graph question answering face three interrelated failure modes. First, schema hallucination: LLMs decomposing queries or generating reasoning plans lack prior knowledge of the target KG's relational vocabulary, so they produce semantically plausible but topologically nonexistent relations (e.g., generating "fly into" when the KG encodes "location.location.nearby_airports"). Second, evidence fragmentation: stepwise beam-search or path-matching approaches operate with only local semantic similarity, making them vulnerable to hub-node explosion and producing disconnected reasoning chains rather than coherent evidence subgraphs. Third, interactive methods that invoke the LLM at every search step scale poorly — especially for multi-answer questions requiring parallel path traversal. These problems collectively degrade Hit@1, F1, and ground-truth coverage, particularly on the more complex CWQ benchmark. Key Idea The key insight is to treat query planning and KG retrieval as a structure translation problem rather than a semantic search problem: first fine-tune specialized models to translate natural language queries into KG-symbolic schema graphs that are topologically valid (bridging the semantic-structural gap), then use a GNN conditioned on those schema triples to compute a soft global structural prior that re-weights edge scores during subgraph isomorphism search — combining structural reasoning and semantic matching rather than relying on either alone. Methodology The pipeline has five stages. (1) SGDA (Schema-Grounded Decomposition Agent): a fine-tuned Qwen3-8B decomposes the query into atomic relational assertions using beam search (B=4) and classifies the query as Precision (single deterministic answer) or Breadth (multiple answers). Trained on ~25k standard examples augmented with ~210k synthetic examples from Structure-to-Query Reverse Generation, which samples subgraphs from the KG and uses Gemini 2.5 Pro to generate aligned questions, enabling pattern generalization rather than fact memorization. (2) SAGB (Symbol-Aligned Graph Builder): a separate fine-tuned Qwen3-8B maps each textual assertion to the KG's symbolic relation vocabulary (e.g., "hockey position" → "ice_hockey.hockey_player.hockey_position"), trained on ~29k entries. Together SGDA+SAGB form the Semantic-to-Structural Projection pipeline. (3) Triple-GNN: a 6-layer message-passing GNN conditioned on query triples (average-pooled embedding EQ), using DistMult aggregation, that outputs per-entity probability scores; top-K entities (K=|T|×4) are connected into the Global Guidance Subgraph. (4) Structure-Tracing Subgraph Retrieval: starts from the highest-scoring anchor entity and recursively matches schema graph edges to KG edges via T-Score = cosine_sim(encoded triple pair) + ITri, where entity-level bias IEnt multiplies scores of Guidance Graph entities by 1.5, and triple-level bias ITri adds 0.5 for Guidance Graph triples. Precision strategy uses greedy edge selection; Breadth strategy retains all edges above threshold θ=0.6. (5) Generation: DFS linearization of the retrieved subgraph into reasoning chains, fed to a separate LLM (Llama-3.1-8B/70B or GPT-4o). Three critical design decisions: pattern-learning over fact-memorization in SGDA/SAGB training (well-justified by ablation), modest bias values (λ=1.5, τ=0.5) to avoid Structural Over-Interpretation (justified by grid search), and beam size B=4 for schema diversity (justified by ablation). Key Results STEM+GPT-4o: 90.94% Hit@1, 76.18% F1 on WebQSP (1,628 test); 74.09% Hit@1, 65.33% F1 on CWQ (3,531 test). Beats RoG+GPT-4o by 2.85%/4.48% Hit@1 on WebQSP/CWQ. Beats FiDeLiS+GPT-4o-turbo by ~6.5% Hit@1 on WebQSP, with improvements across all CWQ metrics. STEM+Llama-3.1-8B outperforms RoG+Llama-3.1-70B on CWQ (68.76% vs 67.43% Hit@1), demonstrating retrieval quality independent of model scale. Ablation (Table 7): removing both GNN biases drops CWQ Hit@1 by 10.18 points; retrieval coverage analysis (Table 12) shows guidance raises ground-truth path coverage from 65.07% to 73.68% on WebQSP and 59.8% to 70.39% on CWQ. Error attribution (Table 26): 83.01% of cases show correct retrieval producing correct answers; retrieval failures cause 57.3% of all incorrect answers. STEM runs at 5.77s/query vs FiDeLiS at 34.97s (~6× faster). Noteworthy weakness: STEM trails GNN-RAG on CWQ 2-hop questions (66.73% vs 69.4% F1, Table 3) — unexplained. ProgRAG+GPT-4o-mini achieves 90.4% Hit@1 on WebQSP without fine-tuning, matching STEM+GPT-4o — a competitive result the paper does not analyze deeply. Baselines are current and representative (FiDeLiS, GNN-RAG, ProgRAG, RoG). Three independent runs reported with averages. Novelty Classification NEW METHOD, NEW SYSTEM/TOOL. The genuine novelty is the integrated pipeline: schema-aware query projection via fine-tuned models that learn KG relation patterns from reverse-generated data, combined with triple-conditioned GNN global guidance injected as soft biases into isomorphism search. The subgraph matching extends SimGRAG; the GNN architecture follows GFM-RAG's query-dependent design; decomposition is inspired by CoG. What is original is the training strategy (pattern learning via reverse generation), the Precision/Breadth strategy classification, and the dual-level (entity + triple) structural bias mechanism — a standard combination of existing ideas but with a novel integration that demonstrably closes the semantic-structural gap. Rigor & Reproducibility Ablations are present and informative: schema pipeline comparison (Table 6), GNN bias ablations (Table 7), coverage rate ablations (Table 12), augmented data ablation (Table 13), and hyperparameter sweeps for K, B, λ, τ, and θ. Results averaged over 3 full runs; no per-run variance or confidence intervals reported. Code is publicly released (https://github.com/PennyYu123/STEM_RAG); synthetic dataset Dsyn (214k entries) to be released. Key methodological concern: bias hyperparameters (λ, τ) and threshold θ were tuned on validation splits of the same benchmarks used for evaluation — standard but worth noting. The Dsyn augmentation data is generated from the same Freebase-based KGs as the evaluation benchmarks, creating potential schema overlap that could inflate generalization estimates. Comparing fine-tuned STEM against zero-shot prompting baselines in the main table (Table 1) without clear visual separation is misleading despite the footnote acknowledgment. Deployability (4-property check) Profile: EMPIRICAL / SYSTEMS - Practical: All components run on commercially available hardware; paper uses 4×NVIDIA H100 for training and single H100 for inference evaluation; the Qwen3-8B and embedding components are accessible open-source models. - Efficient: Per-query latency averages 5.77s (WebQSP) with Precision mode at ~4.9s and Breadth at ~8.4s on a single H100; the 2×B=8 SGDA/SAGB forward passes dominate; GNN forward pass is preindexable; substantially better than interactive LLM methods but slower than pure retrieval baselines (GNN-RAG: 2.64s). - Effective: Direct retrieval coverage measurement (Table 12) and error attribution matrices (Tables 25, 26) provide strong evidence that the retrieval mechanism itself works — 83.01% of correct retrievals produce correct answers — rather than relying solely on end-to-end QA metrics. - Scalable: Only tested on Freebase-based WebQSP and CWQ; no experiments on larger or structurally different KGs; the authors explicitly state STEM is not zero-shot and its transferability to unseen KG schemas is unvalidated. Delivers: code repository, fine-tuned model weights (implicitly via training configs), and synthetic dataset Dsyn. Strengths - The Structure-to-Query Reverse Generation strategy for training SGDA/SAGB is practically effective: the 210k synthetic dataset improves schema graph Recall by 15% on WebQSP and Precision by 15% on CWQ (Figure 7), enabling pattern generalization that raw training data alone cannot provide. - The dual-level GNN bias mechanism (entity + triple, with modest magnitudes) strikes a principled balance between structural guidance and semantic flexibility; the grid-search analysis of Structural Over-Interpretation explains concretely why hard structural constraints fail. - The error attribution matrices (Tables 25, 26) provide rare transparency into pipeline failure modes, revealing that upstream planning errors cause only 16.4% of QA failures while retrieval accounts for 57.3% — actionable for future work. - The Precision/Breadth strategy classification (>90% accuracy per Table 15) is a simple but effective mechanism for handling multi-answer questions, yielding ~4–9% F1 improvements in multi-answer subsets (Table 2). Author-Acknowledged Limitations The authors state three limitations directly: (1) planning deviations can still produce schema graphs that entirely miss the factual KG structure, causing retrieval failure; (2) STEM requires domain-specific fine-tuning and access to the target KG's schema, making it inapplicable zero-shot to unseen KGs; (3) Breadth strategy increases latency. The authors frame these as known trade-offs rather than minimizing them — a credibility positive. Analyst-Identified Gaps - Single-KG confinement is deeper than acknowledged: the Dsyn synthetic data is derived from the same Freebase KG graphs as WebQSP/CWQ, so the SGDA/SAGB effectively memorize Freebase relational patterns; performance on Wikidata, DBpedia, or enterprise KGs is purely speculative. - The CWQ hop=2 underperformance versus GNN-RAG (66.73% vs 69.4% F1, Table 3) is noted but not analyzed — a 2-hop query is the simplest multi-hop case, and underperformance here suggests a structural vulnerability in the schema projection that is not explained. - ProgRAG+GPT-4o-mini achieves 90.4% Hit@1 on WebQSP without any fine-tuning, nearly matching STEM+GPT-4o; this result receives no comparative analysis and raises the question of whether the complexity of STEM's pipeline is necessary for WebQSP. - The efficiency comparison for GPT-4o variants conflates API call latency (variable, not controllable) with local model inference latency; all latency figures use local Llama models and should not be directly attributed to the GPT-4o variant results. - No measurement of false-positive rate in retrieved subgraphs (precision of retrieval, not just coverage/recall of ground-truth paths) — the Breadth strategy's threshold-based branching likely introduces irrelevant triples that inflate context for the LLM. Bottom Line A practitioner building KGQA over Freebase-style structured KGs gains concrete value from STEM's schema projection pipeline and retrieval coverage improvements, but should treat cross-KG and zero-shot generalization claims as unvalidated until tested beyond Freebase benchmarks.

Defragmenting Language Models: An Interpretability-based Approach for Vocabulary Expansion

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces FragMend, an interpretability-based vocabulary expansion method that leverages "subword detokenization"—LLMs' internal progressive reconstruction of subwords across transformer layers—to select vocabulary items and initialize their embeddings, achieving up to 3× more token reduction than the prior interpretability baseline while preserving downstream performance. - **Why it's important:** Speakers of languages written in non-Latin scripts (e.g., Odia, Burmese) face a 5–10× tokenization premium over English speakers, directly inflating API costs and degrading model performance, and the standard BPE-based remedy forces a poor performance–efficiency tradeoff that interpretability-based methods can substantially improve. - **How it does it:** For each candidate subword affix extracted from a small unlabeled corpus, FragMend runs the Patchscopes activation-patching framework to detect whether the LLM internally reconstructs that subword; successfully detokenized subwords are added to the vocabulary with embeddings initialized via an orthogonal Procrustes transformation from the hidden state at the earliest detokenization layer. - **The good:** Interpretability-based item selection (vs. BPE) shifts the entire performance–efficiency Pareto frontier: BPE-expanded models can lose 60+ F1 points for languages like Odia at equivalent budget, while FragMend achieves 20–60% token reduction with single-digit performance drops on SIB200 and Belebele, using only 1000 training sequences and a single H200 GPU. - **The missing:** No evaluation on generative tasks (the method is only tested on topic classification and reading comprehension), and hypernetwork-based baselines (ZeTT, HYPEROFA) are explicitly deferred to future work—leaving the strongest competitors unaddressed. - **Read it or not:** Worth reading for anyone working on multilingual tokenization, vocabulary adaptation, or mechanistic interpretability; practitioners needing generation-quality guarantees should wait for generative task evaluation.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces FragMend, an interpretability-based vocabulary expansion method that leverages "subword detokenization"—LLMs' internal progressive reconstruction of subwords across transformer layers—to select vocabulary items and initialize their embeddings, achieving up to 3× more token reduction than the prior interpretability baseline while preserving downstream performance. - Why it's important: Speakers of languages written in non-Latin scripts (e.g., Odia, Burmese) face a 5–10× tokenization premium over English speakers, directly inflating API costs and degrading model performance, and the standard BPE-based remedy forces a poor performance–efficiency tradeoff that interpretability-based methods can substantially improve. - How it does it: For each candidate subword affix extracted from a small unlabeled corpus, FragMend runs the Patchscopes activation-patching framework to detect whether the LLM internally reconstructs that subword; successfully detokenized subwords are added to the vocabulary with embeddings initialized via an orthogonal Procrustes transformation from the hidden state at the earliest detokenization layer. - The good: Interpretability-based item selection (vs. BPE) shifts the entire performance–efficiency Pareto frontier: BPE-expanded models can lose 60+ F1 points for languages like Odia at equivalent budget, while FragMend achieves 20–60% token reduction with single-digit performance drops on SIB200 and Belebele, using only 1000 training sequences and a single H200 GPU. - The missing: No evaluation on generative tasks (the method is only tested on topic classification and reading comprehension), and hypernetwork-based baselines (ZeTT, HYPEROFA) are explicitly deferred to future work—leaving the strongest competitors unaddressed. - Read it or not: Worth reading for anyone working on multilingual tokenization, vocabulary adaptation, or mechanistic interpretability; practitioners needing generation-quality guarantees should wait for generative task evaluation. Problem & Motivation: Languages written in non-Latin scripts are severely penalized by BPE-based tokenizers, which reflect the Latin-script dominance of pre-training data. Odia requires ~10× more tokens than English for equivalent text; Hindi ~4.5×; Gujarati ~6.8×—even when officially supported by the model. This token over-fragmentation translates directly into higher API costs, higher latency, and worse downstream task performance for affected language communities. Since retraining an LLM with an equitable tokenizer is computationally infeasible, vocabulary expansion—adding target-language items to an existing tokenizer and fine-tuning only the corresponding embeddings—is the practical remedy. Prior work treats frequency-based item selection (BPE merges on a target-language corpus) as an unquestioned default, focusing research effort solely on how to initialize embeddings for the newly added items. This paper challenges that assumption and asks whether interpretability-driven selection can produce a better efficiency–performance tradeoff. Key Idea: LLMs maintain an internal lexicon far richer than their surface vocabulary: when a multi-token word is processed, hidden representations at intermediate layers progressively merge the subword tokens, reconstructing a coherent representation of the full word—a process called "detokenization." The paper extends this finding to subwords: the model also detokenizes subword affixes (e.g., reconstructing "Lay" from "L"+"ay" before reconstructing "Layla" from all three tokens). Subwords that are successfully detokenized by the model's internal machinery are semantically grounded within the model's existing representational space; adding them to the vocabulary and initializing their embeddings from the corresponding activation provides a far better starting point than frequency-based candidates initialized heuristically. Methodology: The pipeline has three stages; this work fixes Stage 3 (LAPT fine-tuning of only the new embeddings, all other weights frozen) and systematically studies Stages 1 and 2. For Stage 1 (Item Selection), the Patchscopes framework (Ghandeharioun et al., 2024) is used: (i) a first forward pass computes hidden activations h^l_{ti} at every layer l for all subword tokens of a candidate word/affix; (ii) a second pass patches each activation as the input to a repetition-seeking prompt ("In [language]: X, X, X, X,") and checks whether the model outputs the target word/affix. A word is "detokenized" if it appears in any output across subwords and layers. FragMend extends the candidate set from unique full words in a training corpus (Tokens2Words) to all unique token affixes (prefixes, infixes, suffixes) of every word, enabling 2–3× more vocabulary additions from the same corpus. Because subwords appear in multiple words, a reduction strategy is required: either average candidate initializations (R_m) or select the earliest-layer instance (R_e). Two additional design choices address "vocabulary expansion perversity" (an HF tokenizer implementation artifact where newly added short items disrupt existing merges): a minimum-length filter removing items shorter than 3 UTF-8 characters (T), and full-word preference (P) when a subword coincides with a standalone word. Ablations identify T as the dominant choice (+15 pts in some languages), yielding preferred config R_e,T,P. For Stage 2 (Embedding Initialization), input and output embeddings for new items are initialized by applying a learned orthogonal Procrustes transformation T_{l,E} / T_{l,U} to the hidden state at the earliest successful detokenization layer. The transformation is fit on in-vocabulary items to map layer-l activations to embedding space. This is compared against Random, FVT (constituent-subword average), and FOCUS (fastText-weighted combination) baselines. Key Results: Primary model: Qwen3-30B-A3B; secondary: Qwen3.5-{0.8B,2B,4B,9B} and TinyAya-Global-3B. Evaluation benchmarks: SIB200 (topic classification, F1), Belebele (reading comprehension, accuracy), bits-per-byte on Glot500c. Training corpus: Glot500c, 14 languages, |C_train| ∈ {1k, 10k} sequences. Three seeds (most experiments). Pareto analysis (Figure 1c / Figure 6): For all five highly over-fragmented languages shown (amh, fas, guj, hin, ory), both Tokens2Words and FragMend dominate the BPE Pareto frontier regardless of BPE initialization method. BPE achieves higher token reduction at maximum budget but at performance costs up to 60+ F1/accuracy points for Odia (SIB200). For Hindi, Tokens2Words outperforms BPE on both dimensions simultaneously. Initialization comparison (Figures 3, 12): With Tokens2Words-selected items, Tokens2Words initialization outperforms FVT, Random, and FOCUS for most non-Latin script languages (~20 pts over baselines in pronounced cases); FVT leads for Latin-script languages. FOCUS is consistently weakest, consistent with prior literature. FragMend vs. Tokens2Words (Figure 4a, Table 5): FragMend is strictly more token-efficient for every language tested. For guj, hin, srp the efficiency gain comes with zero performance drop. For ory (Belebele): FragMend R_e,T,P reaches 63.0% vs. original 81.9% with ~40–55% token reduction; Tokens2Words reaches 73.0% with ~10–15% reduction. For mya: FragMend 61.6–62.2% vs. original 76.0% with ~40–60% reduction. FragMend fails to help for out-of-distribution languages (amh, gla, sot) due to weak model activations. Model scale (Figures 4b–c, 22–23): Token reduction increases monotonically from 0.8B to 9B parameters (4B Qwen3.5 exception: thinking-token generation inhibits detokenization). Larger models benefit more, supporting the hypothesis that interpretability-based methods will grow more effective as models improve. Novelty Classification: NEW METHOD + NEW EMPIRICAL FINDING.
