The core novelty is twofold: (1) empirically establishing that item selection (Stage 1), not initialization, is the fundamental bottleneck in vocabulary expansion—a claim backed by Pareto analysis across five languages and multiple initialization strategies; (2) the discovery that subword-level detokenization is a generalizable phenomenon occurring across diverse language families, extending prior full-word detokenization findings (Kaplan et al., 2025). FragMend's specific combination of subword affix mining, Patchscopes probing, earliest-layer reduction, and length filtering is new. The orthogonal Procrustes embedding initialization and LAPT fine-tuning are from Kaplan et al. 2025, applied to a new (subword) setting. Rigor & Reproducibility: Most experiments use three random seeds ({42, 20, 1984}); BPE Pareto uses one seed but seed stability is shown separately (Figure 7b–d). Error bars are visible in scale analysis figures (Figures 22–23) but not in main bar charts. Ablation of all three FragMend design choices is systematic (Tables 1 and 5), with clear magnitude estimates for each component's contribution—the length filter dominates. Code is publicly released at github.com/utahnlp/fragmend (there is a second anonymized URL in Appendix D, suggesting simultaneous double-blind submission; this is an arXiv preprint, arXiv:2604.16656v1). Evaluation uses standard public benchmarks (SIB200, Belebele, Glot500c). The Pareto analysis caps BPE's budget at the number of unique words in C_train to equalize comparison—a reasonable but potentially flattering constraint for interpretability methods at larger budgets. Hypernetwork-based baselines (ZeTT, HYPEROFA) are explicitly deferred. Deployability (EMPIRICAL): - Practical: Runs on a single H200 GPU (or MIG slice); requires only 1000 unlabeled target-language sentences and no retraining of model weights—deployable by any lab with a single high-end GPU and access to a small monolingual corpus. - Efficient: Patchscopes batch size of 2 (vs. 16 for Tokens2Words) makes FragMend 8× slower per candidate; the expanded candidate set (all subword affixes) is much larger than unique words, so total compute is substantially higher than Tokens2Words, though the paper does not report wall-clock times. - Effective: Achieves the claimed goal—meaningful token reduction with preserved task accuracy on classification and reading comprehension—but generative task performance (translation, instruction-following, summarization) is not evaluated, leaving a gap for production deployments. - Scalable: Tested across 4 model sizes (0.8B to 9B) and one 30B MoE model; method scales favorably with model size. Evaluation covers only 14 languages; extension to all 111 over-fragmented languages and to multi-language simultaneous expansion is untested. The paper delivers a software artifact (GitHub code) and a paper-form method; it does not release pre-expanded models or tokenizers. Strengths: - Reframes the vocabulary expansion problem: showing that item selection is the binding constraint shifts focus from the well-studied initialization problem to an under-studied one, and the Pareto evidence is initialization-independent (holds for Random, FVT, and FOCUS initializations of BPE items). - Subword detokenization as an empirical finding contributes to mechanistic interpretability literature: the layerwise progressive merging across both high- and low-resource, Latin- and non-Latin-script languages is a substantive observation about how LLMs organize lexical knowledge. - Practical accessibility: 1000 training sequences is attainable even for genuinely low-resource languages; the pipeline requires no API access, no bilingual dictionary, no auxiliary embedding space, and freezes all parameters except the new embeddings. - Clean, systematic ablation (Table 1/5): the contribution of each design choice (reduction strategy, length filter, full-word preference) is quantified, the dominant factor is identified, and the preferred configuration is argued from first principles. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The method's effectiveness depends on the quality of the LLM's internal representations; small models (TinyAya-3B, Qwen3.5-4B) show diminished gains due to the "curse of multilinguality" and fewer layers for detokenization. Languages not officially supported by the model (amh, gla, sot, mri in some experiments) show consistently poor detokenization and thus poor initialization. The tuning strategy (LAPT, embeddings-only) is fixed; more sophisticated multi-parameter tuning could improve all methods. The authors frame these honestly and do not minimize them. Analyst-Identified Gaps: Hypernetwork-based baselines (ZeTT/Minixhofer et al. 2024; HYPEROFA/Özeren et al. 2025) are explicitly deferred but are published, available methods—their omission leaves the strongest competitive comparison open. No generative task evaluation means it is unknown whether vocabulary expansion with interpretability-based initialization helps or hurts open-ended generation, translation, or instruction-following—the tasks most affected by API costs for end users. The "vocabulary expansion perversity" fix (minimum 3 UTF-8 character length) is an empirically chosen threshold with no theoretical justification and may not generalize across scripts with very different character byte sizes (Burmese characters are 3 bytes each, so a 3-character Burmese subword ≥ 9 bytes). The paper evaluates single-target-language expansion in isolation; simultaneous multi-language expansion (the practical deployment scenario) is not studied. The Patchscopes prompt template ("In [language]: X, X, X, X,") is English-anchored; its cross-lingual reliability for non-Latin languages that the model does not officially support is not ablated. Bottom Line: A practitioner working on multilingual tokenization or LLM adaptation for low-resource non-Latin-script languages gains concrete, deployable methodology and a clear argument for why to look beyond BPE for item selection—this paper is worth reading; those in generative NLP or seeking production-ready comparisons should treat it as a promising preprint awaiting generative-task and hypernetwork-baseline evaluation.

Self-Evolving LLM Memory Extraction Across Heterogeneous Tasks

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces BEHEMOTH, an 18-dataset benchmark for heterogeneous memory extraction, and CluE, a cluster-based self-evolving framework that groups training examples by extraction scenario to stably improve memory extraction prompts across diverse task types. - **Why it's important:** Deployed LLM assistants encounter wildly different interaction types — personalization chats, math problem-solving, agentic tool use — and no single fixed extraction rule generalizes across them; this work is the first to formalize and benchmark that generalization challenge. - **How it does it:** CluE summarizes each training example's extraction scenario, clusters examples by those scenarios rather than by surface task or domain, performs per-cluster failure/success analysis, then synthesizes cross-cluster insights into a unified updated extraction prompt. - **The good:** CluE achieves +9.04% relative gain over the Simple baseline across 18 datasets and consistently improves all three task categories simultaneously (+12.34% personalization, +8.39% problem-solving, +7.22% agentic), whereas every competing self-evolving framework improves one category at the expense of another. - **The missing:** All experiments use the same model for both memory extraction and target-query answering; no evaluation tests whether prompts evolved with one backbone transfer to a different extraction model. - **Read it or not:** Worth reading for researchers building persistent LLM assistants or studying prompt optimization under heterogeneous data distributions; the BEHEMOTH benchmark and the cluster-by-scenario insight are both reusable contributions.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces BEHEMOTH, an 18-dataset benchmark for heterogeneous memory extraction, and CluE, a cluster-based self-evolving framework that groups training examples by extraction scenario to stably improve memory extraction prompts across diverse task types. - Why it's important: Deployed LLM assistants encounter wildly different interaction types — personalization chats, math problem-solving, agentic tool use — and no single fixed extraction rule generalizes across them; this work is the first to formalize and benchmark that generalization challenge. - How it does it: CluE summarizes each training example's extraction scenario, clusters examples by those scenarios rather than by surface task or domain, performs per-cluster failure/success analysis, then synthesizes cross-cluster insights into a unified updated extraction prompt. - The good: CluE achieves +9.04% relative gain over the Simple baseline across 18 datasets and consistently improves all three task categories simultaneously (+12.34% personalization, +8.39% problem-solving, +7.22% agentic), whereas every competing self-evolving framework improves one category at the expense of another. - The missing: All experiments use the same model for both memory extraction and target-query answering; no evaluation tests whether prompts evolved with one backbone transfer to a different extraction model. - Read it or not: Worth reading for researchers building persistent LLM assistants or studying prompt optimization under heterogeneous data distributions; the BEHEMOTH benchmark and the cluster-by-scenario insight are both reusable contributions. Problem & Motivation: As LLM assistants become persistent across sessions, they must extract and store useful information from prior conversations as memory. Existing extraction systems use static, domain-specific rules: personalization agents extract user preferences, agentic systems extract reusable strategies, problem-solving systems extract solution patterns. A general-purpose assistant, however, encounters all of these simultaneously, and no fixed extraction schema works across the full breadth. The field lacks a benchmark that spans this heterogeneity, and existing self-evolving frameworks — which iteratively refine the extraction prompt from task feedback — were designed for homogeneous distributions. Under heterogeneous training data, these methods exhibit a characteristic failure: updating too frequently causes successive batches of dissimilar examples to pull the prompt in conflicting directions; updating too infrequently washes out fine-grained task signals by averaging over dissimilar scenarios. Key Idea: The core insight is that memory extraction tasks should be clustered by extraction scenario (what type of information must be extracted and what makes extraction hard) rather than by surface task, domain, or dataset label. This allows per-cluster analysis to produce targeted, non-conflicting prompt improvements that can then be synthesized into a single coherent prompt covering all scenarios. Two examples from different datasets (e.g., an agentic trajectory and a math solution) may both require extracting multi-step procedural knowledge, and grouping them together surfaces shared extraction challenges that neither dataset-specific nor fully mixed analysis would reveal. Methodology: The paper formalizes single-step memory extraction as a triple (source conversation c, target query q, reward function R): an extraction model LLM_e produces a memory string m conditioned on an extraction prompt P; a generation model LLM_g answers q given m; quality is measured by R(answer). BEHEMOTH is constructed by repurposing 18 existing datasets into this formulation across three categories — personalization (5 datasets: MemBench-High/Low, PersonaMem-v2, PrefEval, LongMemEval), problem-solving (7: Game of 24, AIME, MMLU Pro Physics/Engineering, MathEquationBalancer, BigCodeBench, GPQA-Diamond), and agentic (6: AlfWorld, FEVER, PDDL, BabyAI, ScienceWorld, ToolBench). Problem-solving and agentic datasets lack multi-turn conversations, so LLM_g is prompted to generate source conversations from task instances; this introduces synthetic data at the source-conversation level. Training uses 20 examples per dataset (330 total, randomly shuffled); held-out OOD test sets are one dataset per category (LongMemEval, GPQA-Diamond, ToolBench). CluE operates in rounds. In each round: (1) Summarizer — an LLM reads each training log and writes a 2–3 sentence extraction scenario summary focusing on what type of information is needed and what makes extraction hard, abstracting away domain specifics; (2) Cluster Manager — an LLM assigns each example to one of up to 7 clusters based on extraction scenario similarity, with the ability to create, merge, or split clusters as distributions evolve; (3) Cluster Analyzer — for each cluster, an LLM with tool access inspects that cluster's training logs, identifies success and failure patterns, and produces targeted prompt recommendations; (4) Proposer — an LLM synthesizes per-cluster analyses into one improved extraction prompt, resolving conflicts by scoping recommendations to appropriate memory categories. CluE uses the same batch size (35) and tournament-based evaluation as MemEvolve, adding 221 optimization LLM calls over 5 rounds (~5.5h wall time). Evaluation uses Macro Accuracy (uniform average across datasets) and Relative Gain (geometric mean of per-dataset improvement ratios over Simple baseline), with three evaluation runs to report mean ± standard deviation. Key Results: On in-distribution test sets starting from the Simple seed (Qwen3-32B), CluE achieves 50.01% macro accuracy and +9.04% relative gain over Simple (46.00%). The three competing self-evolving methods all exhibit category tradeoffs: GEPA reaches +14.08% agentic but −2.16% personalization; MemEvolve reaches +10.76% personalization but −3.25% agentic; ACE shows +8.04% problem-solving but −4.38% personalization. CluE is the only method with positive relative gain in all three categories simultaneously. Starting from the stronger Survey seed, all baseline methods produce negative or flat overall relative gains (ACE: −1.44%, MemEvolve: −0.74%, GEPA: 0%), while CluE achieves +6.54% overall and positive gains in all three categories. On OOD test sets, CluE achieves 63.07% on LongMemEval (vs. 56.82% for MemEvolve), 48.48% on GPQA-Diamond (above the Simple baseline of 47.14%), and 26.67% on ToolBench. With Gemini-3-Flash as extraction backend, CluE achieves +3.40% relative gain; all three baselines either regress or show marginal improvement (ACE: −3.93%, MemEvolve: −0.29%, GEPA: +0.93%). In the continual memory setting, CluE (50.83 on Game of 24; 67.25 on AlfWorld) outperforms MemEvolve (43.33; 62.57). Qualitatively, CluE's evolved prompt contains 936 tokens — shorter than ACE's 1,403 tokens — and develops a structured 5-category memory taxonomy with per-category extraction guidelines. Novelty Classification: NEW PROBLEM + NEW DATASET/BENCHMARK + NEW METHOD. The formalization of heterogeneous memory extraction as a distinct generalization challenge is a new problem framing. BEHEMOTH is a new benchmark (repurposed but systematically unified under one protocol). CluE's cluster-by-extraction-scenario evolution strategy is a new method in the self-evolving prompt optimization space. The core novelty is the scenario-clustering insight, which is non-obvious: prior self-evolving methods were aware of task heterogeneity but attempted to handle it through architectural variants (validation sets in GEPA, atomic operations in ACE) rather than by restructuring the feedback aggregation itself. Rigor & Reproducibility: Ablations are limited — the paper does not ablate the effect of removing any single CluE component (e.g., Summarizer vs. Cluster Manager vs. Proposer), so it is not established which pipeline stage is responsible for performance gains. Error bars (standard deviations over three runs) are reported for evolving framework results but not for static prompt baselines, which are run once. The evaluation is downstream-utility-driven (avoiding LLM-as-judge bias), which is a sound design choice. The benchmark construction has a potential concern: problem-solving and agentic source conversations are LLM-generated by LLM_g (Qwen3-32B), meaning the source conversations and the answering model share characteristics; this could inflate performance for all methods on those categories relative to real-world deployment. Code and data are publicly released at https://github.com/ayyyq/heterogeneous-memory-extraction. This is a preprint; peer review has not been completed. Deployability (4-property check): Profile: EMPIRICAL (system evaluated through experiments). - Practical: CluE requires only API-level or locally hosted LLM access (tested with Qwen3-32B and Gemini-3-Flash); no specialized hardware beyond what runs the extraction and generation models. - Efficient: CluE costs ~5.5h wall time and 221 optimization LLM calls over 5 rounds, marginally more than MemEvolve (~5.0h, 30 calls) but substantially less than ACE (~12.4h, 610 calls); the overhead is one-time prompt optimization, not per-inference cost. - Effective: Evidence shows CluE improves downstream task performance across all three task categories on both in-distribution and OOD test sets, with the advantage holding in a continual memory setting on two tasks; the proxy (single-step extraction evaluated by downstream reward) is well-motivated but not identical to real multi-session deployment. - Scalable: Training uses only 330 examples; scaling to larger training sets or to the full memory lifecycle (storage, retrieval, management) is explicitly left to future work and not tested. The work delivers code, data, and evolved prompts as software artifacts. Strengths: - The cluster-by-extraction-scenario insight is intellectually crisp and solves a concrete failure mode (conflicting gradient-like signals under heterogeneous data) that the authors demonstrate empirically in competing methods. - BEHEMOTH's unified evaluation protocol for 18 heterogeneous datasets under a single downstream-utility metric is a genuine infrastructure contribution that enables fair comparison across methods with different extraction philosophies. - The stronger-seed experiments (Table 4) are a particularly stringent test: CluE is the only method that improves over a well-engineered Survey prompt, while all others degrade it — a result that distinguishes CluE's stability from methods that simply overwrite good rules. - The evolved prompt analysis (Appendix B.3) traces exactly how clusters merge/split over rounds and how cluster insights map to taxonomy sections, providing mechanistic transparency uncommon in prompt optimization papers. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors note that BEHEMOTH's 18 datasets may not cover the full complexity of real-world deployments, which can involve longer interaction histories and greater task diversity. They explicitly scope the work to memory extraction (the first stage of the memory lifecycle) and acknowledge that generalizing to storage, retrieval, and management remains open. They also note that continual evolution directly under the continual heterogeneous regime is left to future work. These are stated directly in Section 7, which is a credibility positive. Analyst-Identified Gaps: - No component ablation: the paper does not isolate which of the four CluE stages (Summarizer, Cluster Manager, Cluster Analyzer, Proposer) drives gains. A version without the Summarizer (clustering raw logs directly) or without per-cluster analysis would clarify the contribution. - Synthetic source conversations: problem-solving and agentic source conversations are generated by the same Qwen3-32B model used as LLM_g. This creates a distributional shortcut where the extraction model and the generation model share stylistic priors. No experiment tests CluE on truly human-generated problem-solving or agentic conversations. - Only 20 training examples per dataset: while the shuffled regime is realistic, all methods are trained on a tiny signal. It is not established whether the relative ordering of methods holds at larger training scales. - CluE with Gemini-3-Flash shows −5.93% agentic regression (Table 7), which the paper attributes to ceiling effects but does not analyze in depth. This is a meaningful failure mode for a method claiming robust cross-category generalization. - The cross-model generalization test changes only the extraction backend, not the optimization model; a test where evolution is performed with one LLM and the evolved prompt is deployed with a different backbone would be more informative for real adoption. Bottom Line: Practitioners building general-purpose persistent LLM assistants will find both the BEHEMOTH benchmark and the cluster-by-scenario evolution strategy directly useful, and the consistent cross-category gains distinguish this work meaningfully from prior self-evolving methods that trade off categories against each other.

SweetSpot: An Analytical Model for Predicting Energy Efficiency of LLM Inference

icpe2026.spec.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces SweetSpot, an analytical model derived from Transformer FLOPs and memory-access complexity that predicts per-token energy efficiency of LLM inference as a closed-form function of input and output sequence lengths, and analytically locates the efficiency-maximizing (n_in, n_out) configuration. - **Why it's important:** LLM inference accounts for up to 90% of model lifecycle energy, yet commercial providers charge linearly per token — a pricing and deployment model this work shows is physically incorrect, with best-vs-worst configurations differing by 33.41× in energy efficiency. - **How it does it:** It derives per-token energy E_tok(n_in, n_out) = θ0 + θ1·n²_in/n_out + θ2·n_in + θ3·n_in/n_out + θ4·n_out + θ5/n_out by separately accounting for quadratic-input prefill FLOPs, linear-in-context decode FLOPs, and KV-cache memory traffic, then fits six scalar parameters per model to measured GPU power traces. - **The good:** Across all 13 models, SweetSpot achieves a mean MAPE of 1.79% (±0.61) versus 31.40% for the next-best baseline, with all five core parameters (θ0–θ4) statistically significant at p < 0.001 in 100% of models. - **The missing:** The model fixes batch size at 1024 and is only fitted and evaluated on the same grid of (n_in, n_out) pairs used to derive the parameters — there is no held-out generalization test, so true prediction accuracy on unseen configurations is undemonstrated. - **Read it or not:** Worth reading for practitioners deploying LLM inference infrastructure who need an analytical handle on energy-optimal prompt and generation length targeting; less essential for researchers focused on training-time efficiency or hardware design.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces SweetSpot, an analytical model derived from Transformer FLOPs and memory-access complexity that predicts per-token energy efficiency of LLM inference as a closed-form function of input and output sequence lengths, and analytically locates the efficiency-maximizing (n_in, n_out) configuration. - Why it's important: LLM inference accounts for up to 90% of model lifecycle energy, yet commercial providers charge linearly per token — a pricing and deployment model this work shows is physically incorrect, with best-vs-worst configurations differing by 33.41× in energy efficiency. - How it does it: It derives per-token energy E_tok(n_in, n_out) = θ0 + θ1·n²_in/n_out + θ2·n_in + θ3·n_in/n_out + θ4·n_out + θ5/n_out by separately accounting for quadratic-input prefill FLOPs, linear-in-context decode FLOPs, and KV-cache memory traffic, then fits six scalar parameters per model to measured GPU power traces. - The good: Across all 13 models, SweetSpot achieves a mean MAPE of 1.79% (±0.61) versus 31.40% for the next-best baseline, with all five core parameters (θ0–θ4) statistically significant at p < 0.001 in 100% of models. - The missing: The model fixes batch size at 1024 and is only fitted and evaluated on the same grid of (n_in, n_out) pairs used to derive the parameters — there is no held-out generalization test, so true prediction accuracy on unseen configurations is undemonstrated. - Read it or not: Worth reading for practitioners deploying LLM inference infrastructure who need an analytical handle on energy-optimal prompt and generation length targeting; less essential for researchers focused on training-time efficiency or hardware design. Problem & Motivation: LLM inference dominates datacenter AI workloads and is estimated to constitute 90% of model lifecycle energy consumption, yet its energy cost is modeled and priced as a simple linear function of input plus output tokens. This linear assumption conflicts with the known computational structure of Transformers: the prefill phase incurs quadratic cost in input length due to attention, while the decode phase incurs linear per-step cost that grows with context length. No prior analytical model correctly characterizes the resulting non-monotonic efficiency landscape. Production systems are therefore unable to reason about energy trade-offs when deciding on prompt length, output budget, or batching strategy. Key Idea: Per-token energy has a minimum with respect to output length for any fixed input length: the quadratic prefill cost is amortized over more tokens as n_out grows (reducing per-token cost), but the linear decode cost eventually dominates (increasing per-token cost). Setting the derivative of E_tok with respect to n_out to zero yields a closed-form optimal output length n*_out = sqrt((θ1·n²_in + θ3·n_in + θ5) / θ4), which defines the efficiency sweet spot as a function of input length and six fitted model-specific scalars. Methodology: The model is built in two stages. First, a FLOPs-only version derives E_tok by computing per-layer prefill FLOPs (dominated by quadratic attention: 4n²_in·d per layer) and decode FLOPs (linear in context length per generated token), summing over L layers, and normalizing by n_out to get a five-term polynomial in n_in and n_out. Second, the full SweetSpot model adds a memory-access analysis of the KV-cache traffic during prefill (reading/writing n²_in·n_q elements per attention layer) and decode (reading (n_in + t − 1) cached KV entries per step), introducing a sixth term θ5/n_out from constant prefill memory overhead. Parameters θ0–θ5 are fitted per model using nonlinear least squares against measured GPU energy traces. Four baselines are compared: constant E_tok (Baseline 1), E_tok ∝ 1/n_out (Baseline 2), E_tok ∝ 1/(n_in + n_out) (Baseline 3), and the Wilkins et al. linear total-energy model normalized by n_out (Baseline 4). Critical design decisions: (1) per-output-token normalization to expose the amortization structure — well-justified theoretically; (2) memory traffic decomposition alongside FLOPs — justified by the 18.6% average MAPE reduction it delivers; (3) single-GPU, fixed-batch-size evaluation to isolate workload geometry effects — reasonable but limits scope. Key Results: SweetSpot achieves a mean MAPE of 1.79% (±0.61) across 13 LLMs ranging from 1B to 9B parameters, compared to 130.45%/130.49% for the output-only baselines, 106.25% for the (n_in + n_out) baseline, and 31.40% for the Wilkins et al. linear model. All five primary parameters (θ0–θ4) are significant at p < 0.001 in all 13 models; θ5 (memory constant term) is significant at p < 0.001 in 8/13 models. The aggregated heatmap across 13 models shows peak efficiency universally at n_in = 64, n_out ∈ [128, 256], with worst-case at n_in = 4096, n_out = 64, a 33.41× (±4.90) gap. GQA models are substantially more efficient than comparable MHA models: OPT 1.3B (MHA) is 42% less efficient than Qwen2 1.5B (GQA 6×) despite fewer parameters. Llama 3.2 1B reaches 120.71 tokens/joule at its peak; Falcon-RW 7B reaches only 18.78 tokens/joule at its peak. Predicted vs. measured peak configurations (Table 5) match well in n_in (universally 64) but show notable error in n_out prediction for some models (e.g., Gemma 2 9B: predicted 226, measured 64). Novelty Classification: NEW METHOD + NEW EMPIRICAL FINDING. The novel contribution is the derivation of a physically-grounded analytical form for per-token energy efficiency that correctly captures the non-linear prefill–decode interplay and yields a closed-form optimality condition; prior work either used purely linear total-energy models or coarse empirical metrics. The empirical finding that efficiency peaks cluster at short inputs (n_in = 64) and medium outputs (128–256 tokens) across diverse architectures — with a 33× best-vs-worst gap — is the key new result. The fitting methodology (nonlinear least squares applied to an analytically-motivated function form) is standard. Rigor & Reproducibility: The dataset is publicly released on Zenodo (doi.org/10.5281/zenodo.18714476). Fitted θ parameters for all 13 models are provided in Table 5, enabling reproduction of peak-configuration predictions. Parameter significance is reported via p-values across all 13 models (Table 4). However, there is no train/test split: all (n_in, n_out) grid points are used both for fitting and evaluation, so the reported MAPE measures in-sample fit rather than predictive generalization to unseen sequence lengths. No error bars are reported on the MAPE values across experimental repetitions (only across models). The experiment fixes max_batch_size = 1024 and N_req = 1000 — these choices are justified empirically (Figure 1, Figure 5) but their interaction with the analytical model is not incorporated into the model structure. Deployability (4-property check): Profile: EMPIRICAL (measurements on real hardware plus an analytical model fit to those measurements). - Practical: Runs on a single NVIDIA H100 GPU using TensorRT-LLM v0.21.0; the measurement and fitting pipeline requires only pynvml power sampling and standard nonlinear least squares — commercially accessible. - Efficient: Model fitting is negligible overhead (six-parameter regression per LLM); online use requires only evaluating a closed-form expression, so deployment cost is essentially zero. - Effective: SweetSpot achieves 1.79% MAPE in-sample and correctly identifies peak efficiency configurations to within ≤100 tokens of measured optima for most models, though Gemma 2 9B is a notable exception (predicted 226 vs. measured 64 tokens); evidence supports the main empirical claim with that caveat. - Scalable: Only tested on 1B–9B parameter models on a single H100; the paper explicitly states that larger models may exhibit different memory footprint behavior and are deferred to future work — no extrapolation evidence is provided. The paper delivers no software artifact; it provides a model formulation, fitted parameter tables, and a public synthetic dataset. Strengths: - The analytical derivation is physically principled: starting from first-principles FLOPs and memory-access accounting of the Transformer architecture and arriving at a model form that matches data at 1.79% MAPE is a genuine contribution, not a black-box regression. - The closed-form optimality condition (Eq. 6) is actionable: it gives production systems a tractable formula for computing energy-optimal output length given an input length and six model-specific constants. - Coverage across 13 LLMs spanning 4 attention mechanism variants (MHA, GQA 2×/4×/6×/7×) and 3 parameter scales, all validated on the same hardware under controlled conditions, makes the efficiency landscape results credible and broadly applicable within the tested scope. - The identification that GQA grouping ratio (not just parameter count) is a primary determinant of energy efficiency is a concrete, actionable finding for architecture selection in energy-constrained deployments. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors directly acknowledge: (1) the model scope is limited to 1B–9B models, which may not generalize to larger LLMs; (2) experiments use a single hardware platform (H100), limiting hardware generalizability; (3) only TensorRT-LLM is evaluated, excluding vLLM, DeepSpeed, and others; (4) batch size is not incorporated into the model and is fixed at 1024, which Section 6.2 shows substantially affects efficiency values and peak locations. These are confronted directly and honestly, which is a credibility positive. Analyst-Identified Gaps: - The MAPE evaluation is in-sample: the same 8×8 grid of (n_in, n_out) pairs used to fit the six θ parameters is also used to compute MAPE. A proper evaluation would hold out a fraction of the grid (or interpolate to off-grid lengths) to test generalization — without this, the 1.79% figure measures fitting quality, not predictive accuracy. - Table 5 reveals that for Gemma 2 9B, the predicted peak n_out is 226 while the measured peak is 64 — a qualitative error (wrong quadrant of the efficiency surface). The paper does not discuss this failure case. - All experiments use synthetically generated datasets with zero-variance fixed token lengths. Real-world inference request distributions have high variance in both dimensions, and efficiency under such workloads may differ from the controlled grid. - The 33.41× efficiency claim compares the absolute best configuration (n_in=64, n_out≈256) to the absolute worst (n_in=4096, n_out=64). Real workloads rarely span this entire range, and the practical gain from alignment strategies targeting the sweet spot rather than the actual workload distribution is not quantified. - The model is validated only at the 64–4096 token range in powers of two; behavior at intermediate lengths (e.g., 300 or 800 tokens) is not directly verified. Bottom Line: This is a clean, well-scoped piece of work that provides the first physically-grounded analytical model for LLM inference energy efficiency with strong in-sample fit (1.79% MAPE) and a practically deployable closed-form optimality criterion — worth reading for anyone building energy-aware LLM serving systems, with the caveat that the evaluation conflates fitting and generalization performance.

An Agentic Approach to Metadata Reasoning

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces the Metadata Reasoner, an LLM-orchestrated agentic system that selects a sufficient and minimal set of tables from an enterprise data lake to answer a given analytical task. - **Why it's important:** Ranking-based retrieval (vector search, BM25, Pneuma) scores tables individually and cannot reason about multi-table combinations, join validity, data quality, or lineage — causing it to fail when tasks require integrating several sources from noisy, large-scale data lakes. - **How it does it:** The system stages metadata across three tiers — discrimination-oriented vector embeddings for initial candidate retrieval, LLM-generated statistical summaries attached at retrieval time, and on-demand tool calls (data_finder, joinability_check, column_profiler) — so the LLM agent consumes only the metadata fragments needed at each reasoning step. - **The good:** On KramaBench, the system achieves 83.16% average F1 vs. 50.77% for vector search and 45.12% for Pneuma, with the gap widest in the 1,498-table Astronomy domain (72.31% vs. 32.80%); downstream Text-to-SQL accuracy improves from 56.38% to 71.28%. - **The missing:** Inference cost and latency of the multi-step agentic loop are never reported, making it impossible to assess the efficiency trade-off against single-pass retrieval baselines. - **Read it or not:** Database engineers and agentic-systems researchers building data discovery pipelines should read it; the three-tier metadata staging design and the failure-case taxonomy are both concrete and actionable.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces the Metadata Reasoner, an LLM-orchestrated agentic system that selects a sufficient and minimal set of tables from an enterprise data lake to answer a given analytical task. - Why it's important: Ranking-based retrieval (vector search, BM25, Pneuma) scores tables individually and cannot reason about multi-table combinations, join validity, data quality, or lineage — causing it to fail when tasks require integrating several sources from noisy, large-scale data lakes. - How it does it: The system stages metadata across three tiers — discrimination-oriented vector embeddings for initial candidate retrieval, LLM-generated statistical summaries attached at retrieval time, and on-demand tool calls (data_finder, joinability_check, column_profiler) — so the LLM agent consumes only the metadata fragments needed at each reasoning step. - The good: On KramaBench, the system achieves 83.16% average F1 vs. 50.77% for vector search and 45.12% for Pneuma, with the gap widest in the 1,498-table Astronomy domain (72.31% vs. 32.80%); downstream Text-to-SQL accuracy improves from 56.38% to 71.28%. - The missing: Inference cost and latency of the multi-step agentic loop are never reported, making it impossible to assess the efficiency trade-off against single-pass retrieval baselines. - Read it or not: Database engineers and agentic-systems researchers building data discovery pipelines should read it; the three-tier metadata staging design and the failure-case taxonomy are both concrete and actionable. Problem & Motivation: Enterprise data lakes contain thousands of heterogeneous tables. When an LLM agent must answer an analytical question (SQL query, ML prediction task), it must first identify the right subset of tables. The problem is harder than single-document retrieval: the agent must find combinations of tables that jointly cover all required entities and attributes, verify that tables can be joined correctly, reason about data quality and lineage, and do so without overwhelming the context window. Existing approaches — keyword-based retrieval, vector search, or hybrid systems like Pneuma — rank individual tables by topic relevance and cannot capture whether a retrieved set is jointly sufficient, joinable, or free of quality defects. Expanding the context window to include all metadata is computationally prohibitive and degrades reasoning quality due to attention dilution in long contexts. Key Idea: The central insight is that different types of metadata are optimal at different stages of reasoning: coarse vector retrieval needs discrimination-oriented embeddings that highlight inter-table differences rather than shared high-level descriptions; per-table reasoning needs attached statistical summaries; and join/value verification needs on-demand tool calls. By routing each metadata type to the stage where it is most informative, the agent avoids both context saturation and the latency of precomputing all pairwise metadata signals (e.g., all join paths). Methodology: The pipeline has four stages. (1) Query decomposition: a Gemini-3-Flash LLM agent decomposes the analytical task into named entities, measures, temporal scope, and granularity constraints, producing a structured search plan. (2) Discrimination-oriented semantic search: metadata embeddings are constructed via a two-stage process — tables are grouped by semantic relatedness, and an LLM generates group-relative descriptions that emphasize distinguishing features (e.g., specific temporal ranges for geomagnetic forecast tables with identical schemas). A state-aware deduplication mechanism suppresses repeated metadata from tables already surfaced, replacing them with recurrence indicators. (3) Reasoning over attached metadata: each retrieved table is accompanied by schema information plus LLM-generated statistical summaries (min/max, top-K values, null ratios, cardinality). (4) On-the-fly tool calls: three specialized tools are invoked when attached metadata is insufficient — column_profiler() for fine-grained column histograms, data_finder() for deterministic value-level presence verification, and joinability_check() for foreign-key overlap between specific candidate pairs. The agent iterates between search and tool calls until it declares a sufficient and minimal set. Critical design decisions: the partition between attached vs. on-the-fly metadata is explicitly tunable (and validated by ablation); the search engine itself uses the same high-performance vector engine (text-embedding-005 with semantic reranking) as the baselines, so improvements are attributable to the agentic reasoning layer, not embedding quality alone. Key Results: On KramaBench (6 real-world domains, up to 1,498 tables), the full system achieves 83.16% average F1 (84.44% recall, 86.63% precision) using an average of 10.10 reasoning steps per task, versus 50.77% for vector search and 45.12% for Pneuma. The gain is largest in the high-cardinality Astronomy domain: 72.31% vs. 32.80% (vector) and 27.70% (Pneuma). On a synthetically scaled BIRD data lake with injected partitions, duplicates, and quality degradation (up to 208 tables per database), the system achieves 85.5% average F1 vs. 30.0% for Top-10 vector retrieval; 99.0% of selected tables are noise-free vs. 64.4% for the baseline. In a downstream Text-to-SQL experiment, MR-selected tables yield 71.28% SQL execution accuracy vs. 56.38% for Top-10 vector results across five BIRD databases, with individual gains ranging from +8.62 to +21.47 points. Ablation confirms that attached metadata provides the largest single improvement (73.43% → 79.66% F1) while simultaneously reducing steps from 13.51 to 8.36; adding on-the-fly tools without attached metadata causes steps to nearly triple (24.14) due to trial-and-error search. Novelty Classification: NEW SYSTEM/TOOL + NEW EMPIRICAL FINDING. The orchestration of three staged metadata tiers within an agentic loop for data-source selection is the primary contribution; discrimination-oriented embedding construction (group-aware meta-prompting to generate contrastive table descriptions) is a secondary technical novelty. Individual components — LLM tool use, vector search, statistical table profiling — are known; the contribution lies in their principled integration and the empirical demonstration that staged metadata access is both more accurate and more efficient than providing all metadata upfront or on-demand alone. Rigor & Reproducibility: Ablations cover four system variants and are run over five seeds with standard deviations reported. Baselines include a strong vector-search engine (text-embedding-005 with semantic reranker) and Pneuma (a recent LLM-hybrid system), both evaluated at their best retrieval threshold K — this is a fair comparison protocol. KramaBench is an external benchmark not created by the authors, providing independent evaluation. The synthetic BIRD dataset is author-created, which introduces Goodhart's Law risk: the noise types injected (lineage suffixes, quality flags) are precisely those the system is designed to reason about, so strong performance on this benchmark may partly reflect design alignment rather than general robustness. No code, model weights, or data are stated as publicly released. The backbone model (Gemini-3-Flash) is a proprietary Google model referenced via an arXiv preprint, making exact reproduction impossible. Inference cost and latency are not reported, which is a significant omission for an agentic system that makes multiple LLM calls per task. Deployability (4-property check): EMPIRICAL/SYSTEMS profile. - Practical: Requires cloud API access to a large LLM (Gemini-3-Flash) and embedding model (text-embedding-005); not deployable on commodity on-premise hardware without equivalent API infrastructure. - Efficient: Average 10.10 LLM calls per task for the full system; the paper does not report wall-clock time or token cost, making it impossible to quantify the efficiency overhead relative to single-pass retrieval baselines. - Effective: The evidence is strong on both benchmarks — 32-point F1 gain over the best baseline on KramaBench, 99% noise-free table selection on synthetic BIRD, and a 14.9-point average gain in downstream SQL execution accuracy. - Scalable: The largest tested environment is 1,498 tables (Astronomy domain); enterprise data warehouses commonly contain tens of thousands of tables. Scalability beyond ~1,500 tables is claimed but not tested. The system exists as a working implementation using the Google ADK framework but is not released as a public artifact. Strengths: - The three-tier metadata staging design is principled and directly motivated by the context saturation problem; the ablation provides clean evidence that each tier contributes independently, with attached metadata providing the best accuracy-efficiency trade-off. - Discrimination-oriented embedding construction (group-aware meta-prompting to generate contrastive descriptions) is a concrete, reusable technique that yields +15 point Recall@5 gains in the Astronomy domain vs. schema-only embeddings at no inference-time cost. - Using KramaBench as the primary benchmark (external, multi-domain, real-world data lake with up to 1,498 tables) provides credible independent evidence; the 32-point margin over Pneuma is large and consistent across domains. - The failure-case taxonomy in Section 5.6 (5 failure modes: missing relation dependencies, granularity mismatch, multi-stage planning failure, redundant relation selection, incomplete partition retrieval) is analytically rigorous and provides a clear agenda for follow-on work. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors explicitly analyze 126 failure cases from KramaBench and attribute them to five concrete failure modes (Section 5.6). They acknowledge limitations in handling multi-stage dependent query planning, granularity inference, exhaustive partition retrieval, and redundant relation filtering. Future work directions include unstructured data support, web search integration, and metadata quality improvement. The authors do not acknowledge the cost/latency issue, the synthetic benchmark circularity risk, or the replication barrier posed by the proprietary backbone. Analyst-Identified Gaps: - Inference cost not quantified: the agentic loop averages 10.10 LLM calls per query; comparing to baselines that make one call without reporting token budget or latency makes the efficiency trade-off opaque and is the most important missing analysis for practitioners. - Synthetic BIRD benchmark circularity: the authors designed the noise injection scheme and the system's quality-reasoning tools simultaneously; strong performance on this benchmark is weaker evidence than performance on an independently constructed noisy benchmark. - Proprietary backbone: all experiments use Gemini-3-Flash (and Gemini-3-Pro for SQL generation), which is unreleased; no ablation with open-weights models (e.g., Llama, Mistral) is provided, making it unknown whether the approach generalizes beyond Google's models. - No human upper-bound on KramaBench: 83.16% F1 is uncontextualized — it is unclear whether the remaining 17% error reflects inherent task ambiguity or system fixable failures. - Scale gap: the 1,498-table Astronomy domain is the largest tested; there is no evidence the approach holds at 10,000–100,000 table scale typical of large enterprise data warehouses, where the planning and deduplication logic may not scale. - Downstream SQL experiment confounds table selection with justification quality: the MR configuration also passes the natural-language justification to the SQL generator, which is not available in the vector search condition, potentially inflating the downstream accuracy improvement attributable to table selection alone. Bottom Line: This is a solid systems paper with a principled design and convincing empirical results on real-world benchmarks; database engineers building LLM-powered data discovery pipelines will find both the three-tier metadata staging architecture and the discrimination-oriented embedding construction directly applicable, but the absence of cost analysis and the reliance on unreleased proprietary models leave the most important deployment questions unanswered.

Thermal-GEMs: Generalized Models for Building Thermal Dynamics

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces the first comprehensive empirical comparison of multi-source general models (MSGMs) versus time series foundation models (TSFMs) for building indoor temperature forecasting, validated on real-world data across three datasets and four MSGM architectures, and identifies a threshold of 16 same-sensor source buildings for MSGMs to consistently outperform TSFMs. - **Why it's important:** Buildings require months-to-years of sensor data to fit accurate thermal models, yet each building is unique — an unsolved cold-start problem that blocks deployment of MPC and fault-detection systems in real estates. - **How it does it:** MSGMs are trained on shuffled windows from up to 200 heterogeneous source buildings and applied zero-shot (or with minimal fine-tuning) to unseen target buildings, with a seasonal four-split evaluation scheme that averages performance across spring, summer, autumn, and winter. - **The good:** MSGMs reduce average MAE by 33–63% over single-source TL across all 40 real-world Ecobee target buildings, with larger gains in lower-data regimes, and the threshold experiment (1–128 source buildings) provides a directly actionable deployment decision rule. - **The missing:** TSFMs are tested exclusively in zero-shot mode — no LoRA or curriculum fine-tuning is evaluated, which prior work has shown changes the ranking, leaving the MSGM vs. fine-tuned TSFM comparison unaddressed. - **Read it or not:** Practitioners deploying building energy management systems and researchers working on data-efficient time series forecasting for the built environment should read this; it provides concrete, actionable guidance on when to choose an MSGM over a TSFM based on available source buildings.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces the first comprehensive empirical comparison of multi-source general models (MSGMs) versus time series foundation models (TSFMs) for building indoor temperature forecasting, validated on real-world data across three datasets and four MSGM architectures, and identifies a threshold of 16 same-sensor source buildings for MSGMs to consistently outperform TSFMs. - Why it's important: Buildings require months-to-years of sensor data to fit accurate thermal models, yet each building is unique — an unsolved cold-start problem that blocks deployment of MPC and fault-detection systems in real estates. - How it does it: MSGMs are trained on shuffled windows from up to 200 heterogeneous source buildings and applied zero-shot (or with minimal fine-tuning) to unseen target buildings, with a seasonal four-split evaluation scheme that averages performance across spring, summer, autumn, and winter. - The good: MSGMs reduce average MAE by 33–63% over single-source TL across all 40 real-world Ecobee target buildings, with larger gains in lower-data regimes, and the threshold experiment (1–128 source buildings) provides a directly actionable deployment decision rule. - The missing: TSFMs are tested exclusively in zero-shot mode — no LoRA or curriculum fine-tuning is evaluated, which prior work has shown changes the ranking, leaving the MSGM vs. fine-tuned TSFM comparison unaddressed. - Read it or not: Practitioners deploying building energy management systems and researchers working on data-efficient time series forecasting for the built environment should read this; it provides concrete, actionable guidance on when to choose an MSGM over a TSFM based on available source buildings. Problem & Motivation: Building operators who wish to deploy model-predictive control or fault-detection systems face a universal cold-start problem: each building has its own thermal signature driven by construction year, envelope parameters, occupancy, climate zone, and HVAC configuration, yet accurate ML-based thermal models require months to years of logged sensor data that does not exist at installation time. Transfer learning from a single source building mitigates this partially but inherits sensitivity to source-target mismatch. Multi-source general models (MSGMs) trained across many buildings promise better generalization, but prior work (Raisch et al. 2025) demonstrated MSGMs only on synthetic data. Independently, time series foundation models pre-trained on large heterogeneous corpora have been proposed as an alternative for sparse-data settings, but no head-to-head comparison between MSGMs and TSFMs for building thermal dynamics existed, and the practically critical question — how many same-sensor source buildings does a deployer need before MSGMs beat TSFMs — was unanswered. Key Idea: The core insight is that a model pre-trained on shuffled sliding windows from hundreds of heterogeneous buildings learns a general-purpose thermal dynamics representation that transfers zero-shot to unseen buildings better than both single-building pre-training and domain-agnostic TSFMs, because the MSGM is exclusively exposed to building physics while TSFMs are diluted across unrelated domains. The secondary insight is that this advantage has a data threshold: below ~16 same-sensor buildings, the MSGM's domain specificity cannot overcome the TSFM's breadth. Methodology: The pipeline compares three strategies — single-source TL, MSGM, and zero-shot TSFM — on 1-hour-ahead indoor temperature forecasting (4 × 15-minute steps, 96-step lookback). Three datasets are used: D1 EcoBee (real-world, 932 North American homes, 5 climate zones), D2 Ideal Households (real-world, 32 UK homes, 255 rooms), D3 European Households (non-public simulated, 450 Central European homes built 1949–present). Each dataset is split into source (88–96%) and target (4–11%) buildings; the target set is stratified by location. Data is downsampled to 15-minute intervals. Four MSGM architectures are evaluated: LSTM (baseline from prior work), standard Transformer with cosine positional embedding, Mamba (structured state-space model), and xLSTM. Hyperparameters are tuned per architecture per dataset using Bayesian optimization with Optuna; each configuration is trained 4 times and the best validation-RMSE model is selected. Source training uses up to 200 time series (180 train / 20 val), AdamW optimizer. Three TSFMs are evaluated zero-shot: Chronos-2 (encoder Transformer, synthetic pretraining), TimesFM 2.5 (decoder-only autoregressive, univariate mode), Toto (decoder-only, 151M parameters, 2T training points). Evaluation follows a four-way seasonal fine-tuning split: each year is partitioned into spring/summer/autumn/winter segments, each with a variable training set (0.5–2.5 months), 0.5-month validation, and 3-month test. Final MAE and RMSE are averaged across four seasons per target building. Three critical design decisions: (1) weight initialization (not layer freezing) for fine-tuning — justified by prior ablation in [38]; (2) zero-shot deployment mode for MSGMs on real-world data, motivated by the finding that fine-tuning increases error; (3) reporting best-of-4 runs for MSGM with ≤16 sources in the sparsity experiment — favorably biases MSGM in low-source regime. Key Results: Architecture ablation (zero-shot, all-in-one scenario): LSTM and Transformer outperform Mamba and xLSTM; Mamba is the clear outlier. Architecture choice among LSTM/Transformer/xLSTM has minor effect. Best architecture is dataset-dependent: Transformer for European Households, LSTM for EcoBee and Ideal. Average 1-hour MAE is ~0.1 K on Ideal and Euro datasets, ~0.25 K on EcoBee. MSGM vs. single-source TL (EcoBee, real-world): MSGM outperforms single-source TL on all 40 target buildings across all four data-sparsity scenarios. Zero-shot: MSGM MAE 0.254 K vs. single-source 0.683 K (63% reduction); RMSE 0.382 vs. 1.089 K (65% reduction). With 2.5 months of fine-tuning data, single-source TL MAE drops to 0.475 K, still well above the zero-shot MSGM at 0.269 K. Surprisingly, fine-tuning the MSGM on real-world data consistently increases error regardless of fine-tuning data volume; the authors hypothesize seasonal overfitting but do not verify this. MSGM vs. TSFMs (all three datasets, zero-shot): MSGM outperforms all three TSFMs on average across all datasets. TSFMs exceed the MSGM only on 12.5% of EcoBee buildings and 8% of Euro buildings by MAE; they lose on all Ideal buildings. Chronos wins best average rank across TSFMs and datasets. Unexpectedly, univariate TimesFM outperforms multivariate Chronos and Toto on the Ideal dataset — the authors attribute this to multivariate TSFMs failing to capture building thermal inertia from heating power signals. Same-sensor building sparsity (1–128 sources, 2× power steps): MSGM performance improves monotonically with source count, with diminishing returns above 64 sources. MSGM crosses the TSFM baseline at 16 sources (EcoBee, Euro) and 32 sources (Ideal) for MAE; at only 2–8 sources for RMSE. The Ideal dataset requires more sources because rooms from the same building introduce within-building homogeneity that limits diversity benefit. Novelty Classification: NEW EMPIRICAL FINDING + NEW BENCHMARK EVALUATION. The genuine novelty is the first real-world validation of multi-source TL for building thermal dynamics and the first head-to-head MSGM vs. TSFM comparison on this task. The source-count threshold experiment is a novel empirical contribution with direct practical value. The architectures, TSFMs, and transfer learning protocol are all drawn from existing work; this paper's contribution is the empirical synthesis and the actionable threshold finding. Rigor & Reproducibility: The seasonal fine-tuning split with 4-way averaging is a meaningful robustness improvement over single-season evaluations in prior work. Models are trained 4 times from different random seeds; the best is selected rather than averaged, which inflates reported MSGM performance. No statistical significance tests or confidence intervals are reported for cross-building comparisons, making it impossible to judge whether the MSGM's wins on individual buildings are meaningful or noise. The EcoBee target set is only 40 buildings (4.3% of available data). For the source-count experiment with ≤16 sources, best-of-4-trials reporting selects the luckiest random sample, creating an optimistic bias for MSGMs in the critical low-data regime. Code is promised on GitHub [anonymous — anonymized for review]. The European Households dataset is non-public, limiting full reproducibility for that split. Both real-world datasets (EcoBee, Ideal) are publicly available. Deployability (4-property check): Profile: EMPIRICAL — multiple trained models evaluated across real and simulated building datasets. - Practical: Training runs on a single H100 GPU (94 GB VRAM); inference for MSGMs takes 0.0002–0.0004s per forecast, well within 15-minute control cycle requirements for real deployment. - Efficient: MSGMs are 100× faster at inference than TSFMs (0.03–0.05s per forecast for TSFMs vs. sub-millisecond for MSGMs); MSGM pretraining takes 1.2–20.4 hours depending on architecture and dataset, a one-time cost amortized over many target buildings. - Effective: Evidence on real-world buildings (EcoBee, Ideal) shows that zero-shot MSGMs substantially outperform single-source TL and TSFMs for 1-hour temperature forecasting; the surprising finding that fine-tuning hurts performance on real-world data is unexplained and limits confident deployment of the fine-tuning pathway. - Scalable: Only tested up to 128 source buildings and 1-hour forecast horizons; longer prediction horizons needed for MPC (typically 6–24 hours) are not evaluated and represent an untested assumption. The work delivers trained model weights (promised via GitHub) but no software library or CLI tool for end-user deployment. Strengths: - First real-world validation closes the critical gap left by prior work (Raisch et al. 2025) that relied on simulated data only, substantially strengthening the case for MSGM deployment. - The source-count threshold finding (16 buildings for MAE, 2–8 for RMSE) gives practitioners a concrete, actionable decision rule that was previously absent from the literature. - The four-season evaluation design is a genuine methodological improvement over prior work that ignores seasonal effects; averaging across all four seasons reduces cherry-picking risk. - The zero-shot MSGM fine-tuning degradation finding is a counter-intuitive and practically important result not previously reported in the building thermal dynamics literature. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors explicitly acknowledge: (1) TSFMs were tested in zero-shot only and naive fine-tuning increases errors, leaving advanced fine-tuning (LoRA, curriculum learning) as future work; (2) single-source TL experiments were capped at 3 months of fine-tuning data; (3) same-sensor building sparsity constrains MSGM applicability, and feature-invariant MSGMs that handle heterogeneous sensors are a future direction. These are genuine limitations confronted directly, which adds credibility. Analyst-Identified Gaps: - The explanation for fine-tuning degradation (seasonal overfitting) is a post-hoc hypothesis; no ablation disentangles seasonal overfitting from distribution shift or regularization mismatch, and this finding fundamentally affects the practical deployment recommendation. - Reporting the best of 4 random source samples for ≤16-source MSGM experiments introduces optimistic bias precisely where the result is most commercially sensitive; median-of-4 would be more defensible. - Forecast horizon is fixed at 1 hour (4 × 15-minute steps); practical MPC requires 6–24-hour horizons, and the relative performance of MSGMs vs. TSFMs likely changes at longer horizons where TSFMs' broader temporal representations may become more competitive. - The Ideal dataset's source-count anomaly (needing more sources due to within-building room homogeneity) exposes that the diversity measure for source buildings is building count rather than thermal diversity — a potentially misleading metric when rooms and buildings are mixed. - No significance testing is applied to per-building win rates (12.5% TSFM wins on EcoBee), making it unclear whether those wins are systematic or random. - Single-source TL uses LSTM only, not the best MSGM architecture per dataset; it is possible the architecture choice, not the multi-source pretraining, drives part of the observed gap. Bottom Line: Any practitioner deploying data-driven building thermal models who can assemble 16+ same-sensor buildings for pretraining will find specific, real-world-validated guidance here — but the unexplained fine-tuning degradation and the absence of longer-horizon and fine-tuned-TSFM evaluations mean the deployment recipe is still incomplete.

A Mechanistic Analysis of Looped Reasoning Language Models

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper proves theoretically and demonstrates empirically that looped (recurrent-depth) language models tend toward cyclic fixed-point behavior in latent space, and shows that each recurrence within the loop replicates the same "stages of inference" observed in feedforward Transformers. - **Why it's important:** Looped LLMs are a promising test-time scaling paradigm, but their internal mechanics have been poorly understood — clarity here enables principled architectural design (norm choice, input injection, sandwich layers) and explains why some models generalize to unseen recurrence counts while others degrade. - **How it does it:** The paper combines a formal Lipschitz contraction argument (showing attention patterns stabilize when inputs converge to a cyclic fixed point) with empirical measurement of ColSum concentration, sink rates, and mixing scores across six pretrained looped models (Ouro 1.4B, Huginn-0125, Retrofitted Llama/OLMo/TinyLlama) run up to 128 recurrences on 256 GSM8k examples. - **The good:** The finding that Ouro 1.4B — trained from scratch with recurrence — spontaneously develops feedforward-like stages of inference in each recurrence block, with no explicit training signal encouraging this, is the most striking result and strongly supports the claim that these stages are emergent from the Transformer architecture itself. - **The missing:** The paper cannot explain analytically *why* specific architectural choices (pre-norm + input injection) produce stable fixed points, nor why stages of inference emerge; it empirically identifies what correlates but leaves the causal mechanism for future work. - **Read it or not:** Researchers designing or analyzing looped/recurrent Transformer architectures should read this; it provides actionable guidance on norm and injection choices and a principled lens for understanding recurrent depth that is absent from prior work.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper proves theoretically and demonstrates empirically that looped (recurrent-depth) language models tend toward cyclic fixed-point behavior in latent space, and shows that each recurrence within the loop replicates the same "stages of inference" observed in feedforward Transformers. - Why it's important: Looped LLMs are a promising test-time scaling paradigm, but their internal mechanics have been poorly understood — clarity here enables principled architectural design (norm choice, input injection, sandwich layers) and explains why some models generalize to unseen recurrence counts while others degrade. - How it does it: The paper combines a formal Lipschitz contraction argument (showing attention patterns stabilize when inputs converge to a cyclic fixed point) with empirical measurement of ColSum concentration, sink rates, and mixing scores across six pretrained looped models (Ouro 1.4B, Huginn-0125, Retrofitted Llama/OLMo/TinyLlama) run up to 128 recurrences on 256 GSM8k examples. - The good: The finding that Ouro 1.4B — trained from scratch with recurrence — spontaneously develops feedforward-like stages of inference in each recurrence block, with no explicit training signal encouraging this, is the most striking result and strongly supports the claim that these stages are emergent from the Transformer architecture itself. - The missing: The paper cannot explain analytically why specific architectural choices (pre-norm + input injection) produce stable fixed points, nor why stages of inference emerge; it empirically identifies what correlates but leaves the causal mechanism for future work. - Read it or not: Researchers designing or analyzing looped/recurrent Transformer architectures should read this; it provides actionable guidance on norm and injection choices and a principled lens for understanding recurrent depth that is absent from prior work. Problem & Motivation: Looped Transformers — architectures that reapply a shared block of Transformer layers multiple times at inference — have emerged as a mechanism for scaling test-time compute, with models like Huginn, Ouro, and retrofitted LLaMA variants showing reasoning improvements with more recurrences. Despite growing empirical success, the internal dynamics of these models are poorly understood: it is unclear how the latent states evolve across recurrences, how this differs from feedforward computation, and why some looped models generalize gracefully to more test-time recurrences than they were trained on while others do not. The field lacked both a theoretical account of limiting behavior and an empirical characterization of whether looped models discover the same computational structure ("stages of inference") that feedforward models are known to exhibit. Key Idea: The core insight is that cyclic fixed-point convergence — where each individual layer within the recurrent block converges to its own distinct fixed point, rather than all layers collapsing to the same state — is both the dominant empirical behavior across trained looped models and a sufficient condition for attention patterns to stabilize across recurrences. This means: once the latent trajectory is cycling, the mixing behavior (what each attention head attends to) becomes constant recurrence-over-recurrence, and the recurrent block effectively replicates the same computational stages at each iteration, mirroring a feedforward model in miniature. Methodology: The paper proceeds in two main parts. The first (Section 4) establishes cyclic fixed-point behavior. Proposition 4.1 proves by induction that if an (l,k)-recurrent block S_k reaches a fixed point X' = S_k(X'), then each of the k constituent blocks B_1,...,B_k individually reaches a (possibly distinct) fixed point via a cyclic permutation argument. Proposition 4.2 proves, under a bounded-input assumption and using the 1/2-Lipschitz bound on softmax (Nair, 2025), that attention patterns S_ℓ(X_{ℓ,t}) converge at a rate ≤ L_sm · (2Bκ_ℓ/√d) · ‖X_{ℓ,t} − X_{ℓ,t-1}‖, so attention stabilizes as the residual stream converges. Empirical validation uses Frobenius norm between attention matrices across recurrences (Fig. 2), residual stream difference norms (Fig. 3), and cosine similarity to approximate fixed points computed at recurrence 128 (Fig. 4), on 256 random GSM8k test examples. The second part (Section 5) measures stages of inference using ColSum concentration C = 1 − H_col ∈ [0,1] (normalized entropy of column sums of attention matrices), plus sink rates and mixing scores, across realized depth in each recurrent block, comparing these profiles against feedforward baselines (Llama 3.2 1B, OLMo-2 1B, etc.). Section 5.1 investigates whether stages self-organize during training using small from-scratch looped models (configurations (2,4⊗4,2), (2,8⊗4,2), (2,12⊗4,2)) trained on a Nanochat-derived setup with dimension 512, constant 4-recurrence schedule, cross-entropy on the final output. Section 5.2 tests stability to out-of-distribution recurrence counts (up to 128) for models trained on ≤32. Key design decisions examined: norm position (pre-norm vs. Huginn/Ouro sandwich norms), input injection (present vs. absent), and presence of sandwich (prelude/coda) layers. Key Results: Across Huginn-0125 (3.5B) and Retrofitted Llama/OLMo/TinyLlama (0.8–1B), attention matrices of the same layer at different recurrences show strong diagonal structure in Frobenius norm heatmaps, confirming cyclic fixed-point behavior; these models reach approximate fixed points within the first 1–20 recurrences. Ouro 1.4B does not reach a strict fixed point but converges to small successive differences. Non-fixed-point behaviors (orbits, sliders) are rare: without a system prompt, only ~0.02% of tokens exhibit non-fixed-point behavior; with the Huginn "Long Persona" prompt, this rises to 0.14% of tokens (2.81% of examples). Input injection is necessary for stable fixed-point behavior across all norm types except pre-norm; without input injection, all non-pre-norm models fail to converge stably. Stages of inference (characteristic ColSum concentration profiles across block depth) observed in feedforward models are faithfully reproduced within each recurrence of Ouro 1.4B, Retrofitted Llama, and Retrofitted OLMo — with Ouro trained from scratch, ruling out inheritance from a feedforward base model. Huginn-0125 does not reproduce these stages, attributed to its norm structure (normalizing residual stream magnitude, preventing the massive-activation growth that drives compression behavior and hence sink formation). Small ablation (zeroing massive-activation MLP output in Retrofitted Llama) eliminates its stages of inference. Models with stable fixed points (Retrofitted Llama, Huginn) maintain consistent stages of inference at 128 recurrences; Ouro's stages drift significantly at out-of-distribution recurrence counts. Experiments are evaluated solely on GSM8k (256 examples) and HellaSwag (256 examples, Appendix E.4); no task-accuracy numbers are reported — this is purely an internal-dynamics study. Novelty Classification: NEW EMPIRICAL FINDING + NEW FORMULATION. The formal cyclic fixed-point framework (Propositions 4.1–4.2) is a new result that was not previously established for cyclic recurrent architectures. The application of stages-of-inference analysis to looped models, and the empirical demonstration that these stages emerge spontaneously from training, is a new empirical finding. The work is not a new method or system; it uses ColSum concentration and mixing metrics introduced by prior work (Queipo-de Llano et al., 2025) applied to a new setting. Rigor & Reproducibility: The theoretical propositions are fully proved in the appendix (Appendix A), with stated assumptions (bounded inputs, shared weight matrices, submultiplicative norms). Empirical results are averaged over 256 random GSM8k test examples (same subset throughout), and small training experiments use 3 random seeds (Fig. 6). No error bars or significance tests are reported for the main empirical claims in the paper body, though standard deviations are shown in some appendix figures. Code is referenced (adapted from github.com/TrelisResearch/nanochat/tree/recursive) but no dedicated repository for this paper's analysis code is stated. Models are all publicly available on HuggingFace (Table 2). A methodological concern: the non-fixed-point detection algorithm (Algorithm 1) has hyperparameters (τ=0.05, ρ=0.9) selected manually via visual inspection, which the authors themselves flag. The paper evaluates only on GSM8k and HellaSwag; no claim is made about task performance, only internal dynamics — this is appropriately scoped. Deployability (4-property check): EMPIRICAL profile (internal dynamics analysis of existing trained models, plus small training experiments). - Practical: All experiments run on existing pretrained models obtainable from HuggingFace; inference with 128 recurrences on models up to 3.5B is feasible on standard GPU hardware. - Efficient: The analysis itself is post-hoc observation (no training cost beyond small from-scratch experiments at dimension 512); the study does not propose any new inference procedure, so no efficiency comparison is applicable. - Effective: The study demonstrates its claims about internal dynamics convincingly across six models, two datasets, and up to 128 recurrences; however, it does not demonstrate that architectural choices improving fixed-point stability also improve task performance. - Scalable: All pretrained models studied are ≤3.5B parameters; whether the cyclic fixed-point / stages-of-inference picture holds for larger models (7B+, 70B+) is untested and not claimed. The work delivers no software artifact, trained model, or dataset beyond the analysis; it is a paper-form empirical and theoretical study. Strengths: - The formal proof that cyclic fixed-point convergence implies per-layer fixed points (Proposition 4.1) and that attention patterns stabilize under this convergence (Proposition 4.2) grounds the empirical observations in a principled theoretical framework — prior work had only observed this empirically. - Demonstrating that Ouro 1.4B, trained from scratch with recurrence and without any feedforward model as a base, spontaneously develops stages of inference that mirror Llama's feedforward profile is a striking and non-obvious result that substantially strengthens the claim of emergent structure. - The paper cleanly isolates the role of architectural choices (norm type × input injection) through controlled randomly-initialized experiments, separating the effect of pretrained weights from architectural inductive bias. - The connection between fixed-point stability and generalization to out-of-distribution recurrence counts is practically actionable: it explains empirically observed failure modes of Ouro at test-time recurrence extrapolation. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The analysis is restricted to cyclic recurrence (one shared block looped repeatedly) and does not extend to sequential recurrence with multiple distinct recurrent blocks, for which the authors refer readers to Pappone et al. (2025). The authors acknowledge they have not established analytically why specific architectural choices lead to stable fixed points, nor whether stable fixed-point behavior is desirable or restrictive for reasoning tasks. The non-fixed-point detection algorithm hyperparameters were tuned manually. The paper also acknowledges that stages of inference for Huginn-0125 are absent, and attributes this to norm structure, but does not fully resolve this — it is labeled a negative result. Analyst-Identified Gaps: The paper measures internal dynamics exclusively on a 256-example subset of GSM8k (reasoning) and HellaSwag (commonsense), and never reports whether the architectural choices that improve fixed-point stability (pre-norm + input injection) also improve task accuracy — this disconnect between mechanistic findings and downstream performance is the central unaddressed question. The small-scale training experiments (dimension 512, Nanochat-derived) may not reflect behavior at the scale of the pretrained models being analyzed; the authors note this caveat, but the gap from toy scale to 1–3.5B parameters is large. The claim that massive activations are required for stages of inference to form rests on a single ablation (zeroing one MLP output) on one model; a more systematic ablation across models would be needed to establish this. No comparison is made between looped models and deep feedforward models at matched parameter count, which would clarify whether the stages-of-inference correspondence is coincidental or structurally meaningful. Bottom Line: A mechanistically careful and theoretically grounded study that gives the first principled account of why looped Transformers behave as they do internally; practitioners designing recurrent-depth architectures will find the norm/injection guidance actionable, though the missing link to task performance means the work is explanatory rather than prescriptive.

ClawMark: A Living-World Benchmark for Multi-Turn, Multi-Day, Multimodal Coworker Agents

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
