**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces ClawMark, a benchmark of 100 multi-turn, multi-day, multimodal office-workflow tasks scored by 1,537 deterministic Python checkers over five stateful sandboxed services, designed to evaluate persistent coworker agents under exogenously mutating environments. - **Why it's important:** Existing agent benchmarks run within single static episodes and are largely text-centric, leaving the coworker regime — where agents must track independently evolving state across multiple workdays and parse raw multimodal evidence — essentially unmeasured by any prior benchmark. - **How it does it:** Each task simulates 2–6 in-universe workdays against live sandboxed services, with between-turn state mutations injected as both announced loud events and unannounced silent mutations, then scored by deterministic Python functions against post-turn service state rather than LLM-as-judge. - **The good:** The failure taxonomy (pooled 10,759 checker evaluations across 7 models) reveals that silent-change detection (56.5% per-evaluation fail rate) and backend writeback (53.6%) are the dominant failure modes — nearly 2× the benchmark-wide average — providing a precise, actionable diagnostic that LLM-as-judge evaluation would miss. - **The missing:** Only 100 tasks across 13 scenarios (one scenario has a single task), all run under a single agent harness (OpenClaw); run-to-run variance is quantified for only 2 of 7 models, leaving statistical confidence in close-cluster rankings unestablished for 5 models. - **Read it or not:** Essential reading for researchers building or evaluating persistent LLM agents — the benchmark uniquely identifies two specific, measurable failure modes (silent-change detection, backend writeback) that prior benchmarks structurally cannot surface.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces ClawMark, a benchmark of 100 multi-turn, multi-day, multimodal office-workflow tasks scored by 1,537 deterministic Python checkers over five stateful sandboxed services, designed to evaluate persistent coworker agents under exogenously mutating environments. - Why it's important: Existing agent benchmarks run within single static episodes and are largely text-centric, leaving the coworker regime — where agents must track independently evolving state across multiple workdays and parse raw multimodal evidence — essentially unmeasured by any prior benchmark. - How it does it: Each task simulates 2–6 in-universe workdays against live sandboxed services, with between-turn state mutations injected as both announced loud events and unannounced silent mutations, then scored by deterministic Python functions against post-turn service state rather than LLM-as-judge. - The good: The failure taxonomy (pooled 10,759 checker evaluations across 7 models) reveals that silent-change detection (56.5% per-evaluation fail rate) and backend writeback (53.6%) are the dominant failure modes — nearly 2× the benchmark-wide average — providing a precise, actionable diagnostic that LLM-as-judge evaluation would miss. - The missing: Only 100 tasks across 13 scenarios (one scenario has a single task), all run under a single agent harness (OpenClaw); run-to-run variance is quantified for only 2 of 7 models, leaving statistical confidence in close-cluster rankings unestablished for 5 models. - Read it or not: Essential reading for researchers building or evaluating persistent LLM agents — the benchmark uniquely identifies two specific, measurable failure modes (silent-change detection, backend writeback) that prior benchmarks structurally cannot surface. Problem & Motivation Existing LLM agent benchmarks — WebArena, OSWorld, tau-bench, TheAgentCompany — evaluate within a single static session where the environment does not change independently between steps. Real professional workflows span multiple workdays: emails arrive overnight, calendar entries shift, files are silently overwritten, and critical evidence is distributed across audio, video, scanned PDFs, and spreadsheets rather than clean pre-transcribed text. No prior benchmark jointly evaluates agents on (a) multi-day persistence, (b) exogenous between-turn environment mutation, and (c) raw multimodal evidence, while maintaining deterministic, reproducible scoring. This gap means current benchmarks cannot diagnose whether a model fails because it cannot parse a scanned PDF, fails to refresh stale state after a silent overnight mutation, or commits a compliance violation that prose-based judges overlook. Key Idea The core insight is that coworker evaluation requires treating the environment as a first-class participant that mutates independently of the agent between turns — not merely as a passive substrate that the agent writes to. Operationally: inject both announced loud events and unannounced silent mutations into five live sandboxed services between turns, then score by deterministic Python checkers querying post-turn service state, so an agent that reasons correctly but never commits the result to the right backend fails, and a compliance violation that does not appear in the prose trajectory but does appear in service state is caught deterministically. Methodology Each task is defined in a single `task.py` specifying per-turn wake-up prompts, service seed hooks, between-turn inject layers (loud events announced in prompts; silent mutations injected into services without notification), and a weighted checker rubric of 6–29 deterministic Python functions. Five sandboxed services run per task in an isolated docker-compose group: Docker-mounted filesystem, GreenMail SMTP/IMAP, Notion-compatible knowledge base, Google-Sheets-compatible spreadsheet, and Radicale CalDAV server. Tasks run 2–6 turns (mean 3.6), with 1,072 raw multimodal artifacts across the corpus. Scoring uses two metrics: weight-normalized task score (Eq. 1, continuous [0,1]) and strict Task Success requiring every checker to pass (Eq. 2, binary). 55 of 1,537 checkers are red-line constraints (premature-decision, compliance-bypass, data-exfiltration, irreversible-write) implemented as high-weight ordinary rubric entries — no separate evaluation path. The four-phase construction pipeline proceeds: task authoring (authors write task.py under three invariants: every silent mutation tied to at least one checker, every cross-modal contradiction spans two modalities, every red-line is a state check not prose match); evidence sourcing via web collection, original recording, or AI synthesis; a 3–5 round review loop combining human multimodal integrity inspection, checker-hacking audits, task-checker correspondence checks, and trajectory review by a Codex-class reviewer agent running against two reference model trajectories; and a release gate requiring bit-identical checker verdicts and diagnostic messages across two independent re-runs. Tasks failing the gate more than twice are redesigned or dropped. Seven frontier models (Claude Sonnet 4.6, Claude Opus 4.6, GPT-5.4 high, Kimi K2.6, Gemini 3.1 Pro Preview, Qwen 3.6 Plus, Kimi K2.5) ran under a single harness (OpenClaw) with provider-default inference and extended thinking enabled where supported. Three critical design decisions: (1) Bit-identical re-run gate operationally enforces the no-LLM-as-judge claim — well-justified as the main validity guarantee. (2) Dual metrics (weighted score + Task Success) jointly reported — justified because weighted score rewards partial progress while Task Success answers the deployment-relevant all-or-nothing question. (3) All seven models run under one harness to isolate model-side differences — appropriate for leaderboard comparison but limits external validity, as the paper explicitly acknowledges. Key Results Weighted scores (single-sweep): Claude Sonnet 4.6 at 75.8, Claude Opus 4.6 at 74.6, GPT-5.4 at 72.0, Kimi K2.6 at 68.4, Gemini 3.1 Pro at 68.2, Qwen 3.6 Plus at 57.2, Kimi K2.5 at 56.0. Under strict Task Success the ordering changes: Opus 4.6 leads at 20.0%, Sonnet at 14.0%, GPT-5.4 at 9.0%, Kimi K2.5 at 0.0%. The top three models cluster within 3.8 pp weighted score — the authors flag this band as tentative given single-sweep evaluation. Hardest scenario: Project Management, all models below 44.0, mean across 7 models ≈ 35.1. Per-scenario best distributed across four models, not dominated by one. Day-by-day trajectory on the 73 three-turn tasks: 6 of 7 models drop on Day 2 (first exogenous mutation). Largest Day-1→Day-2 declines: Opus 4.6 (80.6→69.0, −11.5 pp), Sonnet 4.6 (83.1→72.6, −10.5 pp), Kimi K2.6 (75.4→65.8, −9.6 pp). Qwen 3.6 Plus is the lone exception (+1.2 pp). Recovery by Day 3 is partial; 6 of 7 models remain below Day-1 baseline. Failure taxonomy (10,759 pooled evals, 3,404 failures, 31.6% overall): silent-change detection 56.5% fail rate, backend writeback 53.6% — both nearly 2× average. Backend writeback is the largest absolute bucket (567 failures, 16.7% of all failures). Red-line incidents are concentrated: Sonnet 4.6 and GPT-5.4 at 3.6%, Qwen 3.6 Plus at 14.5% (4× frontier top-3). pm_task2: all 7 models trip at least one red-line. Run-to-run stability (Appendix D, 3 sweeps for Kimi K2.6 and GPT-5.4): 2.8 pp range and 1.0 pp range respectively, small relative to the 19.8 pp cross-model spread. Novelty Classification NEW DATASET/BENCHMARK + NEW EMPIRICAL FINDING. The benchmark corpus and harness (multi-day tasks, exogenously mutating sandboxed services, deterministic scoring) constitute the primary contribution; the empirical finding — that silent-change detection and backend writeback fail at nearly 2× the benchmark-wide rate, and that compliance violations are surface-invisible but state-detectable — is a novel, actionable diagnostic. Individual components (sandboxed services, deterministic scoring, multi-turn evaluation) are established; novelty lies in their specific combination enforced by construction discipline (bit-identical re-run gate, checker-hacking audits) that makes the no-LLM-as-judge guarantee credible rather than aspirational. Rigor & Reproducibility Scoring determinism is operationally enforced, not just claimed: tasks are admitted only after bit-identical checker verdicts across two independent re-runs against frozen service state. Run-to-run variance is quantified for only 2 of 7 models (Kimi K2.6 and GPT-5.4, Appendix D); the remaining 5 have single-sweep results, so rankings within the top-3 cluster (3.8 pp spread) carry no stated statistical confidence. No error bars, significance tests, or confidence intervals are reported for model comparisons. Checker-hacking audits during construction actively guard against rubric items satisfiable by keyword emission rather than backend state change — methodological discipline rare in benchmark papers. Silent/loud annotation uses author-supplied comments in task.py and is acknowledged as "sensitive to borderline cases at the per-scenario level." The benchmark, harness, construction pipeline, and 700 execution traces are released publicly (github.com/evolvent-ai/ClawMark). This is a preprint (arXiv:2604.23781v1, April 2026), not peer-reviewed. A broken section cross-reference ("§??") in Table 2's caption indicates the manuscript was not fully finalized before posting. Deployability (4-property check) Profile: SYSTEMS — a built benchmark harness evaluated by deployment against seven frontier models. - Practical: The harness runs on standard x86 infrastructure via docker-compose with 4–8 concurrent compose stacks; no specialized hardware is required beyond sufficient RAM to run five concurrent sandboxed services per task. - Efficient: Full sweeps take 18.9–33.3 wall-clock hours across 100 tasks; the dominant cost is LLM API calls (214M–339M input tokens per model), not benchmark infrastructure — but this makes large-scale regression testing expensive for researchers without frontier-model API budgets. - Effective: The deterministic checkers evaluate actual service state, not proxy signals; the insurance_task1 case study demonstrates they catch compliance violations that pass prose-level evaluation, confirming the benchmark measures the intended property. - Scalable: The current corpus is 100 tasks; the construction pipeline is released to support growth, but the 3–5 round human review loop per task is a significant labor bottleneck, and no test of harness performance at 10× task count is reported. The work delivers a software artifact: benchmark tasks, deterministic evaluation harness, construction pipeline, and 700 execution traces. Strengths - The no-LLM-as-judge guarantee is operationally enforced by a bit-identical re-run release gate, closing the main validity gap in agent evaluation where scoring inconsistency routinely dominates noise. This is technically concrete, not a design aspiration. - The loud-event / silent-mutation distinction is a precise, testable formalization of a property real coworkers encounter (proactive state refresh vs. passive notification response), and the failure taxonomy validates it empirically: silent-change detection fails at 56.5%, nearly 2× the average, giving the distinction practical bite rather than leaving it as a conceptual claim. - Red-line checkers catch compliance violations defined by service state rather than prose, demonstrated concretely in insurance_task1: Kimi K2.5 passes 7 turn-3 prose-level checks while approving a claim before the technical report arrives, a violation visible only in the backend state and costing −9.3 pp. - Checker-hacking audits during construction — explicitly reviewing rubric items to ensure they cannot be satisfied by surface keyword emission — represent methodological discipline that most benchmark papers omit and that directly strengthens the benchmark's construct validity. Author-Acknowledged Limitations The authors directly acknowledge: (1) single-sweep results for 5 of 7 models with run-to-run variance bounded only for Kimi K2.6 and GPT-5.4; rankings within the 3.8 pp top-3 cluster are explicitly flagged as tentative. (2) Silent/loud classification uses author-supplied comments and is "sensitive to borderline cases." (3) EDA contains one task and its row is flagged as a case-level result, not a stable trend. (4) All models run under OpenClaw; no claim of empirical equivalence across different agent frameworks. The authors confront these limitations directly, which is a credibility positive, though the caveats appear only in footnotes and subsections rather than a consolidated limitations section. Analyst-Identified Gaps - With 1–6 tasks in several scenarios (Clinical Assistant: 4, EDA: 1, Legal Assistant: 6), per-scenario conclusions are statistically fragile. The authors flag EDA but not the other small-N scenarios; Legal Assistant (6 tasks, GPT-5.4 scores 59.5 vs. Sonnet's 84.1) may reflect a single task's characteristics rather than a generalizable gap. - All seven models run under OpenClaw with six model-specific harness patches applied; the authors disclaim cross-harness equivalence but do not assess whether those patches differentially affect model scores, leaving open whether the observed gaps partly reflect harness compatibility rather than intrinsic model capability. - The construction pipeline relies on a Codex-class reviewer agent for trajectory review, creating a potential circularity: if the reviewer agent shares failure modes with the evaluated models, those design flaws pass the release gate and systematically inflate scores for all models on the same tasks. No human validation of the reviewer agent's coverage is reported. - Day-2 performance drops are attributed to exogenous state changes, but the confound with longer prompt context (more history at Day 2) and accumulated tool-call errors is not disentangled. A condition with equivalent context length but no exogenous mutation would isolate the causal contribution of the mutation itself. - No human-performance baseline is reported, making it impossible to assess whether the benchmark is well-calibrated for difficulty or whether some scenarios are trivially solvable by a human professional (which would contextualize the low Task Success rates). - The task distribution across scenarios (15 Research Assistant tasks vs. 1 EDA task) is uneven and unmotivated; it is unclear whether the corpus represents real coworker workload distributions or reflects authoring convenience. Bottom Line ClawMark fills a genuine and well-defined gap — it is the only benchmark combining multi-day tasks, exogenous state mutation, full multimodal evidence, and deterministic scoring — and its failure taxonomy delivers the most operationally precise diagnostic of coworker-agent weaknesses currently available; practitioners building persistent agents should read it, though the 100-task corpus size and single-harness evaluation limit the statistical weight of its model rankings.

Large Language Models Explore by Latent Distilling

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces Exploratory Sampling (ESamp), a decoding algorithm that reweights next-token probabilities at each generation step using prediction error from a lightweight online-trained MLP (Latent Distiller) mapping first-layer hidden states to final-layer hidden states, steering generation toward semantically novel regions. - **Why it's important:** Test-time scaling via candidate sampling is bottlenecked by semantic redundancy — increasing k in Pass@k yields diminishing returns when candidates share the same underlying reasoning path, limiting the ability of selection mechanisms (voting, reranking, verification) to find correct solutions. - **How it does it:** A 2-layer MLP is trained online during generation via MSE loss; high prediction error signals a novel representation mapping; the KL-regularized RL framework yields a closed-form logit reweighting logit_new = (1+β)·logit_ref − β·logit_dist, where the Distiller is shared across all K parallel sequences, providing implicit cross-sequence exploration coordination. - **The good:** On GPT-OSS-20B, ESamp achieves Pass@8 performance comparable to baseline Pass@64 on AIME benchmarks; a noise ablation replacing the error vector with magnitude-matched Gaussian noise collapses performance to vanilla level, confirming the error direction encodes structured semantic information rather than acting as random perturbation. - **The missing:** All experiments use models ≤32B parameters with context ≤8K tokens; the closest competitor (OverRIDE) is an anonymous under-review submission, weakening the primary comparison; gains on GPQA-Diamond are marginal and the limitation to multiple-choice scientific reasoning is not explicitly discussed. - **Read it or not:** Worth reading for researchers working on test-time scaling, diverse decoding, or LLM serving — the latent-space RND-inspired novelty signal is a non-obvious insight with strong ablation support and a practical open-source implementation.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces Exploratory Sampling (ESamp), a decoding algorithm that reweights next-token probabilities at each generation step using prediction error from a lightweight online-trained MLP (Latent Distiller) mapping first-layer hidden states to final-layer hidden states, steering generation toward semantically novel regions. - Why it's important: Test-time scaling via candidate sampling is bottlenecked by semantic redundancy — increasing k in Pass@k yields diminishing returns when candidates share the same underlying reasoning path, limiting the ability of selection mechanisms (voting, reranking, verification) to find correct solutions. - How it does it: A 2-layer MLP is trained online during generation via MSE loss; high prediction error signals a novel representation mapping; the KL-regularized RL framework yields a closed-form logit reweighting logit_new = (1+β)·logit_ref − β·logit_dist, where the Distiller is shared across all K parallel sequences, providing implicit cross-sequence exploration coordination. - The good: On GPT-OSS-20B, ESamp achieves Pass@8 performance comparable to baseline Pass@64 on AIME benchmarks; a noise ablation replacing the error vector with magnitude-matched Gaussian noise collapses performance to vanilla level, confirming the error direction encodes structured semantic information rather than acting as random perturbation. - The missing: All experiments use models ≤32B parameters with context ≤8K tokens; the closest competitor (OverRIDE) is an anonymous under-review submission, weakening the primary comparison; gains on GPQA-Diamond are marginal and the limitation to multiple-choice scientific reasoning is not explicitly discussed. - Read it or not: Worth reading for researchers working on test-time scaling, diverse decoding, or LLM serving — the latent-space RND-inspired novelty signal is a non-obvious insight with strong ablation support and a practical open-source implementation. Problem & Motivation: Test-time scaling approaches — generating K candidate solutions and applying selection via reranking, majority voting, or verification — are fundamentally capped by the semantic diversity of the candidate pool. Standard stochastic sampling (temperature, top-p, min-p) generates surface-level lexical variation but candidates frequently converge on the same underlying reasoning path, especially for structured tasks like competition math. If all K candidates fail via the same systematic error, selection mechanisms cannot recover the correct answer. Structured search (Tree of Thoughts) achieves genuine exploration but multiplies compute by the branching factor. The gap is a mechanism that efficiently diversifies the reasoning strategies in a candidate batch without proportional overhead. Key Idea: The core insight adapts Random Network Distillation (RND) from RL exploration to LLM decoding: a lightweight MLP trained online during generation will exhibit high prediction error on representation mappings it has not yet seen. By training this MLP to map first-layer hidden states to final-layer hidden states using all parallel sequences in the batch, the distiller learns what reasoning paths the batch has already explored. Projecting the prediction error through the frozen LM head produces a structured logit correction that amplifies tokens leading to under-explored semantic regions. A single shared distiller across K parallel sequences implicitly coordinates exploration — once sequence A claims a semantic region, the distiller fits that mapping and suppresses subsequent sequences from revisiting it. Methodology: The pipeline runs two interleaved CUDA streams per decode step. The Latent Distiller (LD) is a 2-layer Gated SwiGLU MLP with 384-dim intermediate and input/output dimensions matching the host LLM hidden size. At each decode step, Layer 1 of the LLM outputs h_1; the LD asynchronously predicts ĥ_L = f_φ(h_1) while Layers 2..L run on the main stream. Both h_L and ĥ_L are projected through the frozen LM head to produce logit_ref and logit_dist. The reweighted sampling distribution is logit_new = (1+β)·logit_ref − β·logit_dist (Eq. 7), where β=0.25 is the default exploration strength. After sampling, the LD backward pass runs on the GPU during the CPU-bound post-processing interval using batch-averaged MSE loss over all parallel sequences: L(φ) = (1/|B|)Σ ‖h_L − f_φ(h_1)‖². Three critical design choices: (1) Using first-layer hidden states maximizes the GPU-overlap window — this is dictated by system constraints but also concentrates the representation space at the shallowest, most context-agnostic level. (2) The (1+β) fusion formula (vs. naive logit_ref − β·logit_dist) preserves relative probability mass of the base model; the ablation shows naive subtraction at β=0.5 collapses Pass@64 from 46.7% to 13.3% on AIME25. (3) A shared Distiller across all K sequences creates an implicit "first-come, first-served" scheduler without any explicit cross-sequence communication overhead. Key Results: On AIME 2024/2025 (30 competition math problems each), ESamp achieves Pass@64 of 80.0% vs. vanilla 70.0% for Qwen3-8B on AIME24; on GPT-OSS-20B, ESamp Pass@8 matches or exceeds baseline Pass@64 on both AIME benchmarks. On LiveCodeBench v5 (167 code problems), ESamp Pass@1 reaches 51.8% for GPT-OSS-20B at context 4096 vs. vanilla 43.4%, and 61.5% at context 16384 vs. vanilla 52.2%. On GPQA-Diamond (198 science multiple-choice), gains are marginal: ESamp 63.2% vs. vanilla 62.4% Pass@1 for GPT-OSS-20B. In creative writing (BookCorpus, 2000 stories), ESamp achieves the highest Vendi diversity score (1.67 vs. vanilla 1.62) and lowest perplexity (3.55 vs. vanilla 4.08) simultaneously — the only method that improves both metrics. Throughput overhead is 1.81% in standard serving (B=32, K=1) and 4.25% in test-time scaling (B=32, K=16) on an RTX4090 with Qwen3-8B. The noise ablation on Qwen3-8B (Table 10) shows ESamp Pass@16/Pass@64 of 57.0%/63.9% vs. magnitude-matched noise 52.4%/60.0% vs. vanilla 53.6%/58.9% on AIME25, confirming the error direction's structured content. β=0.25 is robust across Qwen3-4B/8B/14B without per-model tuning. Baselines are current: vanilla temperature, Min-p (ICLR 2025), FIRE (NAACL 2025), Tree of Thoughts (NeurIPS 2023), Contrastive Decoding (ACL 2023), and OverRIDE (under review at ICLR). All results are from a preprint (April 2026); not peer reviewed. Novelty Classification: NEW METHOD + NEW SYSTEM/TOOL. The genuine novelty is adapting RND from RL exploration to per-step LLM decoding via online latent distillation in continuous representation space, combined with a batch-shared Distiller that implicitly coordinates multi-sequence exploration without explicit cross-sequence communication. The KL-regularized framework is adapted from Mudgal et al. 2024; the RND analogy from Burda et al. 2019. The vocabulary-space version of the same idea (ablation C.4) is substantially inferior (ESamp 57.0% vs. Vocab-Space 37.4% Pass@16 on AIME25), confirming that the continuous representation space is essential, not a coincidental choice. Rigor & Reproducibility: Ablations are thorough: β sensitivity, logit fusion formulation, noise ablation (C.3), latent vs. vocabulary space (C.4), multi-seed stability over 3 seeds (C.5), baseline hyperparameter grids with 3-point sweeps (C.6), entropy-adaptive baseline comparison (C.7), composability with FIRE and Self-Consistency (C.8), Distiller architecture (C.9), and shared vs. per-prompt Distillers (C.10). Three-seed results for Qwen3-8B report standard deviations (e.g., AIME25 ESamp Pass@64: 67.8 ± 1.9 vs. vanilla 58.9 ± 0.9). Code is open-sourced at github.com/LinesHogan/tLLM; main paper codebase released "upon acceptance." Primary concern: OverRIDE is cited as an anonymous under-review submission — this is the closest competitor and the comparison cannot be independently verified. Secondary concern: AIME 2024/2025 has only 30 problems each; Pass@k curves from 30 problems carry high variance, and confidence intervals for the Pass@k plots are not shown. Deployability (4-property check): Profile: EMPIRICAL (methods evaluated via experiments) + SYSTEMS (the tLLM framework is a deployed software artifact). - Practical: Runs on commodity inference hardware (RTX4090, A100); Distiller adds <200MB VRAM for an 8B model and can theoretically be reduced to ~50MB. No modification to LLM weights is required. - Efficient: 4.25% throughput reduction in the test-time scaling scenario (B=32, K=16); Distiller forward pass requires <0.5ms vs. 15-20ms available window (Llama-3-8B on A100), providing a large overlap margin that may shrink for smaller or faster models. - Effective: The noise ablation directly demonstrates that the improvement is not from added stochasticity; ESamp improves Pass@k across 4 models and 4 benchmarks, while competitors fail on at least one domain (FIRE hurts LiveCodeBench, OverRIDE shows mixed results). - Scalable: Tested up to 32B parameters and K=64 samples; behavior at 70B+ models or >8K token chains is not evaluated. The per-prompt vs. shared Distiller trade-off (Table 18) is task-dependent and may require re-tuning at scale. Throughput benefits diminish under fully saturated GPU utilization (authors note this explicitly). Software artifact: Open-sourced as ESamp within the tLLM framework, built on vLLM v1 with CUDA graph support, FlashInfer sampling, and a producer-consumer abstraction for future runtime-adaptation algorithms. Strengths: - The noise ablation (Section C.3) is the paper's most credible piece of evidence: replacing the Distiller error vector with Gaussian noise of matched magnitude recovers vanilla-level performance, directly falsifying the hypothesis that ESamp is merely injecting beneficial noise and confirming the direction of the error vector carries structured semantic content. - The (1+β) logit fusion formula has a concrete, testable justification: the naive subtraction variant collapses Pass@64 from 46.7% to 13.3% at β=0.5 (Table 2), a 33-point gap that illustrates why probability mass preservation matters for coherence. - ESamp simultaneously achieves the highest diversity (Vendi) and highest coherence (lowest PPL) in creative writing — breaking the diversity-quality trade-off rather than just shifting it — which is the central empirical claim and is backed by both automatic metrics and LLM-as-judge evaluation on 2000 prompts. - The engineering depth is unusual for an algorithms paper: custom CUDA kernels, GPU ring buffers, CUDA Event synchronization (not CPU barriers), mixed-batch guardrails, and a Triton grouped prediction backend are all described, benchmarked, and open-sourced. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors explicitly note: (a) ESamp targets candidate-set coverage rather than single-sample accuracy, and small Pass@1 regressions occur in some settings (Qwen2.5-7B AIME25: 6.0% vs. vanilla 6.6%); (b) throughput benefits diminish under fully saturated GPU utilization; (c) the per-prompt vs. shared Distiller trade-off is task-dependent and adaptive strategies are left as future work; (d) the theoretical vanishing redundancy condition (Prop. A.2) relies on empirically supported but unproved assumptions (Rapid Fitting, Local Generalization). The authors confront these limitations directly rather than minimizing them. Analyst-Identified Gaps: The primary competitor OverRIDE is an anonymous under-review submission ("Anonymous, 2025") whose hyperparameters are taken from its OpenReview supplementary — if the paper is rejected, revised, or withdrawn, the key comparison cannot be reproduced. AIME 2024/2025 each contain exactly 30 problems; Pass@k estimates from 30 problems are noisy (e.g., the difference between 31.7% and 30.3% Pass@16 is 0.4 problems in expectation), but confidence intervals for the Pass@k curves are not shown. Gains on GPQA-Diamond are marginal (<1% across all models) — ESamp's benefit appears concentrated on open-ended multi-step reasoning (math, code) rather than factual multiple-choice science, a limitation not explicitly discussed. The paper does not test whether the latent distillation property holds at 70B+ scale or whether the 384-dim MLP remains a useful predictor of final-layer representations for very deep models. The case study in Section E.6 uses a single math problem evaluated by a single LLM judge (Gemini Flash) — illustrative but not statistically meaningful. Bottom Line: A practitioner building test-time scaling pipelines for reasoning models gains a concrete, low-overhead diversification method with unusually thorough ablation support; the latent RND framing is a genuinely non-obvious insight, and the open-source implementation makes it immediately usable.

AutoResearchBench: Benchmarking AI Agents on Complex Scientific Literature Discovery

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces AutoResearchBench, a 1,000-query benchmark for evaluating AI agents on autonomous scientific literature discovery, comprising two task types — Deep Research (identify the single paper satisfying a conjunction of obfuscated full-text constraints, or establish that none exists) and Wide Research (exhaustively retrieve all papers satisfying a multi-part scientific specification) — over a corpus of 3M+ arXiv papers. - **Why it's important:** Existing agentic benchmarks (BrowseComp, GAIA) measure general web navigation and score above 80% for frontier models, yet they do not test whether agents can perform genuine multi-hop reasoning over full scientific text, verify fine-grained technical constraints, or determine when a literature search is complete — capabilities that are central to autonomous research. - **How it does it:** Queries are constructed via a full-text-first human–machine pipeline that mines subtle constraints from appendices, ablation tables, and citation chains (never headline contributions), applies two-level fuzzification to suppress keyword shortcuts, enforces minimal sufficiency so removing any clue admits alternative papers, and validates each instance through a four-stage adversarial pipeline (LLM shortcut screening, ReAct agent stress testing, timed human search, corpus-level uniqueness audit). - **The good:** All evaluated frontier models — including Claude-Opus-4.6, Gemini-3.1-Pro, and GPT-5.4 — fail to exceed 10% on either task, with most falling below 5%, and a manual audit confirms 96% of model "false positives" in Wide Research are genuine true negatives, establishing both a large unsolved performance gap and high label quality. - **The missing:** The benchmark is restricted to computer science papers in a fixed arXiv corpus; whether difficulty is intrinsic to scientific reasoning or is partly an artifact of the specific corpus, domain, or DeepXiv tool is not disentangled. - **Read it or not:** Essential for researchers building or evaluating autonomous scientific agents; researchers focused on general web browsing agents or non-CS literature will find the scope too narrow to apply results directly.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces AutoResearchBench, a 1,000-query benchmark for evaluating AI agents on autonomous scientific literature discovery, comprising two task types — Deep Research (identify the single paper satisfying a conjunction of obfuscated full-text constraints, or establish that none exists) and Wide Research (exhaustively retrieve all papers satisfying a multi-part scientific specification) — over a corpus of 3M+ arXiv papers. - Why it's important: Existing agentic benchmarks (BrowseComp, GAIA) measure general web navigation and score above 80% for frontier models, yet they do not test whether agents can perform genuine multi-hop reasoning over full scientific text, verify fine-grained technical constraints, or determine when a literature search is complete — capabilities that are central to autonomous research. - How it does it: Queries are constructed via a full-text-first human–machine pipeline that mines subtle constraints from appendices, ablation tables, and citation chains (never headline contributions), applies two-level fuzzification to suppress keyword shortcuts, enforces minimal sufficiency so removing any clue admits alternative papers, and validates each instance through a four-stage adversarial pipeline (LLM shortcut screening, ReAct agent stress testing, timed human search, corpus-level uniqueness audit). - The good: All evaluated frontier models — including Claude-Opus-4.6, Gemini-3.1-Pro, and GPT-5.4 — fail to exceed 10% on either task, with most falling below 5%, and a manual audit confirms 96% of model "false positives" in Wide Research are genuine true negatives, establishing both a large unsolved performance gap and high label quality. - The missing: The benchmark is restricted to computer science papers in a fixed arXiv corpus; whether difficulty is intrinsic to scientific reasoning or is partly an artifact of the specific corpus, domain, or DeepXiv tool is not disentangled. - Read it or not: Essential for researchers building or evaluating autonomous scientific agents; researchers focused on general web browsing agents or non-CS literature will find the scope too narrow to apply results directly. Problem & Motivation: Autonomous research agents repeatedly need to find scientific literature — not topically similar papers, but papers satisfying a conjunction of precise technical constraints whose decisive evidence resides in method details, ablation tables, figure captions, appendices, and citation chains rather than titles or abstracts. This task is qualitatively distinct from general web search on two axes: complexity (technical, fast-moving, domain-specific evidence distributed across full documents) and open-endedness (the number of qualifying papers is unknown and may be zero, requiring agents to reason about exploration completeness and when to abstain). Prior academic-specific datasets (RealScholarQuery, SPAR) are too small and lack interactive search environments; broad agentic benchmarks (GAIA, BrowseComp) do not require full-paper reading or open-ended completeness reasoning. The gap leaves unclear how close current agents are to being useful as autonomous literature reviewers. Key Idea: The core technical insight driving Deep Research task design is the minimal-sufficiency constraint principle: each query is built from full-text evidence such that the conjunction of clues uniquely locates exactly one paper in a 3M-paper corpus, removing any single clue admits alternative papers, and no lexical reformulation retrieves the target via shallow keyword matching. This forces genuine multi-hop evidence integration rather than paraphrastic retrieval. For Wide Research, the complementary idea is entity-graph-based query construction: shared multidimensional attributes (method, dataset, results) are extracted from a candidate pool, encoded as a strict logical conjunction, and used to define a query whose complete answer set must be exhaustively recovered — testing set-boundary reasoning, not just recall-oriented search. Methodology: The benchmark contains 600 Deep Research and 400 Wide Research queries spanning eight CS domains (CV 30.7%, ML 20.2%, NLP 12.5%, Multimodal 11.5%, others). For Deep Research, under-exposed high-quality papers (10–100 citations, excluding surveys) are selected; annotators read full text including supplementary material and extract verifiable constraints from secondary methodological choices, proof details, and fine-grained empirical observations — explicitly avoiding headline contributions. Constraints are fuzzified at topic and detail levels and pruned to the minimal sufficient set. No-answer instances (10% of Deep queries) are created by perturbing one core constraint so no valid paper exists. Four-stage adversarial verification retains an instance only if: (1) GPT-5.4 reformulations cannot retrieve the target shallowly, (2) Claude-Sonnet-4.6 and Gemini-3-Flash ReAct agents fail to solve it, (3) human annotators cannot solve it within 10 minutes using the same tools, and (4) corpus-level audit confirms exactly one valid paper. For Wide Research, candidate pools are domain-sourced, an entity graph is constructed, a strict-conjunction query is formulated and verified, answer sets are expanded via 7,136 LLM-generated search queries yielding 31,734 candidates that are screened to 23,217 by abstract filtering, 20,251 verified for full text, and 4,887 admitted by unanimous three-LLM majority vote; human experts audit 50% of passing papers and require ≥75% precision per query. Evaluation runs all models in a standardized ReAct loop (max 30 turns, soft context budget 1.1×10^5 tokens, temperature 0.6) over the DeepXiv full-text search tool. Deep Research is scored by exact-match accuracy; Wide Research by IoU = |Ŷ ∩ Y| / |Ŷ ∪ Y|. Critical design decisions: (1) full-text-first constraint mining over metadata-only — well-justified by the task's requirement that evidence be non-surfaceable via abstracts; (2) minimal-sufficiency pruning — well-justified as it prevents benchmark contamination by over-specified queries; (3) fixed ReAct evaluation loop shared across all models — appropriate for isolating model capability but does not distinguish model vs. tool-interaction effects. Key Results: Best Deep Research accuracy: Claude-Opus-4.6 at 9.39% (28.1 avg turns, 642s). Best Wide Research IoU: Gemini-3.1-Pro at 9.31% (4.55 avg turns, 235s). Most evaluated models score below 5% on both tasks. GPT-5.4 achieves 7.44% Deep accuracy in only 6.1 turns versus DeepSeek-V3.2's 4.21% in 28.8 turns, showing that more search iterations do not compensate for weak evidence integration. End-to-end systems: GPT Deep Research 11/50 queries correct (Deep), Alphaxiv 0/50, AI-studio Gemini-3.1-Pro 7/50. Thinking mode (extended reasoning) is inconsistently helpful: Qwen3-Max THINK 2.33% vs NOTHINK 3.24% (Deep); Gemini-3-Flash THINK 1.83% vs NOTHINK 2.75% (Deep). DeepXiv full-text search outperforms open-web (Jina) search by 1.45 percentage points averaged across four models on Deep Research (5.42% vs 3.97%). Test-time scaling (pass@k up to k=16) helps Deep Research more than Wide Research, indicating that Deep failures are brittle trajectory decisions while Wide failures are recall bottlenecks that repeated runs do not resolve. Claude-Sonnet-4.6 achieves the highest recall on Wide Research (0.3408) but the lowest precision (0.0586), versus Seed-2.0-Pro's highest precision (0.1787) with low recall (0.1304). Manual audit of model over-predictions: 96% were genuine true negatives confirming annotation completeness. Dataset construction cost: ~$6,700 API + ~580 expert-hours; per-model evaluation cost ~$27. Novelty Classification: NEW DATASET/BENCHMARK + NEW EMPIRICAL FINDING. The benchmark construction methodology (full-text-first, minimal-sufficiency, four-stage adversarial verification) is technically non-obvious and represents a genuine advance over metadata-based or backward-constructed academic search benchmarks. The empirical finding that all frontier models fail to exceed 10% — while exceeding 80% on BrowseComp — quantifies a previously unmeasured and substantial capability gap. The task formulations (exact-match conjunctive paper identification; open-ended exhaustive set retrieval) are applied to a new domain but draw on established paradigms from information retrieval and agentic web search. Rigor & Reproducibility: Dataset quality is validated by a rigorous, multi-stage human-LLM pipeline and independently confirmed by the 96% true-negative audit. Code, dataset, and evaluation pipeline are publicly released (https://github.com/CherYou/AutoResearchBench). No ablations are presented on construction design choices (fuzzification depth, 10% no-answer ratio, constraint count per query), so the sensitivity of task difficulty to these parameters is unknown. Inter-annotator agreement rates are not reported, making independent assessment of annotation reliability difficult. The benchmark is a preprint and has not undergone peer review. Evaluation relies on the DeepXiv platform, which is not a standard public service; reproducibility is contingent on continued platform access. Deployability (EMPIRICAL): - Practical: The evaluation environment requires API access to frontier LLMs and the DeepXiv full-text search platform; LLM APIs are commercially available, but DeepXiv's operational continuity for external researchers is not guaranteed. - Efficient: Per-model evaluation costs approximately $27 in API fees; construction required ~$6,700 and ~580 expert-hours, both within reach of well-resourced academic groups but not trivially reproducible. - Effective: The benchmark demonstrably measures a capability distinct from general web browsing (90+ percentage point performance gap versus BrowseComp), and the ground-truth quality audit supports confidence in the labels; however, evaluation is mediated by a specific search tool (DeepXiv), so scores reflect model × tool performance rather than model capability in isolation. - Scalable: The corpus is fixed at 3M+ CS papers; extension to other disciplines, dynamic corpora, and multi-modal evidence is explicitly deferred and would require substantial re-annotation investment. The work delivers a dataset, evaluation pipeline, and code as software artifacts. Strengths: - The four-stage adversarial verification pipeline for Deep Research — including agent-based stress testing and timed human adversarial search — is methodologically rigorous and substantially reduces the risk of benchmark contamination by trivially solvable or ambiguously posed queries. - The dual-task design captures two genuinely distinct and practically important literature-search demands (precise identification vs. exhaustive coverage), whereas prior benchmarks address only one or neither. - The structured nine-category error taxonomy with quantified per-model error distributions (e.g., Claude Opus 4.5 Wide Research dominated 85.3% by precision-unconstrained expansion) provides actionable diagnostic signal beyond aggregate scores. - The controlled 3M-paper full-text corpus with a standardized search tool enables apples-to-apples comparison across models, avoiding the confounds inherent in open-web evaluation. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors explicitly state the benchmark is restricted to CS papers in a fixed corpus, leaving cross-domain science, multi-modal evidence, and continually evolving literature for future work. They acknowledge that exhaustive answer sets for Wide Research may still be incomplete at corpus boundaries despite rigorous construction. Calibration, abstention behavior, and more efficient long-horizon search strategies are identified as open problems. Analyst-Identified Gaps: - All evaluations use a shared fixed ReAct loop and system prompt; the results measure model × tool × prompt interaction rather than isolated model capability, yet no ablation isolates these factors. - The 10% no-answer rate in Deep Research is an arbitrary design parameter; no analysis is provided of whether models are appropriately calibrated to abstain at this rate, or how this choice affects overall benchmark difficulty and model rankings. - Inter-annotator agreement statistics for human verification stages are absent, limiting independent assessment of annotation consistency. - IoU for Wide Research treats all set sizes equally; a query with 2 correct papers and one missed is penalized identically to a query with 20 papers and one missed, yet the latter is trivially harder — no stratified analysis by set size is provided. - The benchmark relies on a proprietary platform (DeepXiv) for both construction and evaluation; long-term reproducibility and community access are unconfirmed. Bottom Line: Researchers building or evaluating autonomous scientific literature agents should read this paper — the benchmark establishes a clear, reproducible performance floor and provides the most rigorous diagnostic of current agentic scientific search failures available, even if corpus scope and tool-dependency limit generalization.

Understanding Evolving Graph Structures for Large Discrete-Time Dynamic Graph Representation

www.vldb.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper introduces UnderGS, a scalable GPU-resident framework for discrete-time dynamic graph (DTDG) representation that replaces adjacency-matrix neighbor management with a temporal-cohesive neighbor store, achieving up to 9× training speed-up and 31.36% average accuracy improvement over nine baselines across eight datasets. - **Why it's important:** Adjacency-matrix-based neighbor management for DTDGs consumes O(T|V|²) memory, prevents GPU-native batch processing, and captures only binary connectivity — making existing methods unable to scale to graphs with millions of edges or to faithfully represent structural evolution across snapshots. - **How it does it:** A GPU key-value store retains the top-K neighbors per node scored by a composite temporal influence score combining structural cohesiveness (node importance via subgraph density), path-length decay (α₁^(l-1)), and snapshot recency decay (α₂^(t-t')), together with a late-snapshot gradient aggregation mechanism that prevents information leakage during batch training. - **The good:** UnderGS is the only evaluated method that scales to the Stack-Overflow (2.6M nodes, 63M edges, 92 snapshots) and Wiki-Talk (1.1M nodes, 7.8M edges) datasets without out-of-memory errors, while simultaneously outperforming all baselines on both AUC and MRR tasks. - **The missing:** All experiments run on a single RTX 3090 (24 GB); the paper makes no claim about multi-GPU performance, and scalability beyond this hardware — or behavior on graphs with denser temporal dynamics than the tested datasets — is unevaluated. - **Read it or not:** Worth reading for researchers and engineers working on DTDG representation learning, temporal GNNs, or scalable graph ML infrastructure; it provides a principled, hardware-aware alternative to adjacency-matrix neighbor management with strong empirical evidence.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper introduces UnderGS, a scalable GPU-resident framework for discrete-time dynamic graph (DTDG) representation that replaces adjacency-matrix neighbor management with a temporal-cohesive neighbor store, achieving up to 9× training speed-up and 31.36% average accuracy improvement over nine baselines across eight datasets. - Why it's important: Adjacency-matrix-based neighbor management for DTDGs consumes O(T|V|²) memory, prevents GPU-native batch processing, and captures only binary connectivity — making existing methods unable to scale to graphs with millions of edges or to faithfully represent structural evolution across snapshots. - How it does it: A GPU key-value store retains the top-K neighbors per node scored by a composite temporal influence score combining structural cohesiveness (node importance via subgraph density), path-length decay (α₁^(l-1)), and snapshot recency decay (α₂^(t-t')), together with a late-snapshot gradient aggregation mechanism that prevents information leakage during batch training. - The good: UnderGS is the only evaluated method that scales to the Stack-Overflow (2.6M nodes, 63M edges, 92 snapshots) and Wiki-Talk (1.1M nodes, 7.8M edges) datasets without out-of-memory errors, while simultaneously outperforming all baselines on both AUC and MRR tasks. - The missing: All experiments run on a single RTX 3090 (24 GB); the paper makes no claim about multi-GPU performance, and scalability beyond this hardware — or behavior on graphs with denser temporal dynamics than the tested datasets — is unevaluated. - Read it or not: Worth reading for researchers and engineers working on DTDG representation learning, temporal GNNs, or scalable graph ML infrastructure; it provides a principled, hardware-aware alternative to adjacency-matrix neighbor management with strong empirical evidence. Problem & Motivation: Discrete-time dynamic graphs model systems (retail, finance, social networks) that evolve through discrete snapshots, where each snapshot is a static graph. The central challenge for representation learning is neighbor management: existing approaches either maintain a full adjacency matrix per snapshot — consuming O(T|V|²) memory and restricting scalability — or preprocess multiple adjacency matrices into node sequences for sequence models, incurring hours of preprocessing and CPU–GPU transfer overhead (e.g., FALCON requires 2.2 hours on Reddit-title). Both lines fail simultaneously: snapshot-focused GNN approaches lose cross-snapshot temporal context, while sequence-based approaches lose intra-snapshot structural context. Neither line scales to graphs with millions of nodes or hundreds of snapshots because they cannot batch-process efficiently on a single GPU. The gap is a neighbor management strategy that captures structural evolution, preserves temporal context, and fits in GPU memory without preprocessing. Key Idea: The core insight is to replace the adjacency matrix with a GPU-resident key-value store that, for each node, retains exactly K neighbors selected by a temporal influence score — a single scalar that fuses three quantities: structural importance of the neighbor (cohesiveness of its local subgraph), path-length discount (closer hops score higher), and snapshot recency discount (more recent interactions score higher). Because the store caps at K entries per node, total memory drops from O(T|V|²) to O(|V|K), independent of snapshot count. The store is maintained incrementally as new snapshots arrive: L-hop traversals compute influence scores for candidates; new neighbors displace incumbents if their scores are higher; a snapshot decay pass reduces all stored scores at snapshot boundaries. This design allows direct GPU access for message passing without preprocessing, and the influence score serves as an aggregation weight replacing degree normalization. Methodology: UnderGS consists of four interacting components. (1) Temporal influence score: For node vᵢ(t) and neighbor vⱼ(t') interacting over l hops, Iᵢⱼ(t) = Cⱼ(t') · α₁^(l-1) · α₂^(t-t'), where Cⱼ(t') is the node importance defined as a sum of exponential terms over the interaction counts within vⱼ's subgraph, α₁ ∈ (0,1) is the path-decay hyperparameter, and α₂ ∈ (0,1) is the snapshot-decay factor. Node importance distinguishes structurally cohesive neighbors from peripheral ones, which degree alone cannot. The paper proves three formal properties: Snapshot Decay (Theorem 4.6 — more recent neighbors are preferred when structurally comparable), Cohesive Priority (Theorem 4.7 — neighbors in cohesive subgraphs are retained over sparser ones), and Path Decay (Theorem 4.8 — direct neighbors score higher than equidistant distant ones). (2) Temporal-cohesive neighbor store: A GPU key-value store with O(1) lookup and update per node; initialized from scratch and updated incrementally per batch or snapshot; reduces storage from O(T|V|²) to O(|V|K). K=16 and L=2 in all experiments. (3) Time-aware filter: For nodes with sparse current-snapshot interactions, historical neighbors can dominate. A Bernoulli mask with probability ρ (tuned by dataset density: ρ=0.3 for high density, ρ=0.5 for low density) randomly zeros out historical neighbor influence scores before aggregation, shifting focus toward current interactions. (4) Late-snapshot gradient aggregation: Each snapshot G(t) is partitioned into B(t) mini-batches; losses are computed independently per batch using binary cross-entropy on link prediction labels, but gradients are accumulated across all batches of the snapshot and applied once after the full snapshot is processed (θ ← θ − η · (1/B(t)) ΣᵦᵢB(t) ∇L^b_{G(t)}). This prevents later batches from being influenced by parameter updates from earlier batches within the same snapshot (information leakage). The framework is compatible with six neural network architectures (GCN, GIN, GAT, GraphSAGE, MLP Mixer, Transformer), using influence scores as aggregation weights in lieu of degree normalization; message embedding Mᵢⱼ(t) = [Zⱼ(t-1) ‖ eᵢⱼ(t') ‖ φ(t-t')]Wₘ concatenates node embedding, edge features, and a time encoding. Key Results: Evaluated on 8 datasets spanning multiple domains and scales (UCI-Message: 1,899 nodes; Stack-Overflow: 2.6M nodes, 63M edges, 92 snapshots). Baselines are 9 models: 4 GNN-based (EvolveGCN-H, EvolveGCN-O, Roland, WinGNN) and 5 sequence-based (DGNN-LSTM, DGNN-GRU, DGNN-Transformer, SimpleDyG, FALCON). All experiments use a single GeForce RTX 3090 (24 GB RAM). Key quantitative outcomes: (1) AUC (link prediction): UnderGS achieves best results on 7/8 datasets; up to 13.56% improvement over the best baseline; e.g., 99.97% AUC on Bitcoin-OTC vs. 99.75% for second-best. Only Roland is competitive on small datasets. (2) MRR (link ranking): UnderGS achieves best on all 8 datasets; average 59.20% improvement; e.g., 95.01% MRR on Bitcoin-Alpha vs. 36.20% for second-best (SimpleDyG). (3) Training efficiency: Up to 9× overall speed-up vs. best baseline; up to 6× per-epoch speed-up. (4) Scalability: 4 of 9 baselines (DGNN variants, FALCON) OOM on 5+ datasets; only UnderGS completes all 8 including Stack-Overflow and Wiki-Talk. (5) vs. TGL (multi-GPU framework): UnderGS achieves 94× per-epoch sampling speed-up, 10× overall training speed-up, and 24.07% better AUC+MRR on average — while running on a single GPU. Ablation (Table 3): removing snapshot decay drops MRR by up to 82.30%; removing path decay drops by up to 26.02%; replacing node importance with k-core drops MRR by 9.29% on average; the time-aware filter contributes 4.35% on average. Training pipeline ablation (Table 4): pipeline yields 6.93–10.40× overall speed-up and 3.65–10.44× per-epoch speed-up vs. snapshot-by-snapshot training. Novelty Classification: NEW METHOD + NEW SYSTEM/TOOL + ENGINEERING OPTIMIZATION. The temporal influence score combining structural cohesiveness, path decay, and snapshot decay as a unified neighbor selection criterion is genuinely new; the GPU-resident incremental neighbor store that replaces adjacency matrices in GNN training is a novel infrastructure contribution; the late-snapshot gradient aggregation addressing information leakage in DTDG mini-batch training is a targeted engineering advance. The individual components (influence-weighted aggregation, GPU key-value stores, gradient accumulation) are known; the novelty is their integration into a principled, theoretically grounded neighbor management framework for DTDGs. Rigor & Reproducibility: Ablations are thorough: 5 structural variants tested across all 8 datasets (Table 3); training pipeline ablation on 2 datasets (Table 4); compatibility tested across 6 neural networks with and without influence scores (Figures 6–7). Experiments are repeated 10 times and mean ± standard deviation is reported. Code and data are publicly released at https://github.com/dani0108/UnderGS (PVLDB Artifact Availability badge confirmed). The paper was published in PVLDB Vol. 19, No. 5, 2026 (peer-reviewed). Hyperparameters (K=16, L=2, α₁=0.1, α₂=0.05, filter ratio 40%) are reported; ρ is density-dependent (ρ=0.3 or 0.5) which introduces a tuning step not fully automated. The embedding dimension is fixed at 128 and batch size at 200 for all models, ensuring fair comparison. Theoretical proofs for the three core properties are deferred to a technical report (Sections 2.1 and 2.2 thereof); the main paper contains proof sketches, which limits independent verification from the paper alone. Deployability (4-property check): Profile: EMPIRICAL + SYSTEMS (built framework evaluated by deployment on real datasets). - Practical: Runs on a single commodity GPU (RTX 3090, 24 GB) on all 8 tested datasets including the 63M-edge Stack-Overflow graph. - Efficient: Neighbor store reduces memory from O(T|V|²) to O(|V|K); eliminates preprocessing (2.2 hours for FALCON on Reddit-title); achieves 6–9× training speed-up over the best baselines; per-epoch sampling is 94× faster than TGL. - Effective: Outperforms all 9 baselines on AUC (7/8 datasets) and MRR (8/8 datasets) with statistically meaningful margins (error bars reported); ablations confirm each component contributes materially, not just the aggregate. - Scalable: Tested at up to 2.6M nodes and 63M edges (Stack-Overflow), 7.8M edges with 324 snapshots (Wiki-Talk); no experiment exceeds this scale; behavior at 10×–100× scale on billion-edge graphs is not tested, though the O(|V|K) theoretical bound is favorable. The paper delivers a software artifact (GitHub repository with source code and data). Strengths: - The temporal influence score is principled and theoretically grounded with three proven properties (Theorems 4.6–4.8), giving the neighbor selection mechanism a formal foundation absent in prior adjacency-matrix approaches. - The scalability gap versus competitors is substantial and not marginal: UnderGS is the only method completing all 8 datasets on a single GPU, including graphs where every alternative OOMs; the 94× sampling speed-up over TGL (a multi-GPU system) is the most striking empirical result. - Compatibility with six heterogeneous neural architectures (message-passing and non-message-passing) without architectural modifications demonstrates genuine generality of the neighbor store abstraction. - The late-snapshot gradient aggregation addresses a concrete information-leakage failure mode in DTDG batch training that prior work ignores, and ablation (Table 4) shows it yields 6.93–10.40× speed-up over snapshot-by-snapshot training. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The paper does not include a dedicated limitations section. The conclusion notes that UnderGS targets single-GPU settings, contrasting it with multi-GPU approaches; no explicit failure modes or scope boundaries are stated. The density-dependent filter probability ρ is acknowledged implicitly as a dataset-specific hyperparameter but not discussed as a limitation. The authors do not discuss behavior on highly skewed degree distributions (power-law graphs), adversarially structured temporal graphs, or directed weighted graphs beyond those tested. Analyst-Identified Gaps: - Hyperparameter sensitivity for ρ: The time-aware filter probability is set per dataset based on an undisclosed density threshold (10⁻⁴). The paper does not report a sensitivity analysis for ρ, leaving unclear how sensitive performance is to this choice and how a practitioner would set it without labeled validation data. - Single-hardware evaluation: All experiments use one specific GPU. The O(|V|K) memory claim is theoretically favorable, but the paper does not demonstrate behavior when the neighbor store itself exceeds GPU memory (e.g., billion-node graphs), nor does it discuss CPU-offload strategies for this regime. - Transductive setting only: All downstream tasks (link prediction, link ranking) are evaluated on edges within the training graph's node set. Inductive generalization to entirely unseen nodes at inference time is not evaluated, limiting applicability to dynamic graphs with frequent node arrivals. - Theoretical proof completeness: The three key theorems are proved in a separate technical report not included in the PVLDB submission; the main paper contains only proof sketches, which a peer reviewer cannot independently verify from the paper alone. - No comparison to hyperbolic or geometry-aware DTDG methods (e.g., the authors' own prior work on hyperbolic temporal graphs, refs [23, 47]) which have shown strong performance on sparse social graphs — an omission that slightly narrows the baseline comparison. Bottom Line: A practitioner building DTDG representation learning pipelines gains a concretely useful contribution here — UnderGS offers a principled, GPU-efficient replacement for adjacency-matrix neighbor management with strong empirical support across 8 diverse datasets and publicly released code, making it the current method of choice for single-GPU DTDG training at scale.

Where does output diversity collapse in post-training?

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper demonstrates, through a controlled comparison of three parallel post-training lineages of the Olmo 3 7B model, that output diversity collapse is determined by training data composition—not post-training algorithm—and cannot be recovered at inference time. - **Why it's important:** Inference-time scaling methods (pass@k, majority voting, self-consistency) depend on output diversity; if post-training eliminates that diversity, the scaling benefits are lost, and value-laden tasks risk homogenized outputs. - **How it does it:** The paper exploits Olmo 3's public checkpoints across three lineages—Think (narrow two-teacher CoT distillation), Instruct (broad multi-source), and RL-Zero (RL directly from base)—that share the same SFT→DPO→RL recipe but differ in data, then measures diversity at each checkpoint across 15 tasks with four metrics, and surgically removes CoT at inference to separate format from weights. - **The good:** The CoT suppression experiment (Think-not-thinking) is definitive: removing chain-of-thought format costs 18–32% accuracy on hard tasks but leaves SBERT diversity unchanged at every stage, proving the collapse is weight-embedded, not format-imposed. - **The missing:** The study is purely observational within existing checkpoints; no direct data composition intervention is run, so the causal claim that "more teachers → less collapse" is supported by cross-lineage comparison rather than a controlled experiment. - **Read it or not:** Read it if you work on LLM post-training, inference-time compute scaling, or alignment diversity; it provides concrete numbers and a clean experimental argument that inference-side fixes for diversity collapse are insufficient.
Full summary
TL;DR - What it does: This paper demonstrates, through a controlled comparison of three parallel post-training lineages of the Olmo 3 7B model, that output diversity collapse is determined by training data composition—not post-training algorithm—and cannot be recovered at inference time. - Why it's important: Inference-time scaling methods (pass@k, majority voting, self-consistency) depend on output diversity; if post-training eliminates that diversity, the scaling benefits are lost, and value-laden tasks risk homogenized outputs. - How it does it: The paper exploits Olmo 3's public checkpoints across three lineages—Think (narrow two-teacher CoT distillation), Instruct (broad multi-source), and RL-Zero (RL directly from base)—that share the same SFT→DPO→RL recipe but differ in data, then measures diversity at each checkpoint across 15 tasks with four metrics, and surgically removes CoT at inference to separate format from weights. - The good: The CoT suppression experiment (Think-not-thinking) is definitive: removing chain-of-thought format costs 18–32% accuracy on hard tasks but leaves SBERT diversity unchanged at every stage, proving the collapse is weight-embedded, not format-imposed. - The missing: The study is purely observational within existing checkpoints; no direct data composition intervention is run, so the causal claim that "more teachers → less collapse" is supported by cross-lineage comparison rather than a controlled experiment. - Read it or not: Read it if you work on LLM post-training, inference-time compute scaling, or alignment diversity; it provides concrete numbers and a clean experimental argument that inference-side fixes for diversity collapse are insufficient. Problem & Motivation: Post-trained LLMs produce less varied outputs than their base counterparts, and this homogenization undermines inference-time scaling methods—pass@k, majority voting, and self-consistency—that require diverse samples to aggregate over. The problem extends to creative and value-laden tasks where output homogenization risks imposing a single perspective. Prior work attributed collapse to specific algorithms (DPO in narrative generation, SFT in reasoning, reward in creative tasks) without disentangling whether the algorithm or the training data caused the drop, and without testing whether the chain-of-thought generation format itself constrains diversity at inference. Key Idea: When two models share identical post-training algorithms (SFT→DPO→RL) but differ only in data—narrow two-teacher CoT distillation versus broad multi-source instruction tuning—diversity collapses at different stages and by different magnitudes, proving that data composition, not algorithm, determines when and how sharply collapse occurs. The secondary insight is that suppressing CoT at inference on a collapsed model does not restore diversity, demonstrating the collapse is baked into learned weights rather than imposed by generation format. Methodology: The study evaluates 13 Olmo 3 7B checkpoints across three post-training lineages. Think uses SFT on ∼2.3M synthetic CoT traces from two teachers (QwQ-32B and DeepSeek-R1), followed by DPO on ∼200K Delta Learning pairs and GRPO-based RL on ∼105K prompts. Instruct initializes from Think-SFT, then trains on ∼2.2M multi-source examples (GPT-3.5, GPT-4, GPT-4.1) that strip reasoning traces, uses the same prompt pool for DPO but with GPT-judged pairs, and the same RL stage. RL-Zero applies RL directly to Base, bypassing SFT and DPO, in four domain-specific variants. Four complementary metrics are measured per checkpoint on 15 tasks across six categories: EAD (lexical, unique n-grams normalized for length bias), SBERT (semantic, mean pairwise cosine distance with all-mpnet-base-v2), NLI (logical, pairwise entailment/contradiction scoring with roberta-large-mnli), and Vendi Score (effective number of distinct semantic modes). K=16 outputs per prompt at T=0.6 are generated; for Think-lineage models, reasoning traces are stripped before computing metrics. A Think-not-thinking condition prefills an empty `<think>\n</think>` block to force direct answers, isolating format from weights. Quality-filtered diversity decomposes aggregate Da (SBERT on all outputs) into Dc (SBERT on correct-only subset) and Vc (Vendi Score on correct outputs) for six verifiable tasks. Three critical design decisions: (1) using matched temperature T=0.6 for all models including Base, validated in Appendix H to introduce only ∼11% SBERT reduction versus the 62% collapse observed at SFT—an order-of-magnitude smaller effect; (2) stripping CoT traces before any metric computation so diversity reflects final answers only; (3) using C13 13-gram matching to flag six benchmarks with 7–30% training overlap, though results on contaminated and clean tasks are consistent. Key Results: Think-SFT loses 62% of Base SBERT diversity on average across 15 tasks, versus 38% for Instruct-SFT. Despite initializing from the already-collapsed Think-SFT, Instruct-SFT recovers a median 40% of lost diversity due to its multi-source data. DPO removes 23% further diversity in Instruct versus only 4% in Think (which has little remaining to remove). RL-Zero retains ≥71% of Base diversity with a median of 94%. Both supervised lineages converge to similar final floors: Think retains 38% and Instruct 34% of Base diversity. The Vendi Score drops from ∼3.4 effective semantic modes (Base) to ∼1.8 at final checkpoints, with near-total collapse on math (GSM8K: 1.3 modes, MATH-Algebra: 1.4). EAD (lexical diversity) remains stable or increases even as SBERT drops, a dissociation replicated across all tasks—aligned models use varied vocabulary to express semantically identical content. Value-pluralism tasks show the steepest Think collapse (PRISM −78%, TruthfulQA −79%). The CoT suppression experiment finds zero diversity recovery on any task-stage combination: Think-not-thinking SBERT tracks Think within noise at every stage, while accuracy drops substantially (IFEval −8%, GSM8K −18%, HumanEval −32%, MATH-Geometry −32%). Quality decomposition reveals a task-dependent split: on IFEval, 83.4% of the diversity drop persists in Dc (genuine narrowing among correct outputs); on HumanEval, less than 10% is genuine (most collapse reflects elimination of wrong outputs). Think gains only +0.4 percentage points from majority voting on GSM8K (16 near-identical correct answers), while Base gains +24% and RL-Zero +22–26%. On TruthfulQA, majority voting hurts all models (majority vote@16 < accuracy@1) because collapse amplifies the dominant misconception. Novelty Classification: NEW EMPIRICAL FINDING. The contribution is a controlled dissociation of post-training algorithm effects from data composition effects on output diversity, plus the CoT-suppression falsification test. The experimental apparatus (multi-lineage comparison with identical recipes but different data, plus out-of-distribution inference interventions) is methodologically sharper than prior work, which confounded method and data. The quality-filtered decomposition framework is a useful analytical tool but draws on established ideas. The finding that both supervised lineages converge to the same diversity floor regardless of training trajectory is counter-intuitive and practically significant. Rigor & Reproducibility: Ablations are not applicable in the traditional sense—this is an observational study across existing checkpoints rather than a system with tunable components. The four-metric setup (EAD, SBERT, NLI, Vendi Score) provides complementary views, and the finding that EAD diverges from SBERT is itself a meaningful result. Training-evaluation contamination is measured and flagged; results are consistent across clean and contaminated benchmarks. Temperature sensitivity is assessed in a dedicated appendix and shown to be small relative to the observed effects. Code is publicly released at https://github.com/ckarouzos/where-diversity-collapses/. The LLM-as-judge evaluation (gpt-4.1-mini via OpenAI Batch API) for non-verifiable tasks introduces vendor dependence and known limitations for creative/value-laden tasks, which the authors acknowledge. No error bars or significance tests are reported on the main SBERT diversity comparisons; the study relies on consistent directional patterns across 15 tasks as evidence of reliability. This is a preprint under review. Deployability (4-property check): EMPIRICAL. - Practical: All experiments use vLLM and lighteval on standard HPC hardware; no proprietary infrastructure required beyond API access to gpt-4.1-mini for judge evaluation. - Efficient: The evaluation requires 13 models × 15 tasks × 500 prompts × 16 samples = ∼1.56M forward passes, plus embedding and NLI inference; substantial but within reach of academic compute with HPC access (acknowledged in the paper). - Effective: The evidence convincingly demonstrates that collapse is weight-embedded and data-driven for the specific Olmo 3 7B family; the practical implication—avoid narrow-teacher SFT—is well-supported within this scope. - Scalable: Tested exclusively at 7B; whether the same stage-attribution pattern holds at 70B+ is not examined and remains an open question. The convergence to a similar diversity floor across lineages might differ at scale. The work delivers a code artifact and data analysis rather than a deployable system or trained model. Strengths: - The parallel-lineage design is the strongest feature: Think and Instruct share identical algorithms and differ only in data, providing a cleaner causal structure for attribution than any prior single-lineage analysis. - The CoT-suppression experiment directly falsifies the hypothesis that generation format drives diversity collapse, resolving a confound present in all prior Think-vs-Instruct comparisons. - The quality-filtered decomposition (Da vs. Dc vs. Vc) is a concrete methodological contribution that resolves a genuine debate in the field about whether diversity collapse is "just quality control" or genuine homogenization—and shows it is task-dependent. - Breath of evaluation across 15 tasks spanning six qualitatively different categories produces a coherent picture of task-dependent diversity-quality tradeoffs. Author-Acknowledged Limitations: The authors note they measure final-answer diversity rather than reasoning-path diversity, which may underestimate collapse in the Think lineage where CoT traces are stripped. They distinguish distributional diversity (measured here) from representational diversity (which perspectives are preserved), noting that a model could appear collapsed distributionally while retaining key stances, or vice versa. LLM-judge evaluation of creative and value-laden tasks has known limitations, and those results are presented as supplementary context rather than primary evidence. Analyst-Identified Gaps: The study is observational within existing checkpoints; no direct data composition intervention (e.g., training on 4 vs. 2 vs. 10 teachers with fixed data volume) is run, so the causal mechanism connecting teacher breadth to collapse magnitude is inferred rather than established. The two teachers used for Think (QwQ-32B, DeepSeek-R1) come from the same training lineage and likely occupy a narrow region of output space; the paper does not characterize the diversity of teacher outputs themselves, which would strengthen the mechanistic interpretation. The study covers only 7B-scale models from a single family (Olmo 3); the convergence-to-same-floor finding could be specific to this architecture, pretraining mix, or scale. The RL-Zero diversity advantage (median 94% of Base retained) comes at a massive accuracy penalty (49.8–61% on GSM8K vs. 93% Think), and this tradeoff is described but not deeply analyzed—it is unclear whether entropy-regularized RL could preserve some RL-Zero diversity without the quality collapse. The paper does not examine whether simple interventions like increasing the number of SFT teachers from 2 to 8 would shift the diversity floor or just the trajectory. Bottom Line: A practitioner working on inference-time compute scaling, preference optimization, or model alignment will extract concrete and actionable findings here: diversity collapse is a data problem, not an algorithm problem, and you cannot fix it at inference time—read it.

Rethinking On-Policy Distillation of Large Language Models: Phenomenology, Mechanism, and Recipe

arxiv.org · 📄 Open PDF
**TL;DR** - **What it does:** This paper identifies two governing conditions for on-policy distillation (OPD) success — thinking-pattern consistency between student and teacher, and the teacher possessing genuinely new knowledge beyond what the student has seen — reveals the causal token-level mechanism (progressive alignment on shared high-probability overlap tokens), and proposes two practical recipes (off-policy cold start and teacher-aligned prompt selection) to recover failing OPD runs. - **Why it's important:** OPD is deployed in production post-training pipelines for Qwen3, MiMo, and GLM-5, yet practitioners use teacher benchmark scores as a proxy for distillation quality — a proxy this paper demonstrates is unreliable and can predict the wrong outcome. - **How it does it:** Controlled reverse-distillation experiments hold the student fixed while varying the teacher, and token-level metrics (overlap ratio, overlap-token advantage, entropy gap) are tracked throughout training to distinguish successful from failing runs causally, not just correlationally. - **The good:** The reverse distillation finding — that a weaker 1.5B teacher and a strictly stronger 7B teacher from the same model family drive the student to virtually identical (poor) outcomes despite the 7B outperforming on benchmarks — is a concrete, hard-to-dismiss counter-example to the dominant intuition that stronger teachers are always better. - **The missing:** All experiments use mathematical reasoning benchmarks (AIME 2024, AIME 2025, AMC 2023) with models in the 1.5B–7B range; generalization to code, open-ended tasks, or frontier-scale models is entirely untested. - **Read it or not:** Researchers and engineers designing LLM post-training pipelines should read this; it provides precisely defined diagnostic metrics and empirically validated recipes, though its math-only scope and absence of RL baselines leave the most practically decisive comparison open.
Full summary
